Al-Baqillani's Teachers in Hadith and Kalam
List of al-Baqillani's teachers in hadith and kalam (incomplete).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the names of al-Baqillani's teachers in hadith and the beginning of his kalam teacher's name, and recognize that the passage is incomplete.

Core Idea
Al-Baqillani learned hadith from three masters and kalam from an unnamed teacher.

This brief passage from al-Baqillani's biography lists the scholars from whom he studied hadith and kalam. Although incomplete, it shows the traditional chain of learning. We will examine each name and understand the significance of these teachers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation to get an overview of the content.
Pay attention to the structure: first the hadith teachers, then the kalam teacher.
2
Identify the hadith teachers
Look at the names: Abu Bakr al-Qati'i, Abu Muhammad ibn Masi, and Abu Ahmad al-Naysaburi. These are al-Baqillani's teachers in hadith.
Each name includes a kunya (Abu) and a nisba or family name.
3
Notice the incomplete kalam teacher
The text says 'and he took the science of kalam from Abu...' and cuts off. This is due to a page break in the manuscript.
The teacher's full name is lost, but we know it started with 'Abu'.
4
Reflect on the significance
Listing teachers is a tradition in Islamic scholarship to show authenticity and lineage of knowledge. This passage, though incomplete, shows al-Baqillani's broad education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمع الحديث من أبي بكر بن مالك القطيعي، وأبي محمد بن ماسي، وأبي أحمد الحسين بن علي النيسابوري، وأخذ علم النظر عن أبي
He heard hadith from Abu Bakr ibn Malik al-Qati'i, and Abu Muhammad ibn Masi, and Abu Ahmad al-Husayn ibn Ali al-Naysaburi, and he took the science of kalam (al-nazar) from Abu...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage enumerates the author's teachers in two fields: hadith and kalam. For hadith, three scholars are named: Abu Bakr al-Qati'i, Abu Muhammad ibn Masi, and Abu Ahmad al-Naysaburi. For kalam (the science of theoretical reasoning), it states he studied under a teacher whose name is cut off after 'Abu', likely continued on the next page. The list is incomplete due to the page boundary.

Key Terms
سمع الحديث
أبو بكر بن مالك القطيعي
أبو محمد بن ماسي
أبو أحمد الحسين بن علي النيسابوري
علم النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Baqillani only studied hadith under one teacher.
Correction: The passage names three distinct hadith teachers, indicating a broad education in hadith.

Checkpoint
How many teachers are fully named in this passage for the study of hadith?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's education included multiple hadith teachers and at least one kalam teacher, though the passage is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows al-Baqillani learning from multiple teachers. Are you satisfied with learning from just one source, or do you actively seek out teachers who will challenge and deepen your understanding? What is one step you can take this week to diversify your learning?
?
The name of the kalam teacher is cut off, lost to history. What incomplete knowledge in your own religious education have you left unaddressed? Will you commit today to finding a teacher or book to fill that gap?
?
Al-Baqillani's teachers are recorded with respect. When you learn, do you honor your teachers by acknowledging them and preserving their teachings, or do you take their knowledge for granted? Write down the name of one teacher who taught you something important and thank them this week.

Method of Learning

Foundations: Knowing Allah through Reason and Revelation
Baqillani lists the foundational topics of theology covered in the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational topics of Islamic theology as outlined by Imam al-Baqillani in his preface: the nature of knowledge, proofs for the world's creation, the existence and attributes of God, and His justice.

Core Idea
Theology begins with knowing what knowledge is and then proving the Creator.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his comprehensive work by telling the reader what to expect. In this first part of the preface, he outlines the core topics that form the backbone of Islamic theology: the meaning of knowledge, the rational proofs for the universe having a beginning, the existence of a unique Creator, His necessary attributes, and His perfect justice. This sets the stage for everything that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow of Baqillani's preface.
Notice the traditional opening 'amma ba'd' and the address to the prince.
2
Identify the four main sections
Locate the discussion of knowledge, proofs for the world's creation, divine attributes, and divine justice. Each is a distinct topic introduced by 'wa dhikr' or similar phrases.
The author uses 'wa' (and) to connect these topics.
3
Reflect on the order of topics
Ask why knowledge is mentioned first, then creation, then attributes, then justice. This order is deliberate: epistemology precedes ontology.
4
Connect to 'ilm al-kalam'
Recognize that these are the classical subjects of kalam: epistemology, cosmology, theology proper, and divine justice.
This preface is essentially a table of contents for a kalam manual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بعد : فقد عرفت إيثار سيدنا الأمير أطال الله من دوام العز بقاءه . . . لعمل کتاب جامع مختصر مشتمل على ما يحتاج إليه في الكشف عن معنى العلم وأقسامه وطرقه ومراتبه وضروب المعلومات وحقائق الموجودات. وذكر الأدلة على حدوث العالم وإثبات محدثه وأنه مخالف لخلقه، وعلى ما يجب كونه عليه من الوحدانية وكونه عالماً قادراً في أزله، وما جرى مجرى ذلك من صفات ذاته، وأنه عادل فيما انشأه من مخترعاته من غير حاجة منه إليها ولا محرك وداع وخاطر علل دعته إلى إيجادها تعالى عن ذلك، وجواز إرساله رسلا إلى خلقه وسفراء بينه وبين عباده، وأنه قد فعل ذلك، وقطع العذر في إيجاب تصديقهم بما أبانهم به من الآيات، ودلّ بـه على صدقهم من المعجزات وجمل من الكلام على سائر أهل الملل والمخالفين لملة الإسلام من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس وأهل التثنية وأصحاب الطبائع والمنجمين ونعقب ذلك بذكر أبواب الخلاف بين أهل الحق وأهل التجسيم والتشبيه وأهل القدر والاعتزال والرافضة والخوارج، وذكر جمل من مناقب الصحابة وفضائل الأئمة الأربعة، وإثبات إمامتهم ، ووجه التأويل فيما شجر بينهم ووجوب موالاتهم، ولن آلو جهداً فيما يميل إليـه سـيـدنـا الأمير، الله مهجته، وأعلى كعبه من الاختصار وتحرير المعاني والأدلة والألفاظ وسلوك طريق العون على تأمل ما أودعه هذا الكتاب وإزالة الشكوك فيه والارتياب وأنا بحول الله وقوته أسارع إلى امتثال ما رسمه، وأقف عنده، حرس
To proceed: You have known the preference of our master the prince – may Allah prolong his life in enduring glory – for composing a comprehensive, concise book containing what is needed to uncover the meaning of knowledge, its divisions, its paths, its ranks, the types of objects of knowledge, and the realities of existents. And the mention of proofs for the temporal origination of the world, the establishment of its Maker, that He is different from His creation, and what must be the case for Him of oneness, and that He is knowing and powerful in His pre-eternity, and what follows that from the attributes of His essence, and that He is just in what He has originated of His creations without any need from Him for them, nor any mover, caller, or thought of causes that called Him to bring them into existence – exalted is He above that. And the permissibility of sending messengers to His creation and ambassadors between Him and His servants, and that He has done that, and cutting off the excuse in necessitating the belief in them by the signs He gave them, and the miracles indicating their truth. And a summary of speech concerning all the people of [other] religions and those opposing the religion of Islam: the Jews, Christians, Magians, Dualists, adherents of natures, and astrologers. And I will follow that by mentioning the chapters of disagreement between the people of truth and the people of anthropomorphism and corporealism, the people of qadar and i'tizal, the Rafidah, and the Khawarij. And mention of a summary of the virtues of the Companions and the merits of the four Imams, and the establishment of their imamate, and the aspect of interpretation regarding what occurred among them, and the obligation of loyalty to them. And I will spare no effort in what our master the prince inclines to – may Allah protect his life and elevate his status – in abridgment, refinement of meanings, proofs, and expressions, and taking the path of assistance in contemplating what I have deposited in this book and removing doubts and misgivings about it. And by the power and strength of Allah, I hasten to comply with what he has ordained, and I stand by it. May he be protected.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the author's own preface, stating the purpose and scope of the book. It begins with the traditional phrase 'أما بعد' (to proceed) and addresses the prince who commissioned the work. The author outlines the book's comprehensive and concise nature, covering the meaning of knowledge and its classifications, the proofs for the temporal creation of the world, the existence of a Creator distinct from creation, God's attributes of oneness, knowledge, power, and justice, followed by the sending of messengers and the evidence of their truth. He then mentions refutations of various non-Muslim groups (Jews, Christians, Magians, Dualists, etc.) and heretical Muslim sects (anthropomorphists, Qadaris, Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Khawarij). Finally, he covers the virtues of the Companions and the four Imams, their imamate, and the proper interpretation of early disputes, emphasizing loyalty to them. The author pledges to achieve brevity while refining meanings, proofs, and expressions to remove doubts. The preface serves as a full table of contents for the kalam manual.

Key Terms
العلم
حدوث العالم
إثبات محدثه
الوحدانية
الصفات
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume theology only deals with God's existence and attributes, ignoring epistemology and divine justice.
Correction: Baqillani's preface shows that a complete theology must also define knowledge and affirm God's justice, as these are foundational to the kalam tradition.

Checkpoint
Which of the following topics is NOT mentioned in this passage?
A) The meaning of knowledge
B) Proofs for the temporal origination of the world
C) The four Imams' merits
D) God's oneness and attributes

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's preface shows that Islamic theology must first define knowledge, then establish the Creator, then affirm His attributes and justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that theology must begin with understanding what 'knowledge' is. When was the last time you examined the basis of your own knowledge about Allah? Is it built on clear definitions or borrowed assumptions? Commit today to tracing one belief back to its epistemological root.
?
The author affirms that God created without any need or cause. Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God 'had to' create or that He 'owes' you something? How does this passage expose that as a lack of tawhid? Resolve to repent from any sense of entitlement toward your Creator.
?
Baqillani lists the attributes of essence and then immediately affirms justice. Does your daily life reflect a balance between knowing Who Allah is (attributes) and trusting that He is just? Identify one area where you are struggling with Allah's justice and make a plan to correct your perspective using this lesson.

Refuting False Beliefs and Honouring the Companions
Baqillani outlines refutations and discussion of companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani structures his work to refute various heretical sects and non-Muslim groups, and to defend the stature of the Companions and the four Imams.

Core Idea
A complete theology must answer falsehoods and affirm the righteous early community.

After laying the foundational doctrines, Imam al-Baqillani turns to the practical application of theology: responding to those who deviate. In this continuation of the preface, he lists the groups he will refute, from Jews and Christians to heretical sects like the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. He also commits to discussing the virtues of the Companions and the four Imams, addressing the early conflicts with proper interpretation. This shows that theology is not just abstract but also polemical and communal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Notice how the author transitions from foundational topics to refutations and community affairs.
The passage begins with the topic of messengers and miracles, which bridges the two parts.
2
List the groups mentioned
Identify and categorize the groups: non-Muslims (Jews, Christians, Magians, etc.) and heretical Muslim sects (anthropomorphists, Qadaris, Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Khawarij).
3
Understand the inclusion of Companions and Imams
Reflect on why a theological work includes discussion of early Muslim figures. This shows that orthodoxy is connected to the historical community.
The author promises to explain the disputes among them with proper interpretation (ta'wil).
4
Notice the author's methodology
He promises brevity (ikhtisar) and refinement (tahrir) of meanings, proofs, and expressions to remove doubts.
This is a common theme in kalam works: clarity and conciseness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Which group is NOT listed among the heretical sects in this passage?
A) Rafidah
B) Khawarij
C) Sufis
D) Qadaris

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's theology covers messengers, refutations of all false beliefs, and the defense of the Companions and Imams, making it a complete guide for Sunnism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreements among Muslims today, do you rush to take sides or do you seek the proper interpretation (ta'wil) as Baqillani advocates for the Companions? What specific step will you take this week to understand a disputed issue with charity and fairness?
?
Are you more focused on refuting others or on affirming the truth about messengers and the righteous predecessors? How can you balance both in your own learning, especially when discussing Islam with non-Muslims or those with different views?
?
The author promises to remove doubts and misgivings. What is one doubt about your faith that you have been avoiding? Will you commit to studying this book—or another reliable source—to resolve it, even if it requires effort and humility?

Method of Learning

The Scope of Knowledge and Existence
Baqillani enumerates the topics of knowledge and existents as part of his book's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational topics of knowledge and existence as Baqillani outlines them in his introduction.

Core Idea
Baqillani's book systematically covers knowledge, existence, and divine realities.

This opening passage reveals the comprehensive scope of Baqillani’s work. He begins by listing the essential subjects: the nature of knowledge, its divisions, methods, ranks, and the categories of known things and existents. These form the conceptual groundwork for all subsequent theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read Baqillani's introductory statement
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author directly addresses the prince and states the purpose of the book.
2
Identify the key terms related to knowledge
Look for the terms 'al-ʿilm', 'aqsām', 'turuq', 'marātib', 'ḍurūb al-maʿlūmāt', and 'ḥaqāʾiq al-mawjūdāt'. Consider why these are important for theology.
These terms describe the conceptual framework of the work.
3
Reflect on the scope of the work
Think about how this list sets expectations for the reader. The book is not just about one topic but aims to cover the fundamentals of Islamic theology comprehensively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بعد : فقد عرفت إيثار سيدنا الأمير أطال الله من دوام العز بقاءه . . . لعمل کتاب جامع مختصر مشتمل على ما يحتاج إليه في الكشف عن معنى العلم وأقسامه وطرقه ومراتبه وضروب المعلومات وحقائق الموجودات. وذكر الأدلة على حدوث العالم وإثبات محدثه وأنه مخالف لخلقه، وعلى ما يجب كونه عليه من الوحدانية وكونه عالماً قادراً في أزله، وما جرى مجرى ذلك من صفات ذاته، وأنه عادل فيما انشأه من مخترعاته من غير حاجة منه إليها ولا محرك وداع وخاطر علل دعته إلى إيجادها تعالى عن ذلك، وجواز إرساله رسلا إلى خلقه وسفراء بينه وبين عباده، وأنه قد فعل ذلك، وقطع العذر في إيجاب تصديقهم بما أبانهم به من الآيات، ودلّ بـه على صدقهم من المعجزات وجمل من الكلام على سائر أهل الملل والمخالفين لملة الإسلام من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس وأهل التثنية وأصحاب الطبائع والمنجمين ونعقب ذلك بذكر أبواب الخلاف بين أهل الحق وأهل التجسيم والتشبيه وأهل القدر والاعتزال والرافضة والخوارج، وذكر جمل من مناقب الصحابة وفضائل الأئمة الأربعة، وإثبات إمامتهم ، ووجه التأويل فيما شجر بينهم ووجوب موالاتهم، ولن آلو جهداً فيما يميل إليـه سـيـدنـا الأمير، الله مهجته، وأعلى كعبه من الاختصار وتحرير المعاني والأدلة والألفاظ وسلوك طريق العون على تأمل ما أودعه هذا الكتاب وإزالة الشكوك فيه والارتياب وأنا بحول الله وقوته أسارع إلى امتثال ما رسمه، وأقف عنده، وإلى الله جل ذكره أرغب في حسن التوفيق والامداد بالتأييد والتسديد .
To proceed: Indeed, you have preferred, our lord the amir—may Allah prolong his glory and permanence—the composition of a comprehensive, concise book containing what is needed for revealing the meaning of knowledge, its divisions, its methods, its ranks, the types of known things, and the realities of existents. And the mention of proofs for the temporality of the world, establishing its Originator, that He is unlike His creation, and what must be true of Him in terms of unity, that He is knowing, powerful in His eternity, and what follows from that of the attributes of His essence, and that He is just in what He creates from His inventions without any need on His part for them, nor any mover, caller, or cause that impelled Him to bring them into existence—exalted is He above that—and the permissibility of His sending messengers to His creation and emissaries between Him and His servants, and that He has done so, cutting off every excuse by requiring belief in them through the signs He manifested, and by which He demonstrated their truthfulness—the miracles. And a summary of discourse against all the people of other religions and those who oppose the religion of Islam: the Jews, Christians, Magians, dualists, naturalists, astrologers. And we will follow that with mentioning the chapters of disagreement between the people of truth and the people of anthropomorphism and comparison, the Qadariyyah, Muʿtazilah, Rafidah, and Khawarij, and mentioning a summary of the merits of the Companions and the virtues of the four Imams, establishing their imamate, and the interpretation of what happened among them, and the obligation of loyalty to them. And I will spare no effort in what our master the amir—may Allah protect his life and elevate his station—desires in terms of brevity, precision of meanings, proofs, and expressions, and pursuing a path that aids reflection on what I have deposited in this book, removing doubts and uncertainties. And I, by the power and strength of Allah, hasten to comply with his command and stop at it. And to Allah—glorified be His mention—I seek good success and support through divine aid and guidance.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the author's own introduction to his book, where he states the purpose and outlines the contents. The author addresses the prince who commissioned the book. He says he has composed a concise yet comprehensive work covering the meaning of knowledge ('ilm), its divisions (aqsam), methods (turuq), ranks (maratib), types of known things (duruh al-ma'lumat), and the realities of existents (haqa'iq al-mawjudat). Then he lists the proofs he will include: proof of the world's temporality (huduth al-'alam), establishing the Creator (muhdith), His transcendence (mukhalafah li-khalqihi), unity (wahdaniyyah), attributes of essence (sifat dhatihi) such as knowledge and power, justice ('adl) in creation without need or cause, the permissibility of sending messengers (rusul) with miracles (mu'jizat). The book will then refute other religions: Jews, Christians, Magians, dualists, naturalists, astrologers. It will cover disagreements among Islamic sects: anthropomorphists (mujassimah, mushabbihah), Qadariyyah, Mu'tazilah, Rafidah, Khawarij. It will discuss the merits of the Companions (sahabah), the four Imams (al-a'immah al-arba'ah), their imamate (imamah), and the interpretation (ta'wil) of disputes, stressing loyalty. The author promises brevity, precision, and clarity, seeking Allah's help.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani begins by establishing the conceptual scope of his work: the nature of knowledge and the realities of existent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani begins his book with a systematic plan covering knowledge and every existent. Have you ever taken such a deliberate approach to examining your own beliefs, especially the ones you hold most firmly? What might it reveal if you did? Make a commitment this week to write down the 'scope' of your own worldview and the foundations of each part.
?
The prince commissioned this book for clarity and comprehensiveness. What aspect of your faith is currently unclear to you, and what steps have you actually taken to resolve it—not just passive reading, but active, systematic study? If you haven't, what is the real reason you avoid it? Decide now to work through one unclear topic using a structured resource.

Proofs of the Creator and Prophethood
Baqillani summarizes the proofs for God's existence, attributes, justice, and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key proofs and teachings regarding the Creator, His attributes, and the mission of prophets as outlined by Baqillani.

Core Idea
Baqillani covers proofs of creation, divine attributes, justice, and prophethood.

After establishing the conceptual foundation, Baqillani turns to the central theological doctrines. He lists the proofs for the world's temporality, the Creator's transcendence and unity, His eternal attributes, and His justice. Then he discusses the necessity of prophethood and the role of miracles in confirming messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the portion on the Creator
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the sequence: temporality of the world, establishing the Creator, His attributes, His justice.
2
Note the sequence of arguments
The author moves from proving the world’s temporality to establishing the Creator, then to His attributes, justice, and finally prophethood. This is a logical order.
3
Understand the concept of justice and prophethood
Think about why God's justice is mentioned alongside His attributes. Also consider how miracles serve as proofs for prophets.
This prepares for detailed chapters on 'adl and nubuwwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بعد : فقد عرفت إيثار سيدنا الأمير أطال الله من دوام العز بقاءه . . . لعمل کتاب جامع مختصر مشتمل على ما يحتاج إليه في الكشف عن معنى العلم وأقسامه وطرقه ومراتبه وضروب المعلومات وحقائق الموجودات. وذكر الأدلة على حدوث العالم وإثبات محدثه وأنه مخالف لخلقه، وعلى ما يجب كونه عليه من الوحدانية وكونه عالماً قادراً في أزله، وما جرى مجرى ذلك من صفات ذاته، وأنه عادل فيما انشأه من مخترعاته من غير حاجة منه إليها ولا محرك وداع وخاطر علل دعته إلى إيجادها تعالى عن ذلك، وجواز إرساله رسلا إلى خلقه وسفراء بينه وبين عباده، وأنه قد فعل ذلك، وقطع العذر في إيجاب تصديقهم بما أبانهم به من الآيات، ودلّ بـه على صدقهم من المعجزات وجمل من الكلام على سائر أهل الملل والمخالفين لملة الإسلام من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس وأهل التثنية وأصحاب الطبائع والمنجمين ونعقب ذلك بذكر أبواب الخلاف بين أهل الحق وأهل التجسيم والتشبيه وأهل القدر والاعتزال والرافضة والخوارج، وذكر جمل من مناقب الصحابة وفضائل الأئمة الأربعة، وإثبات إمامتهم ، ووجه التأويل فيما شجر بينهم ووجوب موالاتهم، ولن آلو جهداً فيما يميل إليـه سـيـدنـا الأمير، الله مهجته، وأعلى كعبه من الاختصار وتحرير المعاني والأدلة والألفاظ وسلوك طريق العون على تأمل ما أودعه هذا الكتاب وإزالة الشكوك فيه والارتياب وأنا بحول الله وقوته أسارع إلى امتثال ما رسمه، وأقف عنده، وإلى الله جل ذكره أرغب في حسن التوفيق والامداد بالتأييد والتسديد .
To proceed: Indeed, you have preferred, our lord the amir—may Allah prolong his glory and permanence—the composition of a comprehensive, concise book containing what is needed for revealing the meaning of knowledge, its divisions, its methods, its ranks, the types of known things, and the realities of existents. And the mention of proofs for the temporality of the world, establishing its Originator, that He is unlike His creation, and what must be true of Him in terms of unity, that He is knowing, powerful in His eternity, and what follows from that of the attributes of His essence, and that He is just in what He creates from His inventions without any need on His part for them, nor any mover, caller, or cause that impelled Him to bring them into existence—exalted is He above that—and the permissibility of His sending messengers to His creation and emissaries between Him and His servants, and that He has done so, cutting off every excuse by requiring belief in them through the signs He manifested, and by which He demonstrated their truthfulness—the miracles. And a summary of discourse against all the people of other religions and those who oppose the religion of Islam: the Jews, Christians, Magians, dualists, naturalists, astrologers. And we will follow that with mentioning the chapters of disagreement between the people of truth and the people of anthropomorphism and comparison, the Qadariyyah, Muʿtazilah, Rafidah, and Khawarij, and mentioning a summary of the merits of the Companions and the virtues of the four Imams, establishing their imamate, and the interpretation of what happened among them, and the obligation of loyalty to them. And I will spare no effort in what our master the amir—may Allah protect his life and elevate his station—desires in terms of brevity, precision of meanings, proofs, and expressions, and pursuing a path that aids reflection on what I have deposited in this book, removing doubts and uncertainties. And I, by the power and strength of Allah, hasten to comply with his command and stop at it. And to Allah—glorified be His mention—I seek good success and support through divine aid and guidance.
Source pages p. 23

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani says God created without any need and without any cause impelling Him. If you truly believed this, how would it change the way you complain about your circumstances? What would you say to God right now about a difficulty you face, knowing He did not need to create you but chose to out of wisdom and justice?
?
God cut off every excuse by sending messengers with miracles. Is there an excuse you have been hiding behind to avoid fully submitting to the truth you know? Write down that excuse, and then decide today what step you will take to respond to God's message.
?
You affirm that God is 'knowing, powerful in His eternity' and that His justice is not bound by external causes. How does this truth confront your own sense of entitlement? In what area of your life do you feel God owes you an explanation? Will you surrender that demand today?

Refutations, Sects, and the Companions
Baqillani summarizes his refutations of other faiths and sectarian discussions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani addresses refutations of other religions, sectarian disputes, and the status of the Companions and Imams.

Core Idea
Baqillani defends Islam against other religions and addresses internal sectarian divisions.

The final part of Baqillani's introduction outlines his engagement with critics and sectarian controversies. He will refute other religions—Jews, Christians, Magians, dualists, naturalists, astrologers—and then discuss disputes among Islamic sects. He also covers the merits of the Companions, the four Imams, their imamate, and the proper interpretation of historical conflicts, emphasizing loyalty to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the part on refuting other religions
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the specific groups listed: Yahud, Nasara, Majus, dualists, naturalists, astrologers.
2
Identify the sects mentioned
Look for the names of Islamic sects: Mujassimah, Mushabbihah, Qadariyyah, Mu'tazilah, Rafidah, Khawarij. Understand that these represent opposing views.
3
Understand the treatment of Companions and Imams
Note the focus on merits (manaqib), virtues (fada'il), imamate (imamah), interpretation (ta'wil), and loyalty (muwalat). This shows Baqillani's defense of Sunni orthodoxy.

Baqillani's Definition of Theoretical Knowledge and Its Kasbi Character
Baqillani defines theoretical knowledge and explains its kasbi character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's definition of theoretical knowledge and the meaning of its acquired (kasbi) nature.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge is built on sense and necessity, and is acquired through the servant's originated capacity.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani provides a precise definition of theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) and clarifies what it means for such knowledge to be 'acquired' (kasbi). This distinction is foundational in Sunni theology, as it separates knowledge that requires effort from knowledge that is necessarily present.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the technical term 'lam naql' which may be a variant reading.
2
Understand the definition
Theoretical knowledge is built upon sense and necessity, or upon what is built on them. This grounds it in immediate knowledge.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'kasbi'
It means the knowledge occurs through the servant's effort, and the servant has an originated capacity (qudra muhdatha) over it.
4
Note the nuance
Even knowledge itself participates in the capacity, so it is acquisition relative to the one who acquires it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُجْعَل مكان هذه الألفاظ لام نقل : العلم النظري هو ما بني على علم الحس والضرورة أو على ما بُنِي العلم بصحته عليهما ومعنى قولنا في هذا العلم إنه كَسْبِي أنه مما وُجد بالعالم وله عليه قدرة مُحدثة وكذلك شيء شَرِكَهُ في ذلك أعني العلم في وجود القدرة المُحدثة عليه فهو كسب لمن وجد به.
And sometimes these expressions are replaced by 'lam naql': theoretical knowledge is that which is built upon knowledge of sense and necessity, or upon what knowledge of its correctness is built upon them. And the meaning of our saying that this knowledge is 'acquired' is that it is something that occurs by means of the servant and he has over it an originated capacity, and likewise something that has a share in that—I mean knowledge—in the existence of the originated capacity over it, so it is acquisition for the one by whom it occurs.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage defines theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) and explains its acquired (kasbi) nature. The author first notes a terminological shift: instead of earlier expressions, the term 'lam naql' is used. The definition states that theoretical knowledge is based on sense-perception (hiss) and necessary knowledge (darura), or on that which establishes the correctness of knowledge based on them. This indicates that theoretical knowledge is derived from more immediate forms of knowledge. Next, the meaning of 'acquired' (kasbi) is explained: it is knowledge that comes about through the servant's effort, and the servant has a created power (qudra muhdatha) to attain it. Moreover, even knowledge itself participates in the existence of that power, so it is called 'acquisition' (kasb) relative to the one who acquires it. This clarifies that acquired knowledge is not simply given but involves the servant's capacity, distinguishing it from necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم النظري
كسبي
قدرة محدثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'acquired' means the servant creates the knowledge independently.
Correction: Baqillani emphasizes the servant's capacity as a means while the knowledge itself is from God.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the basis of theoretical knowledge?
A) Pure reason without any sense
B) Knowledge of sense and necessity, or what is built upon them
C) Intuition and inspiration
D) Revelation and scripture only

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge is built on sense and necessity, and its acquisition involves the servant's originated capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something new about your faith, do you take it as 'theoretical knowledge' that you must actively acquire through your own effort, or do you passively rely on it being given? What would change if you saw each new truth as something you must earn through your originated capacity?
?
Baqillani says theoretical knowledge occurs 'by means of the servant' and you have a power over it—yet that power is created, not your own. Do you truly feel that your intellectual efforts are a gift from Allah, or do you privately credit your own intelligence and diligence? How can you remind yourself daily that your capacity to know is originated?
?
If theoretical knowledge is built on sense and necessity, are you careful to ground your beliefs in what is sense-based and self-evident rather than mere speculation or tradition? What is one belief you hold about Allah that you cannot trace back to sense or necessity? How can you rebuild it on a sound foundation?

The Six Sources of Necessary Knowledge: the Five Senses
Baqillani enumerates the six ways of necessary knowledge and details the five senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's classification of the six sources of necessary knowledge and how the five senses produce indubitable knowledge.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge comes from six sources, beginning with the five senses.

Imam Baqillani begins a new chapter on the sources of knowledge. He poses a question: from how many ways does necessary knowledge occur? His answer provides a systematic classification that distinguishes sensory perception from other types of certainty. This lesson examines the first five sources — the senses — and their specific objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the question-and-answer format. The author is defining 'sense' carefully to avoid confusion.
2
Identify the six ways
The passage says there are six paths. Find the five senses listed, and note the brief mention of the sixth type (which is cut off). Understand that the sixth type is generated in the soul without sensory input.
The passage ends mid-sentence for the sixth type.
3
Understand the definition of 'sense'
Baqillani clarifies that by 'sense' he means the perception, not the physical organ. This is important to avoid a common misconception.
4
Examine the objects of each sense
Each sense is specialized for certain genera. Note what each perceives according to ongoing habit. Consider why he says 'in our time' and 'according to ongoing habit' — this allows for exceptions in miracles.
The objects are given for sight, hearing, smell, taste, and touch.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في مدارك العلوم فإن قال قائل : فمِنْ كَمْ وجه يقع العلم بالمعلوم إذا كان ضرورة؟ من ستة طرق فمنها الحواس الخمس : وهي حاسة البصر وحاسة السمع وحاسة الذوق وحاسة الشم وحاسة اللمس وقصدنا بذكر الحاسة ها هنا الإدراك الموجود بالحواس لا الأجسام المؤتلفة على الصورة التي ما حصل عليها من الأجسام سميناه عيناً وأنفاً وأُذُن وفماً ويداً . فكل علم حصل عند إدراك حاسة من هذه الحواس فهو علم ضرورةٍ يلزم النفس لزوماً لا يمكن معه الشكُ في المدرَك ولا الارتياب به وكل حاسة من هذه الحواس تختص في وقتنا هذا على عادة جارية بإدراك جنس أو أجناس فحاسة الرؤية تُدْرَك بها اليوم الألوان والأكوان والأجسام وحاسة السمع يُدْرَك بها الكلامُ والأصوات وحاسة الشم تُدْرَك بها الأرابيحُ وحاسة الذوق تُدرك بها الطعوم وحاسة اللمس وكلُّ عضو فيه حياة تُدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة واللين والخشونة والرخاوة والصلابة، على قول من زعم أن الخشونة والرخاوة واللين والصلابة معانٍ زائدة توجد بالجواهر كالحرارة والبرودة . والضرب السادس منها ضرورة تخترع في النفس ابتداءً من غير أن تكون
Chapter on speaking about the sources of knowledge. So if someone says: 'From how many ways does knowledge occur with respect to the known if it is necessary?' [He replies:] From six paths. Among them are the five senses: the sense of sight, the sense of hearing, the sense of taste, the sense of smell, and the sense of touch. And by mentioning 'sense' here we intend the perception existing by the senses, not the composed bodies in the form that we have named eye, nose, ear, mouth, and hand. So every knowledge that occurs at the perception of a sense from these senses is necessary knowledge that obligates the soul with an obligation that does not allow doubt about the perceived nor uncertainty about it. And each sense from these senses, in our time according to the ongoing habit, is specialized in perceiving a genus or genera: the sense of sight perceives colors, spatial configurations, and bodies; the sense of hearing perceives speech and sounds; the sense of smell perceives odors; the sense of taste perceives tastes; and the sense of touch—every organ in which there is life—perceives heat, cold, softness, roughness, flaccidity, and hardness, according to the view of those who claim that roughness, flaccidity, softness, and hardness are additional meanings existing in substances like heat and cold. And the sixth type among them is a necessity generated in the soul initially without being
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage classifies the sources of necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri). It begins a new chapter on the sources of knowledge. A question is posed: from how many ways does necessary knowledge occur? The answer: six ways. The first five are the senses (hawass khams): sight, hearing, taste, smell, and touch. The author clarifies that by 'sense' he means the faculty of perception (idrak), not the physical organs (e.g., eye, ear). Knowledge obtained through these senses is necessary and indubitable. Each sense has a specific range: sight perceives colors, spatial positions (akwan), and bodies; hearing perceives speech and sounds; smell perceives odors; taste perceives tastes; touch perceives qualities such as heat, cold, softness, roughness, etc., with a note that some consider those qualities as additional entities (ma'ani). The sixth type is 'a necessity generated in the soul initially' without a sensory source—likely referring to primary intellectual principles. The passage provides a detailed empirical epistemology.

Key Terms
مدارك العلوم
الحواس الخمس
علم ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The senses perceive the physical organs themselves rather than the perceptions.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly distinguishes between the organ (e.g., eye, ear) and the perception (idrak) achieved through the sense. The sense is the perception, not the organ.

Checkpoint
How many ways of necessary knowledge does Baqillani mention?
A) Five ways (the senses only)
B) Six ways (five senses plus a sixth type)
C) Seven ways (including intuition)
D) Four ways (excluding touch)

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge through the senses is indubitable and covers specific realms of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin by looking at something forbidden, do you honestly treat that visual perception as 'necessary knowledge' that leaves no room for doubt about what you saw? If so, how does that affect your accountability before Allah?
?
Baqillani distinguishes between the physical organ (eye, ear) and the perception itself. In your next salah, will you consciously shift your attention from the sound of your recitation to the meaning of what you hear? What stops you from doing this now?
?
If sensory knowledge is indubitable, why do you sometimes make excuses like 'I didn't realize' when clear sensory evidence pointed to wrong? How can you train yourself to accept responsibility for what your senses perceive?

The Five Senses and Their Objects
Baqillani enumerates the five senses and their objects, defines them as faculties, and asserts divine creation of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's definition of each of the five senses, their physiological faculties, and the Ash'ari principle that God creates perception directly in the soul.

Core Idea
The senses are faculties through which God creates perception in the soul.

Baqillani begins with a concise list of the five senses and the types of things each perceives. He then provides detailed physiological definitions, repeatedly stating that God creates perception in the soul when the sense is used. This reflects the Ash'ari commitment to divine causality: the sense organ is only the occasion, not the cause of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the first paragraph lists each sense and its objects, while the footnotes offer precise definitions.
The footnotes are part of the original text; they contain the detailed physiology.
2
Analyze each sense definition
For each of the five senses, identify the faculty (quwwa), its bodily location, and the objects it perceives. Note the recurring phrase 'God creates perception in the soul'.
This phrase appears for sight, hearing, and by implication for the others.
3
Reflect on the theological point
Consider why Baqillani emphasizes that God, not the organ, creates perception. How does this protect the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism?
This counters any naturalist view that the senses produce knowledge on their own.

فحاسة الرؤية (1) تُدْرَك بها اليوم الألوان والأكوان والأجسام وحاسة السمع (۲) يُدْرَك بها الكلامُ والأصوات وحاسة الشم (۳) تُدْرَك بها الأرابيحُ (٤) وحاسة الذوق (٢) تُدرك بها الطعوم وحاسة اللمس (٦) وكلُّ عضو فيه (۷) حياة تُدرك بها (۸) الحرارة والبرودة واللين والخشونة والرخاوة والصلابة، على قول من زعم أن (1) الخشونة والرخاوة (١٠) واللين والصلابة معانٍ (۱۱) زائدة (۱۳) توجد بالجواهر كالحرارة والبرودة .

(1) الرؤية : هي قوة مودعة في العصبتين المجوفتين اللتين تتلاقيان ثم تفترقان فتتأديان إلى العينين يدرك بها الأضواء والألوان والأشكال والمقادير والحركات والحسن والقبيح وغير ذلك، مما يخلق الله ادراكها في النفس عند استعمال العبد تلك القوة .
(۲) السمع : هي قوة مودعة في العصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ تدرك بها الأصوات بطريق وصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية الصوت إلى الصماخ بمعنى أن الله تعالى يخلق الإدراك في النفس عند ذلك .
(۳) الشم : وهي قوة مودعة في الزائدتين الناتئين من مقدم الدماغ الشبيهتين بحلمتي الثدي يدرك بها الروائح بطريق وصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية ذي الرائحة إلى الخيشوم .
(٤) في ص : الأرياح.
(٥) الذوق وهي قوة منبثة في العصب المفروش على جرم اللسان يدرك بها المطعوم بمخالطة الرطوبة اللعابية التي هي في الفم بالمطعوم ووصولها إلى العصب.
(٦) اللمس : هي قوة منبثة في جميع البدن يدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ونحو ذلك عند التماس والاتصال به.
(۷) في ص: فيها.
(۸) في ص : معنى ؛ في ف معاني
(9) في ص ف اللين قبل الخشونة .
(10) في ف نقص (الرخاوة).
(11) في ف معاني .
(12) في ص ف نقص (زائدة).
For the sense of sight (1), by it are perceived today colors, spatial forms, and bodies. And the sense of hearing (2), by it are perceived speech and sounds. And the sense of smell (3), by it are perceived odors (4). And the sense of taste (5), by it are perceived flavors. And the sense of touch (6), and every limb in which (7) there is life, by it are perceived (8) heat, cold, softness, roughness, flaccidity, and hardness, according to the claim of those who assert that (1) roughness, flaccidity (10), softness, and hardness are additional (11) attributes (12) that exist in substances like heat and cold.

(1) Sight: It is a faculty lodged in the two hollow nerves that meet then separate, leading to the two eyes. By it are perceived lights, colors, shapes, magnitudes, motions, beauty, ugliness, and other things, which God creates perception of in the soul when the servant uses that faculty.
(2) Hearing: It is a faculty lodged in the nerve spread in the concavity of the ear canal. By it are perceived sounds by way of the arrival of air conditioned by the quality of sound to the ear canal, meaning that God the Exalted creates perception in the soul at that time.
(3) Smell: It is a faculty lodged in the two protruding appendages from the front of the brain, resembling the nipples of the breast. By it are perceived odors by way of the arrival of air conditioned by the quality of the odorous thing to the nose.
(4) In manuscript S: 'al-aryāḥ' (winds).
(5) Taste: It is a faculty diffused in the nerve spread on the substance of the tongue. By it is perceived the tasted thing through the mixing of the salivary moisture that is in the mouth with the tasted thing and its arrival at the nerve.
(6) Touch: It is a faculty diffused throughout the entire body. By it are perceived heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and the like, upon contact and connection with it.
(7) In manuscript S: 'fīhā'.
(8) In manuscript S: 'ma'nā'; in manuscript F: 'ma'ānī'.
(9) In manuscripts S and F: 'al-layn' (softness) before 'al-khushūna' (roughness).
(10) In manuscript F: missing (al-rakhāwa).
(11) In manuscript F: 'ma'ānī'.
(12) In manuscripts S and F: missing (zā'ida).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a definition and enumeration of the five senses and their objects. It is part of Baqillani's discussion of the types of necessary knowledge, specifically knowledge obtained through the senses. The author first lists each sense and what it perceives: sight perceives colors, spatial forms, and bodies; hearing perceives speech and sounds; smell perceives odors; taste perceives flavors; and touch, present in every living limb, perceives heat, cold, softness, roughness, flaccidity, and hardness. He then notes a disagreement: some claim that softness, roughness, flaccidity, and hardness are additional attributes existing in substances like heat and cold. The footnotes provide detailed definitions of each sense, emphasizing that these are faculties (quwwa) lodged in specific bodily organs, and that perception is actually created by God in the soul at the moment the sense organ is used; the organ itself does not cause perception. For example, sight is a faculty in the optic nerves, hearing in the auditory nerve, etc. The variant footnotes document textual differences between manuscripts (S and F) regarding wording and ordering. The overall thrust is to ground the epistemological role of the senses while maintaining that God is the direct cause of perception—a typical Ash'ari position to avoid attributing causal power to natural processes.

Misconception: The sense organs themselves cause perception.
Correction: Baqillani explains that perception is created directly by God in the soul at the time the sense is used; the organ is merely the locus of the faculty.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If every sensation—from the taste of water to the touch of a loved one—is directly created by God, how does that challenge the way you live your life? What one habit could you adopt today to constantly remember this dependence?

Knowledge of Self Through Inner Senses
Baqillani presents knowledge of self-internal states as a form of sensory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of one's own internal states (health, sickness, emotions) is a type of knowledge that arises through the senses, contrary to those who claim all knowledge is empirical only.

Core Idea
You know your own feelings directly—this is a kind of knowledge from inner sense.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his enumeration of knowledge types by highlighting what every person knows immediately about themselves. This knowledge of one's own physical and emotional states—like health, pain, joy—comes through a kind of internal perception. In this passage, he establishes that the senses are not limited to external observation; they also include inner awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify types
Read the Arabic and translation, then list all the inner states Baqillani mentions (health, sickness, pleasure, pain, etc.).
Notice how many different kinds of internal knowledge are covered.
2
Understand the sensory basis
Note the phrase 'مما يدركه الحي إذا وجد به' – what does it tell us about when this knowledge occurs?
It implies the knowledge is present as long as the experience exists.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why Baqillani starts with this category. How does it refute those who deny knowledge beyond external senses?
This establishes that even self-awareness is a form of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن موجودة ببعض هذه الحواس كعلم الإنسان بنفسه وما يجده فيها من الصحة والسقم واللذة والألم والغم والفرح والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة والإدراك والعمى وغير ذلك مما يحدث في النفس مما يدركه الحي إذا وجد به، ومنه أيضاً العلم الواقع بقصد المتكلم إلى ما يقصد يقصده بخطابه دون غيره وأنه قاصد إلى اكتساب ما يوجد به من ضروب مقدوراته من الكلام وغيره، ومنه أيضاً العلم بأن الأجسام متى موجودة فلا بد من أن تكون مجتمعة متماسّة الأبعاض أو مفترقة متباينة وأن الخبر عن وجود الشيء وأنه على بعض الأوصاف لا بد أن يكون صدقاً أو كذباً وأن الخبرين المتضادّ مُخبَرُهُما لا يجوز أن يكونا جميعاً صدقاً أو كذباً وما كان ذلك جرى مجرى من الأمور المنقسمة في العقل إلى أمرين لا واسطة بينهما. ومنه أيضاً العلم بخجل الخَجِل وَوَجَل الوَجل والعلم بالشجاعة والجبن والبر والعُقوق والتحية والاستهزاء والواقع عند مشاهدة الامارات، ومنه أيضاً العلم المُخْتَرَع في النفس بما تواتر الخبر عن كونه واستفاض عن وجوده نحو العلم الواقع عند إخبار المخبرين عن الصين وخراسان وفارس وكرمان وعن ظهور موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلى الله عليه وعلى جميع النبيين والخبر عن الوقائع والفتن والممالك والدول وغير ذلك من الأمور الحاصل الخبر عنها من قوم قطع العُذر نَقلُهم ووجب العلم عند خبرهم.
And among them is that which exists by some of these senses, like a person's knowledge of himself and what he finds in himself of health and sickness, pleasure and pain, grief and joy, ability and inability, will and dislike, perception and blindness, and other things that occur in the soul which the living being perceives when it exists with it. And from it also is the knowledge that occurs by the intention of the speaker to what he intends by his speech and nothing else, and that he intends to acquire what is found in it of the kinds of his capabilities, such as speech and other things. And from it also is the knowledge that bodies, whenever they exist, must be either joined, with parts touching, or separate and distinct; and that the report of the existence of a thing and that it is in some state must be either true or false; and that two conflicting reports cannot both be true or both false; and that which is like that among matters that are divided in the intellect into two things with no intermediary between them. And from it also is the knowledge of the shame of the ashamed and the fear of the fearful, and the knowledge of courage and cowardice, piety and filial impiety, greeting and mockery, which occurs when observing indications. And from it also is the knowledge created in the soul by what the report has been recurrently transmitted about its existence and widespread about its occurrence, like the knowledge that occurs upon the reports of informants about China, Khurasan, Persia, and Kirman, and about the appearance of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon him and upon all the prophets), and the report about events, trials, kingdoms, states, and other matters about which the report comes from a people whose transmission removes excuse and knowledge becomes necessary upon their report.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of various types of knowledge that occur in the soul, demonstrating that knowledge is not confined to sense perception. The author lists several categories: (1) knowledge gained through the senses, such as a person's knowledge of his own physical and emotional states; (2) knowledge of another's intention through speech; (3) knowledge of necessary rational divisions, such as that bodies must be either joined or separated, or that a report must be either true or false, with no middle ground; (4) knowledge from signs, like knowing a person's shame from their blush; and (5) knowledge from recurrent (mutawatir) reports, which compel certainty, such as knowledge of distant lands and past prophets. The argument proceeds by showing that many types of knowledge do not depend on direct sensory experience but are instead created in the soul through other means, thereby refuting those who claim all knowledge comes from the senses alone.

الحواس

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a moment of deep grief or joy, do you regard that as a genuine piece of knowledge about your soul? If you did, how might it change the way you respond to your own emotions?
?
Baqillani says you know your own abilities and disabilities directly. When you feel unable to do something, do you treat that as a fact about yourself, or do you dismiss it as a fleeting feeling? What would it mean to accept it as real knowledge?
?
You know your own will and dislike immediately. How does this knowledge affect your decisions? Are you fully honest with yourself about what you truly will and dislike, or do you sometimes deceive yourself?

Knowledge of Intent through Speech
Baqillani describes knowledge of a speaker's intention through his speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that we can know a speaker's intention through their speech, which is a type of knowledge independent of direct observation.

Core Idea
Speech reveals intention—a knowledge not from the senses but from meaning.

Baqillani now turns to a type of knowledge that arises when we hear speech: we understand that the speaker intends to convey something specific. This knowledge is not based on the phonetic sounds alone but on the meaning intended. In this passage, he highlights how we can know that a speaker is purposefully aiming to produce certain actions or effects through his words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the repetition of 'يقصد يقصده' and the overall meaning.
The text emphasizes the intentional nature of speech.
2
Distinguish from other knowledge types
How is this knowledge different from knowing one's own states or from rational divisions?
It relies on understanding language and context.
3
Consider examples
Think of a situation where someone speaks and you know their intention, even if they don't spell it out.
This shows the pervasiveness of this knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of various types of knowledge that occur in the soul, demonstrating that knowledge is not confined to sense perception. The author lists several categories: (1) knowledge gained through the senses, such as a person's knowledge of his own physical and emotional states; (2) knowledge of another's intention through speech; (3) knowledge of necessary rational divisions, such as that bodies must be either joined or separated, or that a report must be either true or false, with no middle ground; (4) knowledge from signs, like knowing a person's shame from their blush; and (5) knowledge from recurrent (mutawatir) reports, which compel certainty, such as knowledge of distant lands and past prophets. The argument proceeds by showing that many types of knowledge do not depend on direct sensory experience but are instead created in the soul through other means, thereby refuting those who claim all knowledge comes from the senses alone.

قصد
المتكلم
خطاب

We can know a speaker's intention through his speech—a knowledge distinct from sensory perception.

?

?
If Allah knows your intention even before you speak, how does that change your accountability for every word you utter? Will you commit to examining your speech today for hidden intentions?

Knowledge of Necessary Rational Divisions
Baqillani enumerates necessary rational divisions that are known by the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that certain logical divisions—like bodies being joined or separate, reports being true or false—are known necessarily through reason.

Core Idea
Some truths are known by reason alone, like that a report must be true or false.

Baqillani moves to a category of knowledge that is purely rational: necessary divisions that admit no middle ground. He gives examples from physics (bodies are either joined or separate) and logic (reports are either true or false). This type of knowledge is immediate and does not depend on sense experience.

Guided Path
1
Read and list the examples
Read the passage and identify the four specific examples Baqillani gives (bodies, reports, conflicting reports, and the general principle).
Each example illustrates a binary division with no middle.
2
Understand 'no middle'
The phrase 'لا واسطة بينهما' means there is no third option. Why is that important for rational knowledge?
It shows that some truths are self-evident and exhaustive.
3
Connect to theology
How might these divisions be used in arguments about God and creation? For example, is the world eternal or created?
Such binary divisions are foundational in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن موجودة ببعض هذه الحواس كعلم الإنسان بنفسه وما يجده فيها من الصحة والسقم واللذة والألم والغم والفرح والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة والإدراك والعمى وغير ذلك مما يحدث في النفس مما يدركه الحي إذا وجد به، ومنه أيضاً العلم الواقع بقصد المتكلم إلى ما يقصد يقصده بخطابه دون غيره وأنه قاصد إلى اكتساب ما يوجد به من ضروب مقدوراته من الكلام وغيره، ومنه أيضاً العلم بأن الأجسام متى موجودة فلا بد من أن تكون مجتمعة متماسّة الأبعاض أو مفترقة متباينة وأن الخبر عن وجود الشيء وأنه على بعض الأوصاف لا بد أن يكون صدقاً أو كذباً وأن الخبرين المتضادّ مُخبَرُهُما لا يجوز أن يكونا جميعاً صدقاً أو كذباً وما كان ذلك جرى مجرى من الأمور المنقسمة في العقل إلى أمرين لا واسطة بينهما. ومنه أيضاً العلم بخجل الخَجِل وَوَجَل الوَجل والعلم بالشجاعة والجبن والبر والعُقوق والتحية والاستهزاء والواقع عند مشاهدة الامارات، ومنه أيضاً العلم المُخْتَرَع في النفس بما تواتر الخبر عن كونه واستفاض عن وجوده نحو العلم الواقع عند إخبار المخبرين عن الصين وخراسان وفارس وكرمان وعن ظهور موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلى الله عليه وعلى جميع النبيين والخبر عن الوقائع والفتن والممالك والدول وغير ذلك من الأمور الحاصل الخبر عنها من قوم قطع العُذر نَقلُهم ووجب العلم عند خبرهم.
And among them is that which exists by some of these senses, like a person's knowledge of himself and what he finds in himself of health and sickness, pleasure and pain, grief and joy, ability and inability, will and dislike, perception and blindness, and other things that occur in the soul which the living being perceives when it exists with it. And from it also is the knowledge that occurs by the intention of the speaker to what he intends by his speech and nothing else, and that he intends to acquire what is found in it of the kinds of his capabilities, such as speech and other things. And from it also is the knowledge that bodies, whenever they exist, must be either joined, with parts touching, or separate and distinct; and that the report of the existence of a thing and that it is in some state must be either true or false; and that two conflicting reports cannot both be true or both false; and that which is like that among matters that are divided in the intellect into two things with no intermediary between them. And from it also is the knowledge of the shame of the ashamed and the fear of the fearful, and the knowledge of courage and cowardice, piety and filial impiety, greeting and mockery, which occurs when observing indications. And from it also is the knowledge created in the soul by what the report has been recurrently transmitted about its existence and widespread about its occurrence, like the knowledge that occurs upon the reports of informants about China, Khurasan, Persia, and Kirman, and about the appearance of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon him and upon all the prophets), and the report about events, trials, kingdoms, states, and other matters about which the report comes from a people whose transmission removes excuse and knowledge becomes necessary upon their report.

Key Takeaway
The intellect necessarily divides certain matters into two and only two opposites with no intermediate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Do you treat the law of non-contradiction as an arbitrary human convention or as a reflection of how Allah created your intellect? What would change in your worship if you fully embraced that some truths are undeniable by reason?

Knowledge of Inner States from External Signs
Baqillani describes knowledge of inner qualities via external signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that we can know another person's inner states (like shame or courage) by observing external signs, a type of knowledge not based on direct testimony.

Core Idea
A blush tells you of shame—knowledge from signs.

Al-Baqillani now discusses how we can gain knowledge of others' inner qualities through observable signs. When we see someone blushing, we know they are ashamed; when we see someone acting bravely, we know their courage. This knowledge is derived from the external indications (imarat) that accompany these states.

Guided Path
1
List the pairs in the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, then list the pairs of inner states and their external signs (e.g., shame of the ashamed, fear of the fearful).
Each pair shows how knowledge of the inner is gained from the outer.
2
Understand 'imarat'
The key term is 'الامارات' (signs). How are these signs different from direct testimony or logical necessity?
They are physical or behavioral clues.
3
Think of a modern example
Can you think of a situation where you know someone's emotion from their expression without them saying anything?
This is exactly the type of knowledge Baqillani describes.

Knowledge from Recurrent Reports (Mutawatir)
Baqillani explains mutawatir reports as a source of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that recurrent (mutawatir) reports produce certain knowledge in the soul, even without direct observation.

Core Idea
Widespread, uninterrupted reports can generate certain knowledge.

The final category Baqillani presents is perhaps the most important for theology: knowledge generated by tawatur—recurrent transmission. When a report is transmitted by such a large number of people that collusion on falsehood is impossible, it creates certain knowledge in the soul. Examples include knowledge of distant lands and historical events like the appearance of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and examples
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the examples: China, Khurasan, Persia, Kirman, and the prophets Moses, Jesus, Muhammad.
These are historical and geographical facts known through mutawatir.
2
Understand the condition
The phrase 'من قوم قطع العذر نقلهم' – what does it mean for a group's transmission to 'remove excuse'?
It means their number and credibility make doubt impossible.
3
Result: necessary knowledge
The passage ends with 'وجب العلم عند خبرهم' – knowledge becomes necessary upon their report. Why is this important for Islamic theology?
It establishes the certainty of prophetic histories and the Quran.

Knowledge Independent of the Senses
Baqillani explains that most knowledges are created in the soul independent of senses, except for two types that currently depend on perception, but even those can be received directly through miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that most knowledge is created directly in the soul by God independent of the senses, but that knowledge from recurrent reports and from bodily signs currently depends on sensory perception, and this dependence can be overridden by divine miracles.

Core Idea
Most knowledge comes directly from God, not the senses – except two types that currently need your eyes and ears.

We often assume that all knowledge comes through our five senses. But Imam Baqillani, the great Ash'ari theologian, argues that most knowledges are created in the soul by God, regardless of whether senses exist or not. He does, however, acknowledge two exceptions that currently depend on perception – though even those can be bypassed by divine power. This passage clarifies the relationship between God's direct creation of knowledge and the sensory means we normally experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a general rule, then an exception, then a note on miracles.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the general rule and the exceptions
Baqillani says most knowledges are 'created in the soul' regardless of senses. Then he mentions two exceptions that currently need perception: knowledge from recurrent reports and knowledge from bodily signs (like shame or intention). List these.
The exceptions are not permanent – they are 'in our present time' dependent.
3
Reflect on the role of miracles
Baqillani adds that even these exceptions can be known directly by God's power during times of miracles. This shows God's absolute omnipotence over ordinary causality.
This reinforces the Ash'ari emphasis on divine power over natural laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكل هذه العلوم الواقعة لنا بالمعلومات التي وصفناها مُختَرَعةٌ في النفس، وُجِدَت هذه الحواس وما يوجد بها من الإدراكات أو لم توجد سوى العلم الواقع عند الخبر المتواتر والعلم بخجل الخجل ووَجَل الوَجل وقصد القاصد إلى من يقصده وما يقصده بكلامه، فإنه وما جرى مجراه في وقتنا هذا مُضَمَّن بوجود الإدراك للخبر عن المعلوم بمشاهدة الامارات التي عند مشاهدتها يقع العلم بما ذكرناه وقد يصح الله يخترع العلم بوجود المُخبر عنه من غير سماع عنه في الزمن الذي يصح فيه خرقُ العادات وإظهار المعجزات وخروج الأمور عما هي في العادة عليه.
So all these knowledges that occur to us concerning the information we have described are created in the soul, whether these senses and the perceptions that occur through them exist or not, except for the knowledge that occurs through recurrent report, and the knowledge of the shame of the ashamed and the fear of the fearful, and the intention of the one intending to whom he intends and what he intends by his speech; for it and what is like it in our present time is dependent on the existence of perception of the report about the known thing by observing the indications upon whose observation the knowledge of what we mentioned occurs. And God may create knowledge of the existence of the reported thing without hearing about it in a time in which it is proper to break customs, manifest miracles, and for matters to depart from what is customary.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a clarification that most of the aforementioned knowledges are created in the soul independently of whether the senses and their perceptions exist or not. However, two types are currently dependent on sensory perception: (1) knowledge from recurrent reports, and (2) knowledge from signs (such as knowing shame from blushing) and knowledge of intention from speech. These presently require perception of the report or the sign. But even these can be created by God without any sensory means during times of miracles, when the normal laws of nature are broken. Thus the author affirms divine omnipotence in creating knowledge directly, while acknowledging that under normal circumstances some knowledges require sensory input.

Key Terms

The Certainty of Tawatur
Baqillani defines knowledge from tawatur as divinely created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge from mutawatir reports is created directly in the soul by Allah, independent of sensory perception.

Core Idea
Mutawatir knowledge is divinely created in the soul, not derived from senses.

Baqillani begins by establishing that certain types of knowledge, such as historical reports about prophets and events, come from mutawatir transmission. He argues that this knowledge is created directly in the soul by Allah, regardless of whether the senses exist. This lesson explores that concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Baqillani contrasts mutawatir knowledge with sensory perception.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مختَرعة في النفس' (created in the soul).
2
Identify the types of knowledge listed
The passage mentions knowledge from reports, shame, fear, intention, etc. Reflect on why these are grouped together.
3
Understand the independence from senses
Baqillani explicitly says this knowledge occurs whether senses exist or not. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the source of certainty?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبيين والخبر عن الوقائع والفتن والممالك والدول وغير ذلك من الأمور الحاصل الخبر عنها عن قوم قطع العذر نقلهم ووجب العلم عند خبرهم. فكل هذه العلوم الواقعة لنا بالمعلومات التي وصفناها مختَرعة في النفس، وُجِدَت هذه الحواس وما يوجد بها من الإدراكات أو لم توجد سوى العلم الواقع عند الخبر المتواتر والعلم بخجل الخجل ووجَل الوَجل وقصد القاصد إلى من يقصده وما يقصده بكلامه وما جرى مجرى ذلك، فإنه وما جرى مجراه في وقتنا هذا مضمَّن بوجود الإدراك للخبر عن المعلوم بمشاهدة الإمارات التي عند مشاهدتها يقع العلم بما ذكرناه وقد يصح الله يخترع العلم بوجود المخبر عنه من غير سماع خبر عنه في الزمن الذي يصح فيه خرق العادات وإظهار المعجزات وخروج الأمور عما هي في العادة عليه وتسميتهم الإدراكات الموجودة بالجوارح والحواس لمساً وذوقاً وشماً إنما جرت عليها على سبيل المجاز والاتساع لما بينه وبينها من التعلق على طريقتهم في التجوز بإجراء اسم الشيء على ما قاربه وناسبه وتعلق به ضرباً من التعلق، والإدراك في الحقيقة شيء غير اللمس واتصاف سائر الحواس بالمحسوسات وأماكنها وغيرها من ضروب الاتصال.
The prophets and the report about events, trials, kingdoms, states, and other matters about which the report is obtained from a people whose transmission cuts off the excuse and knowledge becomes obligatory upon their report. So all these sciences that occur to us through the information we have described are created in the soul, whether these senses and the perceptions that exist through them exist or not, except for the knowledge that occurs at the report of tawatur and the knowledge of the shame of the shameful, the fear of the fearful, the intention of the one intending to the one he intends and what he intends by his speech and what runs in that course, for it and what runs in its course in our time are conjoined with the existence of perception of the report about the known through witnessing the signs which, when witnessed, knowledge occurs of what we mentioned. And God may create knowledge of the existence of the reported about without hearing a report about it in the time in which it is valid to break customs, manifest miracles, and for matters to exit from what they are upon in the customary state. And their naming of the perceptions that exist through the limbs and senses as touch, taste, and smell is only conducted upon them by way of metaphor and extension due to the connection between it and them, in their way of using metaphor by applying the name of a thing to what approximates it, is appropriate to it, and is connected to it by a kind of connection. And perception in reality is something other than touch and the description of the other senses by the sensed objects and their places and other than the kinds of connection.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of epistemology, focusing on two types of knowledge: knowledge from tawatur (mutawatir reports) and knowledge from sensory perception. Baqillani argues that knowledge from tawatur is certain and created directly in the soul by God, regardless of whether the senses exist. He then explains that sensory perceptions (touch, taste, smell) are actually metaphorical; real perception is something else. The passage also mentions that God can create knowledge directly without a report during prophetic miracles. The key point is that true knowledge is not dependent on physical senses but is divinely created.

Correction: Baqillani argues that mutawatir reports produce certain knowledge created directly in the soul.

First Method of Istidlal: Division into Exclusive Parts
Baqillani explains the first method of reasoning: division into mutually exclusive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of division (qismah) works in kalam reasoning and why it is logically valid.

Core Idea
Division eliminates all but one option, leaving the intellect no choice but truth.

Reasoning in kalam often starts by dividing possibilities into mutually exclusive categories. Baqillani introduces this as the first method of istidlal. In the passage below, he explains how a proof can eliminate one division, forcing the intellect to accept the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage slowly
Read the Arabic once without looking at the translation. Try to identify the key terms: استدلال, قسم, قدم, حدث.
Notice the conditional structure: 'فإن قال قائل... قيل له'.
2
Understand the logical structure
The method works by dividing a thing into two (or more) divisions that cannot all be true. A proof then invalidates one, leaving the other as necessarily true.
This is essentially proof by elimination.
3
Study the example
The example is the division of existence into pre-eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith). If a proof shows something is originated, pre-eternity is automatically false.
This example is foundational for proving God's attributes.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Take the quiz to confirm your understanding of the method's mechanism.
The answer is found in the phrase 'فيقضي العقل على صحة ضده'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الاستدلال. فإن قال قائل: فعلى كم وجه ينقسم الاستدلال؟ قيل له: على وجوه يكثر تعدادها، فمنها أن ينقسم الشيء في العقل على قسمين أو أقسام يستحيل أن تجتمع كلها في الصحة والفساد، فيبطل الدليل أحد القسمين فيقضي العقل على صحة ضده وكذلك إن أفسد الدليل سائر الأقسام صحح العقل الباقي منها لا محالة، نظير ذلك علمنا باستحالة خروج الشيء عن القدم والحدث فمتى قام الدليل على حدوثه بطل قدمه ولو قام على قدمه لأفسد حدثه.
Chapter on reasoning. For if someone says: Upon how many divisions is reasoning divided? It is said to him: Into many divisions, among them that a thing is divided in the intellect into two divisions or divisions such that it is impossible for all of them to be together in validity and invalidity, so the proof invalidates one of the two divisions, and the intellect judges the validity of its opposite; and likewise if the proof invalidates the remaining divisions, the intellect corrects the one remaining inevitably. An example of that is our knowledge of the impossibility of a thing being outside of pre-eternity and temporal origination, so when the proof establishes its temporal origination, its pre-eternity is invalidated, and if it established its pre-eternity, it would invalidate its temporal origination.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on istidlal (reasoning/demonstration) and presents the first method: division (qismah). The method works by dividing a concept into two or more mutually exclusive and exhaustive categories. A proof then eliminates all but one category, forcing the intellect to accept the remaining category as true. The example given is the division of existence into pre-eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith). If a proof shows something is originated, pre-eternity is automatically false, and vice versa. This is a foundational logical tool in kalam for proving attributes of God.

Key Terms
استدلال
قسم
قدم
حدث
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this method is circular or arbitrary.
Correction: It is a valid logical deduction based on mutually exclusive and exhaustive categories. The proof eliminates one option, so the intellect rationally accepts the other.

Checkpoint
In the first method of istidlal, what does the intellect do after the proof invalidates one division?
A) It doubts both divisions
B) It judges the opposite division as valid
C) It seeks a third division
D) It rejects the proof

Key Takeaway
The method of division is a rational tool: if a proof eliminates one of two mutually exclusive possibilities, the intellect must accept the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This method forces the intellect to accept truth when falsehood is eliminated. Are there areas in your life where you have avoided eliminating false options because the truth would be uncomfortable? What false pretense have you been clinging to that this lesson demands you discard?
?
The example uses pre-eternity and temporal origination. When you consider your own existence, do you truly accept that you were brought into being from nothing, or do you secretly entertain the possibility of being eternal? What proof in your heart has eliminated that possibility?
?
The method requires categories that are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. In your understanding of God, have you considered that He is either One or not One? What proof have you seen that eliminates all but His Oneness, and does your life reflect that conviction?

Method of Learning
