Shahid-Ghaib Analogy by Cause
Baqillani explains reasoning from witnessed to unseen by common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen is justified by a common cause ('illah) and why this method is essential for kalam arguments about divine attributes.

Core Idea
If an attribute in the witnessed requires a cause, the same cause must apply in the unseen.

How can we know about the unseen world based on what we observe? Imam al-Baqillani presents a powerful method: if a certain attribute in the witnessed world always arises from a specific cause, then whenever that attribute is described in the unseen, the same cause must be present. This causal analogy is not mere induction but a logical necessity, as the absence of the cause would remove the basis for the attribute. In this lesson, you'll examine this foundational reasoning that underpins many theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani states the principle first, then gives examples.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument moves from: (1) a judgment in the witnessed due to a cause, (2) applying that judgment to the unseen when the attribute is present, (3) supporting examples, (4) the impossibility of the judgment without the cause.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples: body and composition, knower and knowledge. How do they illustrate the principle? What would happen if the cause were absent?
Think about how this applies to proving God's attributes from human attributes.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that this is induction. But Baqillani insists it is a causal necessity. Why is it not induction?
Induction only observes correlation; this is about causal connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن يجب الحكم والوصف للشيء في الشاهد لعلة ما فيجب القضاء على أن من وصف بتلك الصفة في الغائب فحكمه في أنه مستحق لها لتلك العلة حكم مستحقها في الشاهد لأنه يستحيل قيام دليل على مستحق الوصف بتلك الصفة مع عدم ما يوجبها وذلك كعلمنا أن الجسم إنما كان جسماً لتأليفه وأن العالم إنما كان عالماً لوجود علمه؛ فوجب القضاء بإثبات علم كل من وصف بأنه عالم وتأليف كل من وصف بأنه جسم أو مجتمع لأن الحكم العقلي المستحق لعلة لا يجوز أن يستحق عدمها ولا لأجل وجود شيء يخالفها لأن ذلك يخرجها عن أن تكون علة للحكم.
And among them is that a judgment and attribute is necessarily established for a thing in the witnessed (shahid) due to some cause, so it is necessary to judge that whoever is described with that attribute in the unseen (gha'ib) has the same judgment as the one deserving it in the witnessed, because it is impossible for a proof to stand for one deserving the description with that attribute in the absence of what necessitates it. And that is like our knowledge that the body is only a body due to its composition, and that the knower is only a knower due to the existence of his knowledge. So it is necessary to judge the affirmation of knowledge for everyone described as knower, and composition for everyone described as body or composite, because the rational judgment that is deserved for a cause is not permissible to be deserved without it, nor due to the existence of something that contradicts it, because that would remove it from being a cause for the judgment.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of istidlal: reasoning from the shahid (observed world) to the gha'ib (unseen) based on a common 'illah (cause). The principle is: if in the observed world a certain attribute (like being a body) is always accompanied by a specific cause (composition), then whenever that attribute is found in the unseen, the same cause must be present. The examples illustrate: bodies are bodies because of composition, so any body in the unseen must be composite; knowers are knowers because of knowledge, so any knower must have knowledge. This method is crucial for proving divine attributes by analogy with human attributes, while maintaining the distinction of God's transcendence.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
علة
حكم
جسم
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This method is just induction from observed correlations.
Correction: It is not induction but a causal necessity: the attribute in the witnessed is inherently linked to its cause, so the same link must hold in the unseen. Induction only notes regular conjunction, whereas this reasoning asserts a necessary connection based on the nature of the attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for the shahid-ghaib analogy to be valid according to Baqillani?
A) The attribute must be observable in both realms
B) There must be a common cause ('illah) for the attribute
C) The unseen must be identical to the witnessed
D) The proof must be mathematical

Key Takeaway
Reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen is valid only when the same cause underlies the attribute in both realms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is 'alim (knower) because knowledge must be in Him as a necessary attribute, then why do you sometimes act as if He does not see your private thoughts and deeds? What would change if you internalized that His knowledge is as real as your own?
?
This lesson shows that an attribute cannot exist without its cause. In your own life, do you claim to have faith (iman) without its cause—namely, sincere submission and trust? What cause must be present in you for your faith to be genuine?
?
Baqillani argues that it is impossible for a rational judgment to be deserved without its cause. If you believe God's mercy encompasses everything, then what cause—what attribute—must necessarily be in God? How does this compel you to respond to His mercy today?

Inference by Similarity
Baqillani describes inference from validity or impossibility to its like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of inference by similarity (tamthil) and how it applies to divine attributes and impossibilities.

Core Idea
What is true for one thing is true for its exact like.

This lesson introduces a key method of rational inference used by kalam scholars: reasoning from the validity or impossibility of a thing to the same ruling for its like. Baqillani illustrates this with examples from divine power and impossibility. Understanding this method is essential for grasping how theologians argue from observed reality to unseen matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Baqillani.
2
Identify the principle
The passage states that from the validity of a thing, one infers the validity of its like, and similarly for impossibility. This is the core of inference by similarity.
3
Examine the examples
Baqillani gives two examples: one positive (God's power to create a like) and one negative (impossibility of blackness without place). See how each follows from the principle.
The examples ground the abstract principle in concrete theological cases.
4
Reflect on the condition
The method requires exact likeness in all relevant respects. The common misunderstanding is that this is not a general analogy, but a strict inference from one case to its precise like.
This ensures the validity of the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك أن يستدل بصحة الشيء على صحة مثله، وما هو في معناه، وباستحالته على استحالة مثله وما كان بمعناه كاستدلالنا على إثبات قدرة القديم سبحانه على خلق جوهر ولون مثل الذي خلقه وإحياء ميت مثل الذي أحياه وخلق الحياة فيه مرة أخرى بعد أن أماته وعلى استحالة خلق شيء من جنس السواد والحركات لا في مكان في الماضي كما استحال ذلك في جنسهما الموجود في وقتنا هذا.
And among that is that one infers from the validity of a thing to the validity of its like, and what is in its meaning, and from its impossibility to the impossibility of its like and what is in its meaning, like our inference for establishing the power of the Eternal (سبحانه) to create a substance or color like the one He created, and to resurrect a dead person like the one He resurrected, and to create life in him again after causing him to die, and upon the impossibility of creating something of the genus of blackness and movements without a place in the past, just as that is impossible in their genus existing in our time.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage explains the third method: inference by similarity (tamthil). The principle is that if a thing is valid (sahih), its exact like (mithl) is also valid; if a thing is impossible (mustahil), its like is also impossible. The example applies to divine power: since God has created a substance or color in the observed world, He has the power to create its like. Similarly, since it is impossible in the observed world for blackness or movement to exist without a location, it is also impossible for God to create such things in the past. This method relies on the uniformity of possibility and impossibility across similar cases.

Key Terms
صحة
استحالة
مثل
قدرة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that inference by similarity is a general analogy that can be applied loosely.
Correction: Baqillani's method requires exact likeness ('مثله' and 'ما هو في معناه'), not just resemblance. It is a strict inference from one case to its precise like.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as an example to illustrate inference from impossibility?
A) Creating a substance without a color
B) Creating blackness or movement without a place
C) Resurrecting a dead person
D) Creating a like of a body

Key Takeaway
Inference by similarity allows us to extend the ruling of validity or impossibility from a known case to its exact like.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship that seems impossible to overcome, do you truly believe that God, who has already brought about similar relief in the past, can do the same again? Where is your trust lacking, and what would change if you fully applied this principle of inference by similarity to your own life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a miracle or a reported sign of God because it seems too unusual? Reflect on how the consistency of impossibility across similar cases can actually protect your faith from demanding logical impossibilities, while also opening your heart to what God has already demonstrated as possible. Will you let this lesson silence the doubts that arise from false comparisons?
?
Consider a command of God that you find difficult to obey. Do you assume it is impossible for you to follow? The passage shows that what is possible for one like case is possible for another. If others have obeyed, how can you deny the possibility for yourself? What concrete step will you take today to act on that possibility?

Method of Elimination in Rational Proof
Baqillani defines the method of elimination with the example of pre-eternity and temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of elimination works: when a proof eliminates one of two mutually exclusive possibilities, the mind necessarily affirms the other.

Core Idea
Elimination forces the mind to affirm the only remaining possibility.

Baqillani introduces the first of two rational methods—elimination of opposites. He explains that when a valid proof destroys one of two mutually exclusive states, reason has no choice but to accept the other. This method is foundational for proving the existence and attributes of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on elimination
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: if one of two opposites is invalidated, the mind judges the correctness of the other.
The passage uses 'فيُبطل' and 'فيقضي' to show the sequence.
2
Understand the example of pre-eternity and temporality
Baqillani gives the concrete example of a thing being either pre-eternal or temporal. If proof establishes one, the other is invalidated. This illustrates the general rule.
This example is crucial for theological proofs about the world's origin.
3
Reflect on why the mind cannot accept both or neither
Think about why the two states are exhaustive and mutually exclusive. The method works only when the possibilities are truly exhaustive. This is a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلّها في الصحة والفساد ، فيُبطل الدليلُ أحد القسمين فيقضي العقل على صحة ضدّه وكذلك إن أفسد الدليل سائر الأقسام صحح العقل الباقي منها لا محالة، نظير ذلك علمنا باستحالة خروج الشيء عن القدم والحدث فمتى قام الدليل على حَدَيْه بطل قِدَمُه ولو قام على قدمه لأفسد حَدَثَه . ومنها أن يجب الحكم والوصف للشيء في الشاهد لعلة ما فيجب القضاء على أن من وُصف بتلك الصفة في الغائب فحكمه في أنه مستحق لها لتلك العلة حكم مستحقها في الشاهد لأنه يستحيل قيام دليل على مستحق الوصف بتلك الصفة مع عدم ما يوجبها وذلك كعلمنا أن الجسم إنما كان جسماً لتأليفه وأن العالم إنما كان عالماً لوجود علمه ؛ فوجب القضاء بإثبات علم كل من وصف بأنه عالم وتأليف كل من وصف بأنه جسم أو مجتمع لأن الحكم العقلي المُسْتَحَق لعلة لا يجوز أن يُسْتَحَق عدمها ولا مع لوجود شيء يخالفها لأن ذلك يُخرجها عن أن تكون علة للحكم.
All of them [are] concerning correctness and corruption: the proof invalidates one of the two divisions, so the mind judges the correctness of its opposite. Similarly, if the proof invalidates the remaining divisions, the mind corrects the remaining one inevitably. An example of that is our knowledge of the impossibility of a thing exiting from pre-eternity and temporality; so when proof is established for its temporality, its pre-eternity is invalidated, and if it is established for its pre-eternity, it would invalidate its temporality. And among them is that the judgment and attribute for a thing in the witnessed [world] must be due to a cause; so it must be judged that whoever is described with that attribute in the unseen has the same judgment regarding being entitled to it due to that cause as the one entitled to it in the witnessed [world], because it is impossible for proof to be established for one entitled to the description with that attribute while lacking what necessitates it. That is like our knowledge that the body is only a body due to its composition, and that the knower is only knowing due to the existence of his knowledge; so it is necessary to judge the affirmation of knowledge for everyone described as knowing, and composition for everyone described as a body or composite, because the rational judgment entitled due to a cause is not permissible that its absence be entitled, nor with the existence of something that contradicts it, because that would remove it from being a cause for the judgment.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental methods of rational proof. First, the method of elimination: when a proof invalidates one of two possibilities, the mind necessarily affirms the opposite. For example, we know a thing must be either pre-eternal or temporal; if proof establishes its temporality, pre-eternity is negated, and vice versa. Second, the method of causation: attributes in the witnessed world are linked to their causes, so this linkage must apply to the unseen as well. For instance, a body is a body due to composition; therefore, anything described as a body must be composite. Similarly, a knower is knower due to knowledge, so any knower must have knowledge. This is because a rational judgment based on a cause cannot exist without that cause, nor can it be negated while the cause exists.

Key Terms
الدليل
القسمين
القدم
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for a thing to be neither pre-eternal nor temporal.
Correction: Baqillani states that it is impossible for a thing to exit from both pre-eternity and temporality; they are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if proof establishes the temporality of something, what is the rational outcome?
A) Its pre-eternity is also affirmed.
B) Its pre-eternity is invalidated.
C) Both pre-eternity and temporality are invalidated.
D) The mind suspends judgment.

Key Takeaway
When a proof eliminates one of two mutually exclusive possibilities, the mind must affirm the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that when proof eliminates one possibility, the mind must affirm the other. What possibility in your own life have you been avoiding affirming, even though the proof is clear? Will you accept it now?
?
Consider the two possibilities for your ultimate purpose: living for yourself or living for your Creator. What proof has already eliminated one? Have you truly internalized that elimination, or are you still pretending both are open?

Method of Causation: From Witnessed to Unseen
Baqillani explains the method of causation with examples from body and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational method of causation: how attributes in the witnessed world imply the same causal relationship in the unseen, because a rational judgment due to a cause cannot be separated from that cause.

Core Idea
A cause-based attribute in the witnessed world applies equally in the unseen.

Baqillani now turns to the second method—causation. He argues that when an attribute in the witnessed world is necessarily linked to a cause, the same linkage must hold for the unseen. This is not mere analogy but a rational necessity, because the very concept of a cause-based judgment demands its continued presence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on causation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: general principle, examples, and rational justification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يجب الحكم والوصف للشيء في الشاهد لعلة ما'.
2
Understand the examples: body and knower
Baqillani gives two examples: a body is a body due to composition, and a knower is knowing due to knowledge. These show the causal link in the witnessed world.
These examples are typical in kalam for establishing attributes of Allah.
3
Grasp the principle: cause cannot be absent when effect is present
Baqillani states it is impossible for a proof to establish an attribute (like knowing) without its cause (knowledge). The same applies in the unseen.
4
Reflect on why this applies to the unseen
The principle is rational and universal. If a cause is necessary for an attribute here, it must be necessary for that attribute anywhere, because the cause-effect relationship is intrinsic.
This method is key for inferring attributes of God from creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Reasoning by Analogy from the Similar
Baqillani states the rule of inference by likeness and gives two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's principle that the ruling of possibility or impossibility applies uniformly to all members of a kind, and how this underlies theological reasoning from witnessed to unseen.

Core Idea
What holds for one thing holds for its exact like.

Baqillani presents a foundational principle of analogical reasoning: the ruling (correctness or impossibility) of a thing applies equally to anything that is exactly like it. This principle is crucial for extending what we know from the witnessed world to matters of the unseen, especially in arguments about God's power and the nature of created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let Baqillani's own structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the parallel structure: 'صحة الشيء على صحة مثله' and 'استحالته على استحالة مثله'.
2
Identify the two types of inference
The passage presents two directions: inference from correctness to correctness, and from impossibility to impossibility. Underline these in the text.
3
Examine the examples
Baqillani gives two examples: God's power to create like substances/colors, and the impossibility of blackness/movements without location. See how each exemplifies the principle.
The first is a positive inference (possible), the second negative (impossible).
4
Connect to broader theological method
Reflect: Why is this principle important for kalām? It allows arguments from the observed world to the unseen realm without leaping beyond analogy.
This is a standard Sunni method for defending divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك أن يُستدل بصحة الشيء على صحة مثله، وما هو في معناه، وباستحالته على استحالة مثله وما كان بمعناه كاستدلالنا على إثبات قدرة القديم سبحانه على خلق جوهر ولون مثل الذي خلقه وإحياء ميت مثل الذي أحياه وخلق الحياة فيه مرة أخرى بعد أن أماته وعلى استحالة خلق شيء من جنس السواد والحركات لا في مكان في الماضي كما استحال ذلك في جنسهما الموجود في وقتنا هذا
And from that is that one may infer from the correctness of a thing to the correctness of its like and what is in its meaning, and from its impossibility to the impossibility of its like and what is in its meaning, like our inference to establish the power of the Eternal, exalted is He, to create a substance and a color like the one He created, and to revive a dead [person] like the one He revived, and to create life in it again after causing it to die, and upon the impossibility of creating something of the kind of blackness and movements not in a place in the past, just as that is impossible in their kind existing in our present time.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the method of inference by analogy from similarity. If a thing is proven correct or possible, then its exact like is also correct or possible. Conversely, if a thing is impossible, its like is also impossible. The example given is God's power: since He has created a substance or color and revived a dead person, He can create another similar substance or color and revive another dead person. Similarly, since it is impossible for blackness or movements to exist without location in the present, it was also impossible for them to exist without location in the past. This reasoning relies on the principle that the same kind of thing has the same ruling.

Key Terms
مثله
في معناه
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy from witnessed to unseen is invalid because God's power is unique, so what holds for created things does not apply to God.
Correction: Baqillani's inference is not from created to Creator but from one instance of a kind to another instance of the same kind within God's actions. The principle is that if a certain kind of action (e.g., creating a substance) is possible for God based on what He has done, then a like action is also possible. This does not compare God to creation but compares actions of the same kind.

Checkpoint
What is the example Baqillani uses to demonstrate inference from impossibility in the present to impossibility in the past?
A) Creating a substance
B) Reviving a dead person
C) Blackness and movements not existing in a place
D) Creating life after death

Key Takeaway
The same kind of thing carries the same ruling of possibility or impossibility, allowing sound inference from the witnessed to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the miracles you have heard of—like the revival of the dead—do you truly believe that the same power that performed them can act in your life today, or do you subconsciously limit God's ability because you haven't witnessed it with your own eyes? What would change if you consistently applied Baqillani's principle?
?
Think of a situation where you have judged something as impossible for yourself (e.g., changing a deep habit). Are you holding to that judgment based on your own experience alone, or are you open to the possibility that God can do the like of what He has done for others? What would it mean to repent of that limitation?
?
In your discussions about matters of the unseen—like the afterlife or the nature of God—do you rely on the same logical consistency you use in daily life, or do you make exceptions? How would your faith deepen if you insisted on the same principle of likeness in your theology?

How Language Fixes Meaning in Testimony
Baqillani uses linguistic definitions as proof for the stability of meaning in reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how shared linguistic definitions serve as a proof that reports about unseen things refer to the same realities we experience.

Core Idea
Language anchors the meaning of words across reports.

Baqillani presents a key methodological principle: when a truthful speaker reports seeing something, the meaning of that thing is fixed by the language they use. This passage explains how the definitions given by the people of language (e.g., 'fire' means hot and blazing) guarantee that the reporter refers to the same entity we know. This is crucial for arguments based on testimony about the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the structure: Baqillani first states the linguistic definitions, then applies them to reports.
The word 'توقيف' refers to instruction or determination of meanings.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find the two examples Baqillani gives: fire is hot and blazing, a human has a specific constitution. These are given by the people of language.
3
Understand the inference
See how Baqillani argues that a truthful reporter, using the same language, must mean the same thing. The report's meaning is fixed by linguistic convention.
This counters the idea that reports about the unseen could refer to something else entirely.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: why is this important for theology and epistemology? How does it allow us to build knowledge from testimony?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بتوقيف أهل اللغة لنا على أنه لا نارَ إلا حارة ملتهبة ولا إنسان إلا ما كانت له هذه البَنْيَةُ على أن كل من خبرنا من الصادقين بأنه رأى ناراً أو إنساناً، وهو من أهل لُغَتِنا، يقصد إلى إفهامنا أنه ما شاهد إلا مثل ما سُمّي بحضرتنا ناراً أو إنساناً، لا نحمل بعض ذلك على بعض، لكن بموجب الاسم وموضوع اللغة ووجوب استعمال الكلام على ما استعملوه ووضعه حيث وضعوه
And one may infer by the instruction of the people of language to us that there is no fire except hot blazing [fire], and no human except one with this constitution, that everyone truthful who informs us that he saw a fire or a human, and he is from the people of our language, intending to make us understand that he only witnessed what is like what is named in our presence as fire or human, not [that] we carry some of that upon others, but by virtue of the name and the subject of language and the obligation of using speech as they used it and placed it where they placed it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage discusses the use of linguistic definitions as proof. Language informs us that a fire is necessarily hot and blazing, and a human has a specific constitution. Therefore, when a truthful speaker reports that he saw a fire or a human, he means the same thing that we call fire or human. The inference is not based on arbitrary analogy but on the fixed meanings of words in the language. This ensures that reports about unseen things refer to the same realities we know.

Key Terms
توقيف
أهل اللغة
الاسم
موضوع اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reports about unseen things could refer to completely different entities (e.g., a 'fire' in the afterlife might not be hot).
Correction: Baqillani argues that language fixes the meaning: the word 'fire' necessarily implies heat and blazing, so any use of that word refers to that same reality.

Checkpoint

A) Because the reporter is truthful
B) Because language defines fire as hot and blazing
C) Because we have direct experience
D) Because miracles confirm it

Key Takeaway
Linguistic definitions fix the meaning of words, so truthful reports about unseen things refer to the same realities we know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you subconsciously treat them as metaphors or exaggerations? What one step can you take today to train your heart to take those reports with full literal certainty?
?
Baqillani argues that the truthful reporter intends to make us understand exactly what he witnessed. Do you live as if the Prophet's words are perfectly clear and fixed, or do you allow your own desires or doubts to reinterpret them? Commit to one teaching from the Quran or Sunnah that you will accept literally from now on, without mental reservations.
?

Will you take a concrete step today—such as looking up the definition of one key term you may have misunderstood—to ensure your understanding aligns with the linguistic community?

Scriptural Proofs as Branches of Rational Proofs
Baqillani lists scriptural proofs and asserts they are branches of rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scriptural proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) are not independent but are subsidiary to rational proofs in Islamic theology and legal methodology.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs are branches of rational proofs, not independent sources.

Baqillani continues his discussion of evidence by turning to scriptural proofs. While these are commonly cited in legal and theological arguments, he emphasizes that they are secondary to rational proofs. This passage explains the relationship and ensures we do not overstate their role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scriptural proofs listed
Read the passage and note the four sources mentioned: the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the community, and legal analogy derived from spoken principles.
These are the classic sources of Islamic law.
2
Understand their relationship to rational proofs
Notice that Baqillani says these scriptural proofs are 'branches' (فرع) of the proofs of intellects and its judgments. They are subordinate, not co-equal.
This ordering is foundational for kalam methodology.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if scriptural proofs depend on rational proofs, then reason must be the starting point for theological inquiry. This shapes how we evaluate evidence.
This connects to the broader theme of the primacy of reason in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُسْتَدّل أيضاً على بعض القضايا العقلية وعلى الأحكام الشرعية بالكتاب والسنة وإجماع الأمة والقياس الشرعي المُنتزع من الأصول المنطوق بها وما جرى مجرى القياس على العلة من ضرب الاجتهاد الذي يسوغ الحكم بمثله من الشرع على مذهب القايسين، فكل هذه الأدلة السمعية جارية في الكشف عن صحة القياس مجرى ما قدمنا ذكره من الأحكام العقلية، وإن كان فرعاً لأدلة العقول وقضاياها، وفيما أومأنا إليه من وجوه الاستدلال كفاية وتنبيه على ما أضربنا عن ذكره
And one may also infer regarding some rational issues and regarding legal rulings by the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the community, and legal analogy derived from the spoken principles and what runs the course of analogy on the cause from the type of ijtihad that permits judgment by its like from the law according to the school of the analogists. All of these scriptural proofs run in revealing the correctness of analogy in the way of what we have mentioned previously of rational judgments, even if they are branches of the proofs of intellects and its judgments. And in what we have indicated of the ways of inference there is sufficiency and a reminder of what we have omitted.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage lists the forms of scriptural evidence: the Quran, Sunnah, consensus (ijma), and legal analogy (qiyas). Baqillani states that these are all used to infer rational and legal rulings. He connects them to the rational proofs discussed earlier: scriptural proofs operate in the same manner as rational proofs in revealing the correctness of analogy. However, they are subsidiary (far') to the rational proofs. The passage concludes that the discussion has covered sufficient methods of inference, and what has been omitted can be understood from what was mentioned.

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that scriptural proofs are branches of rational proofs, meaning they depend on reason for their validity and interpretation.

Refutation of Relying Solely on Observation
Baqillani's reductio ad absurdum against observation-based epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to refute the claim that only observable things can be known to exist.

Core Idea
Denying the unseen based on observation alone leads to absurdity.

Baqillani presents a powerful logical argument against those who reject anything not directly observed. He shows that applying this principle consistently would force his opponents to deny their own accepted beliefs, like events without beginning or prime matter. Through a vivid example of a person from Zanj, he demonstrates that such a method is self-defeating and based on ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full reductio argument. Note the conditional structure: 'if judgment only by observation, then...'
This is a single, dense sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the opponents' unobserved claims
List the claims that Baqillani's opponents accept but are not observed: events without beginning, eternal body, prime matter with accidents, uncombined natures, pure light and darkness.
These are their own doctrines.
3
Consider the Zanj example
Imagine someone from Zanj who only sees black people, fresh water, and green plants. If only observation counts, they must deny the existence of Romans, sea water, and red/yellow plants. That is absurd.
This illustrates the absurd consequence of the principle.
4
Conclude why observation alone is insufficient
Since the principle leads to absurdity and denial of known truths, it is invalid. The unseen cannot be dismissed simply because it is not observed.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : لو لم يصح القضاء إلا بما شوهد ووُجد لوجب إحالة ما تذهبون إليه من إثبات حوادث لا أوّل لها وإحالة القضاء على قدم الجسم وأنه لا أول لوجوده وإحالة قول من قال منكم بأن الهيولي والطينة قد من الكمية والصورة والكيفية وجميع الأعراض وإحالة قول من قال إن الطبائع الأربع قد كانت غير مركبة في الأجسام وقول من قال إن النور والظلام قد كانا في القدم خالصين غير ممتزجين لأن ذلك أجمع مما لم يُرَ ولم يُشاهَد ولوجب على من نشأ في بلاد الزنج ولم يشاهد فيها إنساناً إلا أسود ولا ماء إلا عذباً ولا زرعاً إلا أخضر أن يقضى على أنه لا إنسان ولا ماء ولا زرع إلا على صفة ما وجد وشاهد حتى ينفي وجود الروم والصقالبة وماء البحار والأحمر والأصفر من النبات فلما لم يجب ذلك أجمع، وكان القضاء بذلك قضاء بالجهل بطل التعلق بمجرد الشاهد والوجود وزال ما يسألون عنه من هذا الجنس .
And it is said to them: If judgment were only valid by what is observed and exists, then it would be necessary to reject what you claim of establishing events without beginning, and to reject the judgment of the eternity of the body and that it has no beginning of its existence, and to reject the statement of those among you who say that matter and clay have [eternity?] of quantity, form, quality, and all accidents, and to reject the statement of those who say that the four natures were uncombined in bodies, and the statement of those who say that light and darkness were in eternity pure and unmixed — because all of that has not been seen or observed. And it would be necessary for someone raised in the land of Zanj who has not seen there any person except black, nor any water except fresh, nor any plant except green, to judge that there is no person, water, or plant except of the quality that he found and observed — until he denies the existence of the Romans, the Slavs, sea water, and the red and yellow of plants. Since all of that is not necessary, and such judgment would be judgment by ignorance, the reliance on mere observation and existence is invalidated, and what they ask about of this kind is removed.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument against those who deny the unseen based on lack of observation. The author lists several claims his opponents themselves accept (e.g., events without beginning, eternal body, prime matter) that are unobserved. He then shows that applying the same observational standard would force absurd conclusions, like a person from Zanj denying the existence of Romans or sea water. Since that is absurd, the principle of relying only on the observed is invalid.

Key Terms
قضاء
شاهد
وجود
مشاهدة
هيولي
طبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only things that have been observed can possibly exist.
Correction: Many true things exist that are not observed, and rejecting them based on lack of observation leads to absurd conclusions, as Baqillani demonstrates.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use the example of a person from Zanj to illustrate?
A) That all people are black.
B) That denying unobserved things leads to absurd conclusions.
C) That water is always fresh.
D) That plants are always green.

Key Takeaway
Reliance on observation alone is invalid because it leads to absurd consequences and rejects known truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You accept many things you have not seen: atoms, gravity, historical events. Why do you accept them? How can you apply that same reasoning to strengthen your belief in the unseen matters of the Hereafter? What is one specific unseen truth you will affirm more firmly starting today?

Refuting Exclusive Reliance on Observation
Baqillani refutes the argument from exclusive observation using the four natures example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why exclusive reliance on empirical observation is insufficient for establishing universal truths in kalam arguments.

Core Idea
Limited observation cannot disprove what it has not seen.

Baqillani presents a powerful counterexample to the claim that we can know the nature of the world solely through observation. If someone raised in a limited environment were to deny the existence of things outside their experience, they would be making a judgment based on ignorance. This passage shows why the method of exclusive observation fails as a foundation for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Baqillani's argument.
2
Understand the example of the person from Zanj
Identify the hypothetical scenario: someone who only sees black people, sweet water, and green plants. What would they wrongly conclude?
This illustrates the limitation of observation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice how Baqillani concludes that relying solely on the witnessed leads to ignorance. Ask yourself: How does this refute the opponent's argument?
The key is that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الطبائع الأربع قد كانت غير مركبة في الأجسام وقول من قال إن النور والظلام قد كانا في القدم خالصين غير ممتزجين لأن ذلك أجمع مما لم ير ولم يشاهد ولوجب على من نشأ في بلاد الزنج ولم يشاهد فيها إنسانا إلا أسود ولا ماء إلا عذبا ولا زرعا إلا أخضر أن يقضى على أنه لا إنسان ولا ماء ولا زرع إلا على صفة ما وجد وشاهد حتى ينفي وجود الروم والصقالبة وماء البحار والأحمر والأصفر من النبات فلما لم يجب ذلك أجمع، وكان القضاء بذلك قضاء بالجهل بطل التعلق بمجرد الشاهد والوجود وزال ما يسألون عنه من هذا الجنس.
Indeed the four natures are not compounded in bodies, and the statement of whoever said that light and darkness were in eternity pure and unmixed—because all of that is not seen and not witnessed—and it would be necessary for one who grew up in the land of Zanj and did not see in it any human except black, any water except sweet, any plant except green, to judge that there is no human, water, or plant except in the condition he found and witnessed, until he denies the existence of the Romans and Slavs, the water of the seas, and the red and yellow of plants. So when none of that was necessary, and judging by that is a judgment of ignorance, the reliance on mere witnessed and existence became invalid, and what they ask about of this kind is removed.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that the world's origin can be known solely from immediate observation. Baqillani gives an example: if a person grew up in Zanj (East Africa) and only ever saw black people, sweet water, and green plants, he might incorrectly judge that no other types exist, e.g., white people (Romans, Slavs), salty seawater, or red/yellow plants. Such a judgment would be based on ignorance, not knowledge. Therefore, one cannot rely exclusively on what is witnessed in a limited context to draw universal conclusions. This dismantles the opponent's reliance on empirical observation as a decisive proof in the discussion about the natures.

Key Terms
الطبائع
الشاهد
الوجود
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against natures relies on empirical observation just like the opponent.
Correction: Baqillani does not rely on observation; he uses a logical reductio to show that exclusive observation leads to absurd conclusions.

Checkpoint

A) That all humans are black
B) That reliance on limited observation leads to false conclusions
C) That natures are eternal
D) That the world is created

Key Takeaway
Exclusive reliance on limited observation leads to ignorance and cannot be used to deny unseen realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What will you do this week to train your heart to affirm what Allah has told you, even when your eyes deny it?
?
When you sin, do you ever justify it by thinking, 'It's not that bad, others do worse'? That is relying on your limited observation to judge right and wrong, instead of Allah's revelation. In what specific area of your life this week will you stop using your own sight as the scale and submit fully to the unseen criterion of the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
You have met people who reject the existence of angels or jinn because they have never seen them. Could it be that in your own heart, you doubt Allah's promises (such as victory for the believers or the reality of Hell) because they do not fully match what you observe right now? Will you continue to trust your limited observation over Allah's unlimited knowledge? If not, what concrete step will you take today to align your certainty with the truth revealed in this lesson?

Refutation of Analogy: Witnessed to Unseen
Baqillani refutes the claim that the unseen must mirror the witnessed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen, and how he uses a reductio ad absurdum to show its absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Blind analogy from the physical world to God leads to absurdity and materialism.

In this lesson, we examine Imam Baqillani's powerful critique of a common method of reasoning about God: drawing analogies from the physical world to the unseen. He challenges the claim that the unseen must mirror the witnessed, and shows that such reasoning would force us to adopt false conclusions that even the opponents would reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the rhetorical style: Baqillani uses questions and conditional statements.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Baqillani's challenge
Look for the phrase 'لم زعمتم' and understand the opponents' position: that the unseen must be analogous to the witnessed. Baqillani asks for their proof.
This is the starting point of the refutation.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Baqillani lists several things the opponents 'did not find' in the witnessed world, then demands they apply the same reasoning to absurd conclusions like an eternal world.
The list includes events, bodies, and agents lacking tools.
4
Understand the Zanj example
Baqillani gives an example of a person from Zanj who only sees sweet water and black people. This illustrates that limited experience cannot determine universal truths.
This shows the method leads to ignorance.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The opponents must either accept the absurdities (joining materialists) or reject their own method. Consider how this refutes the analogical approach.
This is a classic reductio: the method invalidates itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم: لم زعمتم أولاً أنكم إذا لم تجدوا الأشياء في الشاهد إلا على ما وصفتم وجب القضاء على الغائب بمجرد الشاهد وأن الموجود في الغائب لا ينفك من أصناف الموجودات في الشاهد، وما حجتكم على ذلك؟ فإن الخلاف في جهة استدلالكم أعظم؛ والغلط والخطأ فيه أفحش. ثم يقال لهم: فأنتم أيضاً لم تجدوا حادثاً إلا وقبله حادث، ولا شيئاً إلا عن شيء، ولا جسماً إلا وبعده جسم، وفوقه جسم، ومن عن يمينه وشِمَالِه وتجَاهِه وخَلْفِه جِسْم؛ ولا وجدتم فاعلاً اخترع الأجسام وأحدث الأفعال بغير أدواتٍ وآلاتٍ وجوارح وعلاج؛ فاقضوا بذلك على قدم العالم ونفي النهاية عنه، وأن الحوادث لا أوّل لها، وأن الأجسام لا كلّ لها ولا غاية وأنه لا إنسان إلا من نطفة إلا من إنسان، ولا طائِرَ إلا من بيضة ولا بيضَةَ إلّا من طائر أبداً إلى غير نهاية؛ وهذا لحوق بأهل الدهر. وكذلك فاقضوا على أنه لا فاعل لأجسام العالم وأن الفاعل لأغراضه يفعلها بآلات وأدوات وأوجِبُوا على من نشأ في بلد الزنج فلم يجد بها ماء إلا عذباً ولا إنساناً إلا أسود ولا زرعاً إلا أخضر أَن يَقْضِيَ أنه لا ماء إلا عذب ولا إنسان إلا كما وجد وشاهد حتى يوجبوا القضاء بالجهل الذي يُعلمُ بُطْلانُه اضطراراً؛ فإن مروا على ذلك أجْمَع لَحقُوا بأهل الدهر والجهالات، وإن امتنعوا منه نقضوا استدلالهم.
So it is said to them: Why did you claim first that when you do not find things in the witnessed except as you described, it is necessary to judge the unseen by mere witness, and that what exists in the unseen does not separate from the kinds of existing things in the witnessed? And what is your proof for that? For the disagreement concerning the direction of your argument is greater, and the error and mistake in it are more heinous. Then it is said to them: And you also did not find an event except that before it is an event, nor a thing except from a thing, nor a body except that after it is a body, and above it is a body, and on its right and left and in front and behind it is a body; nor did you find an agent who invented bodies and originated actions without tools, instruments, organs, and treatment. So judge by that that the world is eternal and has no end, and that events have no beginning, and that bodies have no totality nor limit, and that there is no human except from sperm except from a human, and no bird except from an egg and no egg except from a bird forever without end; and this is joining the materialists. And likewise, judge that there is no agent for the bodies of the world and that the agent for his purposes does them with tools and instruments; and oblige the one who grew up in the land of Zanj and did not find there water but sweet, nor human but black, nor crop but green, to judge that there is no water but sweet, nor human but as he found and witnessed, so that they would bring judgment with ignorance whose falsehood is known necessarily. For if they pass on all that, they join the materialists and ignorances, and if they abstain from it, they invalidate their own argument.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the method of qiyas (analogical reasoning) from the witnessed world (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-ghaib). The author first challenges the opponents' claim that because they only find things in the witnessed world in a certain way, they must judge the unseen accordingly, and that the unseen cannot contain different kinds. He asks for their proof. Then he turns the argument back on them: if they apply the same analogy, they would have to conclude that every event is preceded by another event (infinite regress), every body is surrounded by bodies, every agent uses tools and organs. This would lead to the claim that the world is eternal (qadim) without beginning or end, and that there is no creator who acts without tools. He also gives an example: someone raised in the land of Zanj (East Africa) might only see sweet water, black people, and green crops, but that does not mean those are the only possibilities. Thus the opponents' method leads to necessary ignorance and joins the materialists (dahriyya). If they accept these conclusions, they are materialists; if they reject them, their method is refuted.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
قياس
استدلال
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unseen world must be exactly like the physical world in all aspects.
Correction: God and the unseen are not bound by the limitations of creation; analogy is valid only with proper conditions and within a framework that respects divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if we apply the same analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to the unseen?
A) We would conclude the world is eternal and has no creator.
B) We would confirm the existence of God.
C) We would prove the resurrection.
D) We would establish prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The unseen (al-ghaib) is not bound by the patterns of the witnessed world (al-shahid); to assume otherwise leads to absurdity and materialism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area of your faith where you have unconsciously assumed that God's ways are just like human ways. How does Baqillani's argument expose that assumption? What will you do today to correct it?
?
Have you ever judged God's wisdom based on your limited experience of the world? Reflect on a specific instance. How does this lesson call you to repent of such arrogance?
?
If the unseen is radically different from the witnessed, how should that change your approach to learning about God? Commit to one concrete way you will seek knowledge with humility rather than presumptive analogy.

Method of Learning
beginner

Challenging the Necessity of Analogy from Witnessed to Unseen
Baqillani questions the necessity of analogical reasoning from witnessed to unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Baqillani questions the opponents' claim that the unseen must necessarily mirror the witnessed world, and why this methodological challenge is foundational to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The unseen is not bound by the categories of the witnessed world.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani directly challenges a key methodological assumption of his opponents: that the unseen world must conform to the patterns of the witnessed world. He asks them to justify this claim and points out that even they disagree on how to apply such an analogy. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion about how we can know about Allah and the unseen without imposing our limited experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that it is a direct, pointed question to the opponents.
Pay attention to the tone of the objection.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Find where Baqillani states what the opponents believe: that the unseen must be judged solely by the witnessed, and that all existents in the unseen fall under the same categories as in the witnessed.
This is the assumption he is challenging.
3
Understand Baqillani's objection
Baqillani asks for proof (ḥujja) and notes that even the opponents disagree among themselves about how to draw this analogy, making their method flawed and error-prone.
The phrase al-khilāf fī jihati istidlālikum aʿẓam highlights internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم : لم زعمتم أولاً أنكم إذا لم تجدوا الأشياء في الشاهد إلا جسم وتحته على ما وصفتم وجب القضاء على الغائب بمجرد الشاهد وأن الموجود في الغائب لا ينفك من أصناف الموجودات في الشاهد، وما حجتكم على ذلك؟ فإن الخلاف في جهة استدلالكم أعظم؛ والغلط والخطأ فيه أفحش .
It is said to them: Why did you claim firstly that when you do not find things in the witnessed world except as body and under it according to what you described, it is necessary to judge the unseen solely by the witnessed, and that the existent in the unseen is not free from the categories of existents in the witnessed? And what is your proof for that? For indeed the disagreement in the aspect of your deduction is greater, and the error and mistake in it are more ugly.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a direct objection to the opponents' method of qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen). The author first asks them to justify their claim that the unseen must necessarily conform to the categories of the witnessed. He then points out that even the opponents themselves disagree about how this analogy should be applied, making their own reasoning internally flawed. The phrase 'الغلط والخطأ فيه أفحش' emphasizes that the error in their deductive method is particularly severe and obvious.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
قضاء
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy from witnessed to unseen is a universally accepted method among theologians.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the method is disputed and its application is flawed, so it is not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
What does the author ask the opponents to justify in this passage?
A) The existence of bodies in the unseen
B) The necessity of judging the unseen based on the witnessed world
C) The origin of the world
D) The attributes of God

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's challenge reveals that the method of qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid is not self-evident and requires justification, a foundational point for Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your understanding of Allah have you unconsciously assumed that the unseen must mirror the witnessed? What specific step can you take today to break that assumption?
?
When you read the phrase 'the error and mistake in it are more ugly,' do you feel that your own reasoning about Allah might be flawed? What will you do to seek the truth instead of relying on your own analogies?
?
If someone asked you why you believe in Allah's attributes as you do, would your answer rely on analogies from this world? How can this lesson reshape your approach to affirming what you cannot see?

The Dilemma of Strict Analogy: Absurd Consequences
Baqillani lists absurd consequences of the opponents' analogy method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani exposes the absurdities that follow from applying strict analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.

Core Idea
If the unseen must exactly mirror the witnessed, reason leads to absurdity.

Baqillani now turns the opponents' method against them. If they insist that the unseen realm must be exactly like the witnessed world, then they are forced to accept conclusions that are clearly false or even atheistic. This passage presents a series of such absurd consequences, showing that the method of strict analogy is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of absurd consequences Baqillani derives.
The passage uses a repetitive structure: 'you did not find... so judge that...'
2
Identify the absurd consequences
Find each absurd consequence Baqillani lists. There are at least four distinct ones, plus a specific example. Write them down.
The consequences include eternal events, infinite bodies, agents needing tools, and infinite reproduction.
3
Understand the dilemma
Baqillani ends with a dilemma: accept all these absurdities (which leads to materialism) or reject them (which invalidates the analogy method). Reflect on why this forces the opponent to abandon strict analogy.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : فأنتم أيضاً لم تجدوا حادثاً إلا وقبله حادث، ولا شيئاً إلا عن شيء، ولا جسماً إلا وبعده جسم، وفوقه جسم، ومن عن يمينه وشِمَالِه وتجَاهِه وخَلْفِه جِسْم؛ ولا وجدتم فاعلاً اخترع الأجسام وأحدث الأفعال بغير أدواتٍ وآلاتٍ وجوارح وعلاج؛ فاقضوا بذلك على قدم العالم ونفي النهاية عنه، وأن الحوادث لا أوّل لها، وأن الأجسام لا كلّ لها ولا غاية وألا إنسان إلا من نطفة إلا من إنسان، ولا طائِرَ إلا من بيضة ولا بيضَةَ إلّا من طائر أبداً إلى غير نهاية؛ وهذا لحوق بأهل الدهر. وكذلك فاقضوا على أنه لا فاعل لأجسام العالم وأن الفاعل لأغراضه يفعلها بآلات وأدوات وأوْجِبُوا على من نشأ في بلد الزنج فلم يجد بها ماء إلا عذباً ولا إنساناً إلا أسود ولا زرعاً إلا أخضر أَن يَقْضِيَ أنه لا ماء إلا عذب ولا إنسان إلا كما وجد وشاهد حتى القضاء بالجهل الذي يُعلمُ بُطْلانُه اضطراراً؛ فإن مروا على ذلك أجْمَع لَحقُوا بأهل الدهر والجهالات، وإن امتنعوا منه نقضوا استدلالهم.
Then it is said to them: You too did not find any event except that before it was an event, nor anything except from something, nor any body except that after it is a body, and above it is a body, and from its right and left and front and behind is a body; and you did not find any agent who invented bodies and produced actions without tools, instruments, limbs, and treatment; so judge by that that the world is eternal and has no end, that events have no beginning, that bodies have no limit or end, and that no human exists except from a drop of sperm except from a human, and no bird except from an egg and no egg except from a bird forever without end; and this is joining the materialists. Likewise, judge that there is no agent for the bodies of the world and that the agent for his purposes does them with tools and instruments, and require that one who grew up in the land of the Zanj and found there no water except fresh, no human except black, and no crop except green, to judge that there is no water except fresh and no human except as he found and witnessed, even to the point of judging by ignorance that is known to be false necessarily; if they accept all that, they join the materialists and the ignorant, and if they refuse it, they invalidate their analogy.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a series of absurd consequences that follow from the opponents' method of strict analogy from the witnessed to the unseen. The author argues that if the opponents insist that the unseen must exactly mirror the witnessed, then they would have to accept: (1) that events have no beginning (since every event is preceded by another), (2) that bodies are infinite in extent (since every body is surrounded by other bodies), (3) that agents always use tools and limbs (since no agent is witnessed without them), (4) that human reproduction is an infinite chain (since every human comes from a human), and so on. These conclusions lead to atheism (materialism) and obvious falsehoods. The example of a person from Zanj who only sees fresh water and black people would absurdly conclude that no other water or people exist. The author concludes that if the opponents accept these conclusions, they become materialists; if they reject them, they undermine their own method.

جسم
فاعل
قدم العالم
دهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unseen realm can be known by strict analogy from the witnessed world, without any qualification.
Correction: Baqillani shows this leads to absurdities, proving that analogy must be qualified or rejected when dealing with divine matters.

Checkpoint
What is one of the absurd consequences the author derives from the opponents' method?
A) That God has a body
B) That events have no beginning
C) That the world was created in time
D) That humans are not from sperm

Key Takeaway
Strict analogy from the witnessed to the unseen leads to false and even atheistic conclusions, undermining the method itself.

?

This passage shows how limited experience can blind us. What experience in your life has subtly shaped your view of Allah's attributes in a way that may not align with revelation? How will you correct it starting today?
?

Refuting the Analogy: The Creator Must Be Temporal
Baqillani shows that the opponents' own analogy would make the Creator temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani turns the opponents' own method of analogy against them to show that it would lead to the absurd conclusion that the Creator is originated.

Core Idea
Analogy from the witnessed world cannot apply to the Creator without contradiction.

Baqillani here employs a powerful reductio argument. He takes a premise that his opponents accept—that every known existent in the witnessed world is originated—and then applies their own method of qiyas (analogy) to the Creator. The result is a dilemma: either they must accept that the Creator is originated, contradicting their belief, or they must reject their own method of proof. This exposes the flaw in using analogical reasoning for divine matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the structure
Read the Arabic then translation. Notice it is a dialogue: the author poses a question, then presents a conditional conclusion.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the shared premise
Find the premise that both sides agree upon: every known existent in the witnessed world is originated (created).
This is key to the argument.
3
Follow the application of analogy
See how the author applies the analogy to the Creator. If the premise is true, then by analogy the Creator must also be originated.
This is the logical consequence the opponents must face.
4
Understand the dilemma
Recognize the forced choice: either accept the conclusion (which contradicts their belief) or reject the method of analogy itself.
This shows the method is self-defeating.
5
Reflect on the lesson for kalam methodology
Think about why analogy from the created to the Creator is invalid. This highlights the need for a different form of reasoning.
This connects to the bigger picture of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : أفليس قد اتفقنا على أنه لا موجود معلوم في الشاهد والمعقول إلا مُحْدَثٌ موجود عن عدم؟ فإذا قالوا : أجل، قيل لهم : فيجب أن يكون صانع العالم جل ذكره، موجوداً محدثاً قياساً على الشاهد؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا مذهبهم، وإن أبوا نقضوا دليلهم.
Then it is said to them: Is it not agreed that there is no known existent in the witnessed and the rational except that it is originated, existing after non-existence? When they say: Yes, it is said to them: Then it must be that the Maker of the world, exalted be His mention, is an originated existent by analogy from the witnessed; if they accept that, they abandon their doctrine, and if they refuse, they invalidate their proof.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
In this passage, the author uses a common premise that the opponents accept: every known existent in the witnessed world is originated (created). The opponents agree to this. The author then applies their own method of analogy to conclude that the Creator of the world must also be originated, since He is an existent known by inference. This leads to a dilemma: if they accept the conclusion, they contradict their belief in an eternal Creator; if they reject it, they reject their own method of analogy. Thus the method is self-defeating.

صانع العالم
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy from the witnessed world can prove attributes of God without contradiction.
Correction: Baqillani shows that applying such analogy leads to the absurd conclusion that God is created, thus the method is self-defeating.

Checkpoint

A) That the Creator is eternal
B) That the Creator is originated
C) That the world is eternal
D) That the Creator has no attributes

Key Takeaway
Analogy from the created to the Creator is invalid because it would force a contradiction, proving that God is created.

The Flaw of Analogy from the Witnessed Realm
Baqillani critiques analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects unrestricted analogy from the witnessed realm for theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Analogy from the physical world leads to absurd conclusions about God.

Imam Baqillani challenges opponents who rely on analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) realm to judge God's actions. He shows that consistent application of their method leads to impossible attributes for God, making their position self-defeating. This lesson examines his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument. Notice the conditional structure: 'if you base... then you must judge...'
The passage is a single continuous argument, not a list.
2
Identify the consequences of analogy
Baqillani points out that if analogy is applied strictly, it forces God to have physical limitations: composition, location, need for motives.
These are attributes the opponents would deny, but their method logically implies them.
3
Understand the dilemma
The opponents have two choices: accept these consequences (which nullify the proof of creation) or reject them (which contradicts their own method).
Either way, their position fails.
4
Reflect on the self-defeating nature
Consider why analogy from the witnessed realm cannot be applied universally to God. This shows the need for a distinct theological methodology.
This passage is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: إن بنيتم الأمر على قبح ذلك في الشاهد بزعمكم، فيجب أن تقضوا على أن الفاعل للعالم لا يفعله إلا لاجتلاب منفعة أو دفع مضرة وداع دعاه إلى الفعل وبعثه عليه؛ وأنه تعالى مؤلف ذو حيز وقبول للأعراض وفي مكان دون مكان لأنكم لم تعقلوا فاعلا في الشاهد إلا كذلك فإن مروا على هذا أبطلوا الحدوث والمحدث وسيقت عليهم مطالبات الدهرية وإن أبوه، نقضوا استدلالهم بمجرد الشاهد والوجود.
And it is said to them: If you base the matter on the ugliness of that in the witnessed realm, by your claim, then you must judge that the doer of the world does not do it except to obtain benefit or repel harm, and a motive that calls him to the act and impels him to it; and that He is composed, possessing space, susceptible to accidents, and in a place without a place, because you have not known an agent in the witnessed realm except like that. If they pass over this, they nullify origination and the originator, and the arguments of the materialists would be brought against them. And if they reject it, they refute their own inference by mere analogy to the witnessed realm and existence.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a methodological critique of the opponents' reliance on analogy from the witnessed realm (shahid). The author argues that if they insist on judging God by the standards of physical agents, they would be forced to attribute to God characteristics like composition, location, and need for motives—which are impossible for God. If they accept this, they undermine the proof of creation (huduth) and fall into materialism (dahriyya). If they reject this, they contradict their own method of reasoning from the witnessed realm. Thus, their position is self-defeating.

Key Terms

قبح
فاعل
منفعة
مضرة
حدوث
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy from the witnessed realm (shahid) is always valid for understanding God's actions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that strict analogy leads to impossible attributes for God, demonstrating its limitation in theology.

Checkpoint

A) They would prove God's unity
B) They would be forced to attribute physical limitations to God
C) They would disprove prophets
D) They would confirm prophethood

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that relying solely on analogy from the witnessed realm leads to contradictions and cannot be used to argue about God.

?
The passage shows that applying physical-world reasoning to God can lead to supporting materialist arguments. In what areas of your faith have you unknowingly adopted assumptions that, if fully applied, would undermine your belief? Write down one area and a practical action to correct it.

Reframing the Prophethood Debate: The Source of Prohibition
Baqillani redirects the debate to the rational source of prohibition and God's absolute ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that discussing specific prophets is meaningless with those who reject prophethood entirely, and that prohibition and permission come only from God's command, not from reason alone.

Core Idea
Prohibition comes from God's statement, not from reason — reason reveals absolute divine ownership.

Baqillani begins by shifting the debate from specific prophets to the fundamental possibility of prophethood. He then challenges the claim that reason can independently determine what is prohibited or permitted. Finally, he argues that reason actually affirms God's absolute freedom as the Owner of all entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ما أنكرتم' which signals a challenge to the opponent.
2
Identify the two main moves
The passage has two distinct sections: first, a methodological reframing about discussing prophethood with those who deny it entirely; second, an argument about the source of prohibition and permission.
Baqillani uses 'ثم يقال لهم' to transition.
3
Analyze the argument about prohibition
Baqillani asserts that prohibition and permission are only from God's statement, not reason. He asks why opponents claim reason has that authority.
4
Examine the rational proof for God's ownership
Baqillani uses reason to show that God, as absolute Owner, may do as He wills without constraint. This is a foundational point for later arguments about suffering.
The phrase 'لأنه لا مالك فوقه ولا زاجر يجد له' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحاً فيه فليس الكلام معكم في نبوة قوم بأعيانهم فإن الكلام في ذلك بين أهل الملل والمجوزين لإرسال الله تعالى الرسل، وأنتم تحيلون أن يرسل الله رسولاً أصلاً فلا معنى للكلام في تعيين رسالة فلان دون فلان فإنه خروج عن الكلام وعجز وانتقال من باب إلى باب. ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون جميع ما ادعيتم حظره في العقل غير محظور فيه ولا مباح أيضاً وأن الحظر والإباحة إنما هما ورود القول المبين مالك الأعيان بإباحة ما أباحه وحظر ما حظره، فلم قلتم إن في العقل إباحة وحظراً؟ ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون العقل قاضياً على أن لخالق الأعيان ومالك الذوات أن يتلفها ويؤلمها وأن يبيح ذلك فيها وأن يبتدئها باللذات بدلاً من الآلام وبالآلام بدلاً من اللذات لأنه لا مالك فوقه ولا زاجر يجد له؟ فلا يجدون إلى دفع ذلك سبيلا.
It is permissible in it, so it is not speech with you about the prophethood of specific individuals, for speech about that is among the people of religions and those who allow the sending of messengers by God Most High, while you deem it impossible that God should send a messenger at all, so there is no meaning to speech about specifying the message of so-and-so without so-and-so, for that is a departure from the discussion, an incapacity, and a transition from one topic to another. Then it is said to them: What prevents you from admitting that all that you have claimed is prohibited in reason is neither prohibited in it nor permissible either, and that prohibition and permission are only the occurrence of the clear statement of the possessor of entities permitting what He permitted and prohibiting what He prohibited? So why do you say that in reason there is permission and prohibition? Then it is said to them: What prevents you from admitting that reason judges that the Creator of entities and the Owner of essences may destroy them and inflict pain on them, and may permit that in them, and may initiate them with pleasures instead of pains, and with pains instead of pleasures, because there is no owner above Him nor any restrainer who finds for Him? So they have no way to repel that.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a reframing of the debate about prophethood and an argument about the rational source of prohibition. Baqillani first addresses opponents who deny prophethood entirely. He argues that discussing specific prophets (like Muhammad or Moses) is pointless with those who reject the very possibility of a messenger from God. Such a discussion would be a departure from the proper topic and a sign of weakness. Then, he poses a challenge: why do they claim that certain acts are prohibited or permitted by reason alone? He asserts that prohibition and permission come only from the explicit statement of the Owner (God), not from reason. Finally, he argues that reason actually supports the opposite: it judges that the Creator, as the absolute Owner, has the right to destroy, cause pain, initiate pleasure or pain without any constraint, because there is no higher authority to restrict Him. This is a foundational point for the later argument about animal suffering.

Key Terms

إباحة
عقل
ملك الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani rejects any role for reason in theology.
Correction: He actually uses reason to affirm God's absolute ownership and the limits of human rational judgment about prohibition, but he does not dismiss reason entirely.

Key Takeaway
Reason cannot independently determine what is prohibited or permitted; that authority belongs solely to God, the absolute Owner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
