Etymology of 'Ism': Proof from Diminutive Forms
Baqillani demonstrates through diminutive forms that 'ism' derives from 'sama'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diminutive form 'sumayy' proves that 'ism' is derived from the root 's-m-w' (sama) and not from 'w-s-m' (wasama).

Core Idea
The diminutive 'sumayy' exposes the deleted letter and reveals the true root.

How do we know whether the Arabic word 'ism' (name) comes from 'sama' (to be high) or 'wasama' (to mark)? Linguists use morphological clues, especially diminutive forms, to uncover a word's origin. Al-Baqillani, a master of Arabic and Islamic theology, demonstrates this method with precision. This passage walks through his evidence step by step, turning a simple diminutive into a decisive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Start with the first few lines where Baqillani states the claim and the key evidence: the Arabs said 'sumayy' in the diminutive, not 'wusaym'.
Focus on the contrast between 'sumayy' and hypothetical 'wusaym'.
2
Understand why a letter must be deleted
The word 'ism' appears to have only two radicals (sīn and mīm) because the alif is conjunctive. Since the minimum root is three, a letter must have been deleted. The two possibilities are the first radical (fā') or the last radical (lām).
Remember: the alif wasl is not a root letter.
3
Follow the diminutive evidence
Examine the examples: 'ibn' becomes 'bunayy' (restoring the lām), and 'iddah' becomes 'wu'aydah' (restoring the fā'). Applying the same pattern, 'ism' becomes 'sumayy', which restores a lām. Therefore the deleted letter is the lām, not the fā'.
This is the crux of the argument.
4
Check the phonological rule
Baqillani explains the sound change: when a yā' and wāw meet with the first quiescent, they assimilate into a shaddated yā'. This confirms 'sumayy' is derived from 'sumayw' (from root s-m-w).
This solidifies the conclusion.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that 'ism' is from 's-m-w', refuting those who claim it is from 'w-s-m'. The method is a model of linguistic reasoning.
Consider how this applies to other words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان «سما يسمو» وليس من «وسم يسم» - أن العرب صغرته فقالت «سُمي». ولو «السمة» لقالوا «وُسَيْمٌ»، كما قالوا في تصغير «عِدَة وزِنة» «وعيدة من ووُزَيْنة». فدل ذلك على أن المحذوف منه لام الفعل دون الفاء. وأهل العربية يعنون بلام الفعل آخر حرف من حروفه ويريدون بفاء الفعل أول حرف من حروفه. وقد أطبقوا على أن «اسم» قد حُذف منه ، لأنه على حرفين ـ السين والميم - وأصله «سمو على ما قلناه والألف التي قبل السين في قولك «اسم» ألف وصل لا يُعتد بها ، وإنما أدخلت في الكلام ليتوصل بها إلى النطق بالساكن. وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، ثبت أن «اسم» على حرفين فقط السين والميم وعلمنا بذلك أنه قد حذف منه حرف، لأن أقل الأصول في الأسماء ما كان على ثلاثة أحرف. فإذا وجدنا اسماً على أقل من ذلك، علمنا أنه قد حذف منه حرف، مثل «يد» و «دم» و «أب» و «ابن» وما جرى مجرى ذلك مما هو على حرفين . وإذا ثبت ذلك، فلا يخلو أن يكون المحذوف منه الفاء أو اللام . ولا يجوز أن يكون المحذوف منه الفاء، لأن العرب قالت في تصغيره «سُمي»، كما صغرت «ابنا» فقالت فيه «بُنَيّ»، فردّت فيه اللام. ولو كان المحذوف منه الفاء، لقالوا «وسيم»، كما قالوا في تصغير «عِدَة وزِنّة وجِهَة» وُعَيْدَة) ووُزَيْنَة ووُجَيْهَة ، لأنه من الوعد والوزن والوجه». وإنما حُذفت الواو من المصدر لأنها حذفت من الفعل. لأنهم قالوا يَعِدُ» مكان «يَوْعِدُ». ومن شأنهم، إذا غيّروا الفعل ضرباً من التغيير أن يحملوا المصدر عليه فيفعلوا فيه كما يفعلون في الفعل. وإنما حذفت الواو من الفعل المضارع إذا كان في أوله الياء لوقوعها بين ياء وكسرة. وذلك مثل قولك وَعَدَ يَعِدُ ووَزَنَ يَزِنُ»، والأصل يوعِد ويَوْزن». وحُمِل باقي حروف المضارعة ـ وهي والنون والألف ـ على الياء لئلا يختلف الفعل، فقالوا «نَعِد وتعد وأعد»، والأصل «نَوْعِد وتوعد وأوعد». فإذا صغرت عدة» قلتَ فيه «وعيدة»، ولم يُعتد بهاء التأنيث . فإذا لم يعتد بها بقي الاسم على حرفين وهما العين والدال. وقد علمنا أنه لا يجوز تصغير الاسم الذي يكون على حرفين، وإنما يصح ذلك فيه إذا كان على ثلاثة أحرف فصاعداً. فاحتجنا أن نَرُدّ المحذوف ـ وهو الواو ـ فقلنا في تصغيره وعيدة». وكذلك القصَّةَ في تصغير «زنة وجهة». فإذا صغرت اسماً»، قلت فيه «سُمَى» ؛ وذلك لأنك رددت المحذوف - وهو الواو - ثم أدخلت باء التصغير إليه ساكنةً. فاجتمع يـاء وواو، والأول منهما ساكن، فقلبت الواو ياء وأدغمت الياء فيها ، فصار «سُميّ) - كقولك حُجَيْر وجُبَيْل وبُني . وهذا أصل مطرد في كلامهم : إذا اجتمعت واو وياء، والأول منها ساكن، أدغم أحدهما في صاحبه فصار ياءً مشددة. مثال ذلك قولهم سيد وميت» . وأصله «سَيود وميوت»، لأنه «فيعـل» من «ساد يسود ومات يموت». فلما اجتمعت الياء والواو ، والأول منهما ساكن ، أُدغم أحدهما في صاحبه فصار ياءً مشدّدةً . فلذلك قالوا في تصغير «اسم» «سُميّ»، وفي تصغير «ابن» «بُنَيّ». وهذا واضح في إبطال قول من زعم أنه من «وسم يسم، وأن المحذوف منه فاء الفعل، ودلّ على أنه «سما يسمو»
It was 'sama yasmu' and not from 'wasama yasimu' - that the Arabs gave its diminutive and said 'sumayy'. And if it were from 'as-simah' (the mark) they would have said 'wusaym', just as they said in the diminutive of 'iddah' and 'zinah': 'wu'aydah' and 'wuzaynah'. So that indicates that the deleted letter from it is the lām of the verb (final radical) and not the fā' (first radical). And the grammarians mean by 'lām of the verb' the last letter of its letters, and by 'fā' of the verb' the first letter of its letters. And they have agreed that 'ism' has had a letter deleted from it, because it is on two letters - the sīn and mīm - and its origin is 'samw' according to what we said, and the alif before the sīn in your saying 'ism' is a conjunctive alif (alif wasl) which is not counted; it was only introduced into speech to enable pronunciation of a quiescent letter. And if that is so, it is established that 'ism' is only on two letters, the sīn and mīm, and we know thereby that a letter has been deleted from it, because the least of roots among nouns is that which is on three letters. So if we find a noun on less than that, we know that a letter has been deleted from it, such as 'yad', 'dam', 'ab', 'ibn', and what runs in that course among what is on two letters. And if that is established, it is inevitable that the deleted letter from it is either the fā' or the lām. And it is not permissible that the deleted from it is the fā', because the Arabs said in its diminutive 'sumayy', just as they gave the diminutive of 'ibn' and said in it 'bunayy', restoring the lām. And if the deleted from it were the fā', they would have said 'wasīm', just as they said in the diminutive of 'iddah', 'zinnah', and 'jihah': 'wu'aydah', 'wuzaynah', and 'wujayhah', because it is from al-wa'd, al-wazn, and al-wajh. And the wāw was deleted from the verbal noun because it was deleted from the verb; because they said 'ya'idu' instead of 'yaw'idu'. And their custom is, when they change the verb in a manner of change, to carry the verbal noun upon it and do in it as they do in the verb. And the wāw was deleted from the imperfect verb when its first letter was the yā' due to its occurrence between a yā' and a kasrah. And that is like your saying 'wa'ada ya'idu' and 'wazana yazinu', the origin being 'yaw'idu' and 'yawzanu'. And the rest of the imperfect prefixes - which are the tā', nūn, and alif - were carried upon the yā' so that the verb would not differ, so they said 'na'idu', 'ta'idu', and 'a'idu', the origin being 'naw'idu', 'taw'idu', and 'aw'idu'. So when you give the diminutive of 'iddah', you say in it 'wu'aydah', and the feminine ending is not counted. And if it is not counted, the noun remains on two letters, which are the 'ayn and dāl. And we know that it is not permissible to give the diminutive of a noun that is on two letters, and that is only valid in it if it is on three letters or more. So we needed to restore the deleted letter - which is the wāw - and we said in its diminutive 'wu'aydah'. And such is the story in the diminutive of 'zinnah' and 'jihah'. So when you give the diminutive of 'ism', you say in it 'sumayy'; and that is because you restored the deleted letter - which is the wāw - then you introduced the yā' of diminution to it as a quiescent letter. So a yā' and a wāw met, with the first of them quiescent, so the wāw was turned into a yā' and the yā' was assimilated into it, so it became 'sumayy' - like your saying 'hujayr', 'jubayl', and 'buniyy'. And this is an established rule in their speech: when a wāw and a yā' meet, with the first of them quiescent, one of them is assimilated into the other and it becomes a shaddated yā'. An example of that is their saying 'sayyid' and 'mayyit'. Their origin is 'saywud' and 'maywut', because it is 'fay'al' from 'sāda yasūdu' and 'māta yamūtu'. And when the yā' and wāw met, with the first of them quiescent, one of them was assimilated into the other and it became a shaddated yā'. Therefore they said in the diminutive of 'ism' 'sumayy', and in the diminutive of 'ibn' 'bunayy'. And this is clear in refuting the claim of whoever asserted that it is from 'wasama yasimu', and that the deleted from it is the fā' of the verb, and it indicates that it is 'sama yasmu'.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic argument from morphology to determine the root of the Arabic word 'ism' (name). The author, Imam al-Baqillani, uses the evidence of the diminutive form to prove that 'ism' is derived from the root 's-m-w' (to be high) rather than 'w-s-m' (to mark). He begins by stating the two possible origins, then notes that the Arabs said the diminutive 'sumayy'. If the root were 'w-s-m', the diminutive would have been 'wasīm' (like 'wu'aydah' from 'iddah'). That the diminutive is 'sumayy' shows the deleted letter is the final radical (lām al-fi'l), not the first (fā' al-fi'l). He explains that grammarians mean by lām the last letter, and by fā' the first. The word 'ism' has only two letters (sīn and mīm) because the alif is a conjunctive alif, not part of the root. Since the minimum root is three letters, one letter is deleted. The only possibilities are fā' or lām. He eliminates fā' because the diminutive 'sumayy' restored lām, like 'bunayy' from 'ibn'. If the deleted were fā', they would have said 'wasīm'. He then discusses the deletion of wāw in verbs like 'wa'ada' and the rule of assimilation of wāw and yā'. Finally, he concludes that 'sumayy' clearly refutes those who claim the root is 'w-s-m' and proves it is 's-m-w'.

Key Terms
اسم
سما
وسم
تصغير
فاء الفعل
لام الفعل
إدغام
ألف وصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ism' is derived from 'wasama' (to mark) because both involve identification.
Correction: The diminutive form 'sumayy' shows the deleted letter is the lām (final radical), not the fā' (first radical), therefore the root is s-m-w, not w-s-m.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Baqillani use to prove that the deleted letter in 'ism' is the lām (final radical) and not the fā' (first radical)?
A) The word 'ism' is used in poetry with a long alif.
B) The Arabs said the diminutive 'sumayy', restoring the lām, like 'bunayy'.
C) The plural 'asmā' shows the root.
D) The word appears in the Qur'an with a fatha.

Key Takeaway
The diminutive 'sumayy' proves that 'ism' derives from the root 's-m-w', not 'w-s-m', by revealing which letter was deleted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how a single word's defense leads to a powerful conclusion. Now ask yourself: When you utter the word 'Allah', are you merely using a label, or do you recognize that its very root (al-Ilah) points to the devotion He alone deserves? Let this lesson call you to a more conscious invocation.
?
The proof from the diminutive shows that Arabic is not arbitrary; it follows rules that reveal truth. How does this precision deepen your trust in the Qur'an as revelation using this language? Commit to reciting more reflectively, knowing each word carries a burden of meaning.
?
Al-Baqillani refuted a false etymology using careful reasoning. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah or His names that you have not examined as rigorously? Will you let this lesson push you to investigate your own assumptions and deepen your certainty?

The Mudari' Verb's Resemblance to Nouns and Refutation of Grammarians
Baqillani argues for the verb's similarity to nouns and refutes grammarians about 'an al-khafīfa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Bāqillānī uses grammatical evidence to demonstrate that the present-future verb shares key properties with nouns, refuting the claim that the lightened 'an' severs this resemblance.

Core Idea
The mudari' verb is like a noun in key respects, and 'an al-khafīfa' does not break that similarity.

Imam al-Bāqillānī presents a linguistic argument to defend the verbal category's connection to nouns. He shows that the present-future verb can take the lām of inception like a noun, and that verbal agents like 'lan' still govern it even when it occupies a nominal position. He then invokes the authority of al-Khalīl to counter grammarians who claim that the lightened 'an' removes this resemblance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the translation. Let Baqillani's argument unfold.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two main evidences
Notice the two proofs: (1) lām al-ibtidā' enters the verb as it enters nouns, and (2) verbal agents like 'lan' govern the verb despite its nominal position.
These are presented in the first half of the passage.
3
Examine al-Khalīl's testimony
Baqillani cites al-Khalīl to show that the verb's resemblance to nouns remains even after 'an al-khafīfa', and only 'sīn' and 'sawfa' affect tense.
4
Understand the refutation
The grammarians' claim that 'an' annuls resemblance lacks transmitted proof; Baqillani concludes their statement is invalid.
The burden of proof is on the grammarians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنّ لام الابتداء يدخل عليه كما يدخل على الاسم، كقولك «إن زيداً ليقوم فهو بمنزلة قولك: «إن زيداً لقائم». ومنه قوله تعالى: إن ربك ليحكم بينهم أي: إنه لحاكم بينهم. فقد وقع الفعل المضارع موقع الاسم في هذه المواضع، ولم يمنع ذلك من أن يدخل عليه عوامل الأفعال. ألا ترى أنك تقول: زيد لن يقوم، فنصبته بلن؟ ولم يمنع وقوعه موقع الاسم من أن يدخل عليه عامل الفعل فينصبه على الأصل الذي يجب في حكم إعراب الأفعال. وهذا مبطل لاعتمادهم إبطالاً ظاهراً. ومما يدل على بطلان ذلك وفساده أن الخليل بن أحمد وغيره من جلة أهل العربية قالوا: إن الفعل الذي في أوله الزوائد الأربعة مضارع للاسم من الوجوه التي ذكرناها وقالوا إنه مضارع، وإن وقع بعد أن الخفيفة. وقال الخليل: إن الفعل المضارع يصلح أن يكون للحال ويصلح أن يكون للاستقبال. فبهذا الوجه أيضاً ضارع قولك «رجل»، الذي يصلح أن تريد به زيداً ويصلح أن تريد به عمراً. ولم يقل إن دخول أن الخفيفة عليه يُخرجه عن هذه المضارعة؛ وإنما قال: إن السين وسوف يُخرجانه عن الحال إلى الاستقبال. فمن ادعى أن أن الخفيفة في هذا الباب بمنزلة السين وسوف وأنها مبطلة للمضارعة كان عليه الدلالة على ذلك. لأن ذلك غير محكي عن العرب حكاية اللغة، وقد أفسدنا ما احتج به القائلون بذلك من النحاة. فبطل قولهم.
And among them (the proofs) is that the lām of inception enters it (the verb) as it enters the noun, as in your saying, 'Indeed Zaid is standing' (inna Zaydan la-yaqūmu), so it is equivalent to your saying, 'Indeed Zaid is standing' (inna Zaydan la-qā’imun). And from it is His saying, the Exalted: 'Indeed your Lord will judge between them' (inna rabbaka la-yahkumu baynahum), meaning: He is indeed judging between them. So the present-future verb (al-fi‘l al-muḍāri‘) has occurred in place of the noun in these instances, and that did not prevent verbal agents from entering it. Do you not see that you say, 'Zaid will not stand' (Zaydun lan yaqūma), and you give it accusative case with lan? And its occurring in place of the noun did not prevent the verbal agent from entering it and giving it accusative case according to the principle required in the ruling of verbal inflection. And this clearly invalidates their reliance. And among what indicates the invalidity and corruption of that is that al-Khalīl ibn Aḥmad and others of the leading scholars of Arabic said: 'The verb that has the four prefixes is similar (muḍāri‘) to the noun in the aspects we have mentioned, and they said it is similar, even if it occurs after the lightened an (an al-khafīfa). And al-Khalīl said: The present-future verb is suitable for the present state and suitable for the future. So by this aspect also, it resembles your saying 'man' (rajul), which is suitable for you to mean Zayd and suitable for you to mean ‘Amr. And he did not say that the entry of the lightened an upon it removes it from this resemblance (al-muḍāra‘a). Rather, he said: The sīn and sawfa remove it from the present state to the future. So whoever claims that the lightened an in this matter is like sīn and sawfa and that it annuls the resemblance, he must provide proof of that. Because that is not narrated from the Arabs as a transmitted language, and we have refuted what the grammarians who claim that have argued. So their statement is invalid.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical proof presented by Imam al-Bāqillānī to demonstrate that the present-future verb (al-fi‘l al-muḍāri‘) shares properties with nouns, refuting certain grammarians who claimed that the lightened particle 'an' (an al-khafīfa) removes this similarity. The author begins by showing that the lām of inception (lām al-ibtidā’) enters the verb just as it enters nouns, citing examples like the Quranic verse 'inna rabbaka la-yahkumu baynahum' (Q. 16:124). Here the verb 'yahkumu' stands in the place of a noun. He then argues that this nominal position does not prevent verbal factors (like 'lan') from giving the verb accusative case. This refutes the grammarians' claim that the verb cannot be governed by verbal agents when it resembles a noun. Next, he cites the authority of al-Khalīl ibn Aḥmad and other leading Arabic linguists, who state that the verb with four prefixes is indeed similar (muḍāri‘) to the noun, even after the lightened 'an'. Al-Khalīl further explains that the verb can indicate present or future time, just as a noun like 'rajul' can refer to different individuals. Crucially, al-Khalīl did not say that 'an' removes this resemblance; rather, only 'sīn' and 'sawfa' shift the verb from present to future. Therefore, anyone claiming that 'an' annuls the resemblance must provide transmitted evidence from the Arabs, which is lacking. Since such evidence is absent and the grammarians' arguments have been refuted, their position is invalid. The argument preserves the verb's unique properties while demonstrating its grammatical kinship with the noun.

Key Terms
لام الابتداء
فعل مضارع
أن الخفيفة
مضارعة
عوامل الأفعال
الخليل بن أحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lightened 'an' (an al-khafīfa) removes the verb's resemblance to nouns.
Correction: Al-Khalīl and others state that the verb retains its resemblance to nouns even after 'an'; only 'sīn' and 'sawfa' change its temporal reference.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bāqillānī, what evidence from al-Khalīl refutes the claim that the lightened 'an' annuls the verb's resemblance to nouns?
A) Al-Khalīl said that 'an' gives the verb accusative case.
B) Al-Khalīl said that verbs with prefixes resemble nouns even after 'an', and only 'sīn' and 'sawfa' change the tense.
C) Al-Khalīl said that verbs cannot occur after 'an' at all.
D) Al-Khalīl said that 'an' changes the verb to a noun.

Key Takeaway
The mudari' verb retains its resemblance to nouns even after the lightened 'an', as shown by lām al-ibtidā', verbal government, and al-Khalīl's testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume you know the meaning of a Quranic verse or a religious text without examining the precise linguistic tools? This lesson shows that even a small particle like 'an' carries significant weight for interpretation. Will you commit to studying Arabic grammar more deeply to avoid superficial understanding?
?
When confronted with evidence that refutes your long-held opinion, do you react with defensiveness or humility? The grammarians refused to accept al-Khalīl's testimony; are you willing to submit to scholarly consensus and clear proof, even when it humbles you?
?
This lesson demonstrates that every claim about the religion must be backed by transmitted evidence (naql) or sound reasoning. Think of a belief you hold without clear evidence. Are you prepared to abandon it if it cannot be substantiated from the Quran or Sunnah?

Why Only a Sample of Errors?
Baqillani explains his selective treatment of Mu'tazila errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani deliberately limits his discussion of Mu'tazila errors to a small sample.

Core Idea
A focused book teaches more than an exhaustive one.

Baqillani has been refuting Mu'tazila doctrines throughout this section. Now he pauses to explain a deliberate choice: he is not trying to list every error, but only a representative sample. This short methodological note shows his awareness of the reader's patience and his own purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani lists the types of errors he would have to cover if he were exhaustive.
He uses three terms: ضلالة (misguidance), قبح (ugliness), شنيع (disgraceful).
2
Identify the author's reasoning
He gives two reasons for not being exhaustive: the book would become too long, and he would stray from his intended goal. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the purpose of a sample
Baqillani says even prolixity would only cover a little. This shows humility and strategic focus. Why might a sample be more effective than a full catalogue?
Consider the reader's attention and the author's main argument.
4
Appreciate the patron's role
The passage ends by saying the sample is for 'our master the prince.' This reminds us the book was written for a specific audience with practical needs.
The name is cut off in the manuscript.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو تتبعتُ ذكر ضلالتهم وقبح مذاهبهم وشنيع ما أدخلوا في الدين وخالفوا به توقيف السنن وقول كافّة المسلمين وسائر السلف الصالحين، لطال بذلك الكتاب ولخرجنا بذكره عما لنا قصدنا، ولم نأت مع الإسهاب فيه إلا على القليل منه. وإنما ذكرتُ طرفاً من ذلك لسيدنا الأمير [؟]
Had I pursued the mention of their misguidance, the ugliness of their doctrines, and the disgraceful things they introduced into religion and by which they contradicted the restrictions of the Sunnas and the statement of all Muslims and the rest of the pious predecessors, the book would have been prolonged by that, and we would have departed by mentioning it from what we intended, and we would not have come, even with prolixity therein, except to a little of it. I have only mentioned a part of that for our master the prince [?].
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is the author's concluding remark about his treatment of Mu'tazila errors. Baqillani says that if he were to fully document their misguidance, the book would become too long and would deviate from his main purpose. He acknowledges that even if he were verbose, he could only cover a fraction of their errors. He states that he has only mentioned a sample (ṭaraf) for the sake of his patron, the prince (al-amīr). This shows the author's awareness of the vastness of the topic and his deliberate limitation for practical reasons.

Three Meanings of 'Created in Truth'
Baqillani gives three interpretations of the Qur'anic phrase 'except with the truth'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological meanings Imam Baqillani extracts from the Qur'anic phrase 'except with the truth' regarding creation.

Core Idea
The phrase 'with the truth' carries layered meanings: divine speech, justice, and purpose.

The Qur'anic verse 'We did not create the heavens and the earth except with the truth' is rich with meaning. Imam Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, unpacks this phrase in three ways, each highlighting a different aspect of God's relationship to creation. This lesson explores those interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is structured as a series of possibilities: 'ya'ni', 'yumkin', 'yuhtamil'.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The passage presents three distinct interpretations. Find each one and note what 'haqq' refers to in each case.
The first is explicit, the second and third are introduced as possible meanings.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Ask yourself why each interpretation matters. How does each one answer a different question about God's action in creating?
This connects to the broader theme of God's attributes and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما إلا بالحق يعني أنه ما خلق ذلك إلا بقوله وكلامه الذي هو الحق. ويمكن أن يكون عنى: إني ما خلقتهما ظالماً لخلقهما ولا تجاوزت بفعلهما أمر آمر ولا زجر زاجر. ويحتمل أن يكون عنى: إني ما خلقتهما وكلّفت أهلهما إلا وأنا مريد لإثابة الطائعين وعقوبة العاصين.
And His statement 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them except with the truth' means that He did not create that except by His statement and His speech which is the truth. It is possible that He intended: 'I did not create them while being unjust to their creation, nor did I exceed in My action regarding them the command of any commander or prohibition of any prohibitor.' And it is possible that He intended: 'I did not create them and charge their inhabitants except while I am desiring to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient.'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical discussion. Baqillani presents three possible meanings for the phrase 'إلا بالحق' in the Qur'anic verse. The first interpretation is that 'haqq' refers to God's speech and command—He created by His word which is truth. The second interpretation is that 'haqq' means justice: God did not create unjustly or arbitrarily. The third interpretation links 'haqq' to the purpose of creation: reward for obedience and punishment for disobedience. Each interpretation is distinct and shows different theological emphases.

Key Terms

بقوله
كلامه
ظالماً
إثابة الطائعين
عقوبة العاصين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'except with the truth' has only one fixed meaning.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the phrase admits multiple valid interpretations, each with different theological implications.

Checkpoint

A) God's justice
B) God's speech and word
C) God's power
D) God's existence

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the Qur'anic phrase 'with the truth' admits multiple valid theological interpretations concerning God's speech, justice, and purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Baqillani emphasizes that God did not create unjustly. Think of a time when you felt life was unfair. How does this lesson's insistence on God's inherent justice challenge you to trust Him even when you cannot see the justice? Will you surrender that grievance to Him right now?
?
The third meaning says God created with the desire to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient. Examine your actions this past week: are you living as though that reward and punishment are real? If you truly believed that your obedience matters for your eternal outcome, what would you change today? Write down one concrete step.

Three Views on General Plural Forms
Baqillani enumerates three scholarly views on the interpretation of an unqualified plural form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main scholarly positions on interpreting an unqualified plural form in Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
A general plural doesn't reveal its intention—scholars disagree on what it means without context.

Baqillani opens this lesson by surveying how different schools of thought approach a plural form that appears without a specifying indicator. The disagreement reveals a fundamental tension in uṣūl al-fiqh: should we assume generality, particularity, or suspend judgment? This passage introduces three distinct approaches that set the stage for the broader discussion of takhṣīṣ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Read the Arabic and translation, then list the three positions: the wāqifiyya, aṣḥāb al-khuṣūṣ, and the majority of jurists.
Pay attention to the Arabic names for each group.
2
Understand each position
For each group, ask: what do they do with the plural? Do they take it as general, particular, or suspend?
3
Connect to the bigger picture
This disagreement is foundational for later discussions on specification. Notice how the majority's view (generality) is the one Baqillani's school adopts.
This lesson sets the stage for the rules of takhṣīṣ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلفوا في صيغة الجمع إذا كانت مطلقة عن قرينة التخصيص منها : فتوقفت الواقفية فيها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد من عموم أو خصوص وحملها أصحاب الخصوص على ثلاثة وتوقفوا في الزيادة عليها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد بها وحملها جمهور الفقهاء وكثير من المتكلمين من أصحابنا وغيرهم على العموم في جنسها ولم يخصوا شيئاً منها إلا بدلالة. واختلفوا في أقل الجمع العددي فزعم أهل الظاهر أنه اثنان وقال الشافعي ومالك وأبو حنيفة : إنه ثلاثة. وأجمع أصحابنا على جواز تخصيص القرآن بالقرآن وعلى جواز تخصيصه بالخبر المتواتر والخبر المستفيض الذي يجري مجرى التواتر وأجمعوا على تخصيص السنة بالقرآن والسنة بالسنة . وأما تخصيص القرآن بخبر الواحد فقد أجازه أكثر أصحابنا وأباه بعض المتأخرين منهم . والقياس إذا كان جلياً أو في معنى أصله جاز تخصيص العموم به. وأما القياس الخفي فالخلاف في تخصيص العموم به كالخلاف في تخصيص العموم بخبر الواحد .
And they have differed concerning the plural form (ṣīghat al-jam‘) when it is free from any indicator of specification (qarīnat al-takhṣīṣ). Among them: al-wāqifiyya suspended judgment until evidence reveals whether general or particular is intended; the proponents of particularism (aṣḥāb al-khuṣūṣ) interpreted it as three [or as particular?] and suspended judgment on exceeding that until evidence clarifies the intended meaning; and the majority of jurists and many theologians (mutakallimūn) among our fellows and others interpreted it as general in its genus and did not specify anything from it except by evidence. And they differed concerning the minimum of numerical plurality: the Ẓāhirīs claimed that it is two, while al-Shāfi‘ī, Mālik, and Abū Ḥanīfa said it is three. And our fellows (aṣḥābunā) have unanimously agreed on the permissibility of specifying the Qur'an by the Qur'an, and on the permissibility of specifying it by the mutawātir report and the mustafīḍ report that is equivalent in status to tawātur, and they have unanimously agreed on specifying the Sunna by the Qur'an and the Sunna by the Sunna. As for specifying the Qur'an by a solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid), most of our fellows permitted it, but some later ones forbade it. And analogical reasoning (qiyās), if it is clear (jalī) or falls under the meaning of its original case, it is permissible to specify the general by it. As for hidden analogy (qiyās khafī), the disagreement about specifying the general by it is like the disagreement about specifying the general by a solitary report.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a survey of scholarly positions on the interpretation of general plural forms and the rules of specification (takhṣīṣ). Baqillani first notes that when a plural form appears without any contextual indicator (qarīna) of specification, scholars differ: some (al-wāqifiyya) suspend judgment until evidence reveals the intended meaning; others (aṣḥāb al-khuṣūṣ) take the form as indicating a particular number (likely three) and suspend judgment on more; while the majority (jumhūr al-fuqahā' and many mutakallimūn, including Baqillani's own school) take the form as general in its genus and do not specify without evidence. Then he discusses the minimum number of plural (aqall al-jam‘ al-‘adadī): the Ẓāhirīs hold it is two, while the three major imams (al-Shāfi‘ī, Mālik, Abū Ḥanīfa) hold it is three. Finally, Baqillani lays out the agreed and disputed rules of takhṣīṣ: unanimous agreement on specifying the Qur'an by the Qur'an itself, by mutawātir and mustafīḍ reports, and on specifying the Sunna by the Qur'an or other Sunna; disagreement on specifying the Qur'an by solitary reports (khabar wāḥid) – most of his school permit it, some later ones forbid; and analogy (qiyās) – clear analogy (qiyās jallī) and analogy under the same 'illah permit specification, while hidden analogy (qiyās khafī) is disputed, parallel to the dispute over solitary reports. This lesson shows the nuanced methodology of Islamic legal theory in interpreting general scriptural statements.

Key Terms
صيغة الجمع
قرينة التخصيص
واقفية
أصحاب الخصوص
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They suspend judgment
B) They interpret it as particular (three)
C) They interpret it as general in its genus
D) They always specify it to the minimum

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of an unqualified plural form is disputed: some suspend, some assume a minimum, and most assume generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Wāqifiyya represent intellectual humility—they wait for evidence before taking a stance. In your own study of deen, do you rush to conclusions or wait for clarity? Which of these three positions best describes your own approach, and what does that reveal about your intellectual character?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have been treating a general religious ruling as applying to everyone without exception, while ignoring possible contextual specifications? What would it mean to truly examine the evidence as Baqillani suggests?

Minimum Number for a Plural: Two or Three?
Baqillani records the disagreement on the minimum number of a plural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous disagreement between the Ẓāhirīs and the majority of imams regarding the minimal number for a plural.

Core Idea
Is the minimum for a plural two or three? Scholars disagree based on linguistic and legal reasoning.

A fundamental question in Arabic linguistics and uṣūl al-fiqh is: what is the smallest number that qualifies as a 'plural'? Baqillani records the split between the Ẓāhirī school, which holds two, and the three major imams, who hold three. This seemingly narrow dispute has significant implications for interpreting general statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence on the minimum plural and its translation.
Notice that only two positions are mentioned.
2
Identify the two views
Who says two and who says three? Why might this matter?
3
Reflect on implications
If the minimum is two, then a plural could refer to just two things. If three, it implies at least three. This affects how general commands are understood.

?

Rules of Specification (Takhṣīṣ)
Baqillani lists consensual and disputed rules of specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the agreed and disputed rules of specification among Baqillani's school, including the role of the Qur'an, Sunna, reports, and analogy.

Core Idea
Specification of a general text is permissible through certain evidences, but scholars disagree on the use of solitary reports and hidden analogy.

After laying the groundwork on general plural forms, Baqillani turns to the rules of takhṣīṣ: what sources can specify a general ruling? Here he presents the positions of the Ash‘arī school (aṣḥābunā) on which textual or rational evidences are valid for specification. The passage reveals layers of consensus and disagreement that shape Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensuses
List the types of specification that are unanimously agreed upon by Baqillani's school: Qur'an by Qur'an, Qur'an by mutawātir/mustafīḍ, Sunna by Qur'an and Sunna.
2
Note the disputed cases
Where is there disagreement? On specifying the Qur'an by khabar wāḥid, and on qiyās khafī. Notice the parallel Baqillani draws between the two disputes.
3
Understand the logic
Why are certain evidences accepted? Because they are definitive (like Qur'an and mutawātir) or obviously relevant (qiyās jalī).

Specification is permissible through various sources, but solitary reports and hidden analogy are disputed, mirroring each other.

Classification of General Expressions in Arabic
Baqillani lists five categories of general expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of Arabic expressions that indicate generality according to Baqillani.

Core Idea
Generality in Arabic is expressed through specific noun forms, not just by common sense.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani systematically categorizes the types of Arabic expressions that are understood as general (عموم). He lists five distinct categories, from plural forms without a plural sign to words like 'every' and 'all'. This classification is foundational for interpreting Islamic legal and theological texts, as it helps determine whether a command or statement applies universally or is subject to specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's careful enumeration of categories.
The passage begins with a dialectical context, but the focus is on the list of general expressions.
2
Identify the five categories
As you read, list the five types of general expressions mentioned. Each is introduced by 'ومنها' (among them).
The first category has two sub-types.
3
Understand the concept of relative generality
Notice the opening lines about 'specific in addition to what is more general'. This shows that a word can be general in one sense and specific in another.
Example: 'animal' is general relative to its types but specific relative to 'bodies'.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: How does knowing these categories help in interpreting verses like 'The thief, cut off their hands'?
The definite article on a singular noun can indicate generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : المراد بذلك العاصي الكافر الذي لا إيمان ولا حسنة. خصوص بالإضافة إلى ما هو أعم منه، وإن كان عموماً في نفسه كالحيوان خصوص الأجسام وعموم في أنواعه والأسماء المحمولة على العموم كثيرة منها اللفظ الموضوع للجمع بلا علامة للجمع وهذا نوعان: أحدهما ما له واحد من لفظه كالناس والأنس والجن، والثاني ما ليس له واحد من لفظه كالخيل والإبل والبقر والغنم والنساء. ومنها اسم موضوع للجمع بعلامة الجمع التي هي الواو والنون والياء والنون في الذكور العقلاء والألف والتاء في جمع الإناث وما لا يعقل. ومنها اسم الجنس المفرد إذا دخل عليه لام التعريف من غير إشارة إلى معهود قد سبق ذكره كقوله والسارق والسارقة فاقطعوا أيديهما. وقوله : الزانية والزاني فاجلدوا كل واحد منهما مائة جلدة، ومنها الأسماء المبهمة مثل من وما وأين وكيف ومتى وأي، ومنها ألفاظ يؤكد بها العموم مثل كل وأجمعون واكتعون وابصعون. وقد اختلفوا في صيغة الجمع إذا كانت مطلقة عن قرينة التخصيص منها : فتوقفت الواقفية فيها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد من عموم أو خصوص وحملها أصحاب الخصوص على ثلاثة وتوقفوا في الزيادة عليها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد بها وحملها جمهور الفقهاء وكثير من المتكلمين من أصحابنا وغيرهم على العموم في جنسها ولم يخصوا شيئاً منها إلا بدلالة.
It was said to him: What is intended by that is the disobedient disbeliever who has no faith and no good deed. Specific in addition to what is more general than it, even if it is general in itself, like 'animal' is specific in relation to 'bodies' and general in its types. And the nouns carried upon generality are many: among them the word placed for the plural without a sign of the plural, and this is two types: one of them has a singular from its word like 'people', 'humans', and 'jinn', and the second has no singular from its word like 'horses', 'camels', 'cattle', 'sheep', and 'women'. And among them is a noun placed for the plural with a sign of the plural, which is the waw and nun, ya and nun for rational males, and alif and ta for the plural of females and what does not reason. And among them is the singular genus noun when the definite article enters it without reference to a previously mentioned known, like His saying: 'The male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands.' And His saying: 'The female fornicator and the male fornicator, flog each one of them a hundred lashes.' And among them are ambiguous nouns like 'who', 'what', 'where', 'how', 'when', and 'which'. And among them are words by which generality is emphasized, like 'every', 'all', 'all together', and 'altogether'. And they differed regarding the form of the plural when it is unrestricted from a context of specification: the al-Waqifiyya suspended judgment in it until evidence uncovers what is intended, whether general or specific. And the proponents of specification carried it upon three and stopped regarding exceeding that until evidence uncovers what is intended by it. And the majority of jurists and many of the theologians among our companions and others carried it upon generality in its genus and did not specify anything from it except by evidence.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a classification of general Arabic expressions (al-alfaz al-mahmula 'ala al-'umum) and a discussion of the disagreement about the form of the plural when it is unrestricted. The author begins by stating that when a threatening verse like 'Their reward is hell' is cited, it is answered that the intended meaning is the disobedient disbeliever who has no faith and no good deed, not the sinful believer. The phrase 'specific in addition to what is more general' introduces the concept that a word can be general in one context but specific in another. For example, 'animal' is general in its types but specific relative to 'bodies'. Then the author lists five categories of nouns that indicate generality: (1) nouns that are plural without a plural mark, like 'people' and 'horses', (2) nouns that are plural with a plural mark (waw-nun for rational males, alif-ta for others), (3) singular genus nouns with the definite article without reference to a specific previously mentioned individual, as in 'the thief' meaning every thief, (4) ambiguous nouns like 'who' and 'what', and (5) emphasizing words like 'every' and 'all'. After listing these, the author addresses the disagreement about the form of the plural (sigha al-jam') when it is unrestricted, i.e., without a context that specifies it. Three positions are mentioned: (a) al-Waqifiyya suspend judgment until evidence clarifies whether it is general or specific, (b) the proponents of specification (ashab al-khusus) restrict it to exactly three and stop, and (c) the majority of jurists and many theologians (including the author's school) hold that it indicates generality in its genus unless there is evidence to specify it.

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
لفظ الموضوع للجمع
اسم الجنس المفرد
الأسماء المبهمة
ألفاظ التأكيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definite article on a singular noun always refers to a specific previously mentioned individual.
Correction: When the definite article enters a singular genus noun without reference to a known individual, it indicates generality, as in 'the thief' meaning every thief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT among the categories of general expressions?
A) Plural without plural sign
B) Plural with plural sign
C) Singular noun with possessive pronoun
D) Ambiguous nouns

Key Takeaway
Baqillani identifies five distinct linguistic forms that indicate generality in Arabic: plural forms (with or without plural signs), the singular genus noun with the definite article, ambiguous nouns, and emphasis words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'Cut off the hand of the thief' (السارق), do you automatically apply it to every thief without exception? How does Baqillani's classification of 'singular genus noun with definite article' challenge that automatic assumption? What must you do before you understand the ruling?
?
Think of a time in your daily life when you used a general statement (e.g., 'People always...') and later realized it was inaccurate. How might Baqillani's categories help you be more precise in your own speech and understanding of Islamic texts? Write a specific commitment to pause and verify before you generalize.
?
The lesson shows that even a simple word like 'الناس' (people) has a specific linguistic category. How does recognizing these categories change the way you read the Quran? What Arabic grammar skill will you seek to learn this week to better understand Allah's words?

Disagreement on the Unrestricted Plural Form
Baqillani reports three opinions on the unrestricted plural form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on whether an unrestricted plural indicates generality or specificity.

Core Idea
When a plural has no context, scholars disagree: some suspend judgment, some specify to three, and most hold it remains general.

After listing the categories of general expressions, Imam Baqillani turns to a key dispute: does a plural form that appears without any context of specification (qarina takhsis) indicate generality or not? He records three distinct positions held by different theological and legal schools, including the stance of the majority of jurists and theologians from his own school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the disagreement
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the three groups mentioned: al-Waqifiyya, ashab al-khusus, and the majority.
The passage is short but dense; each group's position is stated succinctly.
2
Identify the three positions
List the three positions: 1) al-Waqifiyya suspend judgment until evidence clarifies. 2) Ashab al-khusus restrict it to three and stop. 3) The majority hold it indicates generality in its genus unless specified.
The majority includes 'jumhur al-fuqaha' and 'kathir min al-mutakallimin'.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each position
Consider why each group might adopt its position. For example, the Waqifiyya are cautious, while the majority rely on the default understanding of plural forms.
The passage does not give reasons, but reflect on the implications.
4
Relate to the previous lesson
Note that this disagreement concerns the 'form of the plural' which is one of the general expressions listed earlier. This shows the practical application of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : المراد بذلك العاصي الكافر الذي لا إيمان ولا حسنة. خصوص بالإضافة إلى ما هو أعم منه، وإن كان عموماً في نفسه كالحيوان خصوص الأجسام وعموم في أنواعه والأسماء المحمولة على العموم كثيرة منها اللفظ الموضوع للجمع بلا علامة للجمع وهذا نوعان: أحدهما ما له واحد من لفظه كالناس والأنس والجن، والثاني ما ليس له واحد من لفظه كالخيل والإبل والبقر والغنم والنساء. ومنها اسم موضوع للجمع بعلامة الجمع التي هي الواو والنون والياء والنون في الذكور العقلاء والألف والتاء في جمع الإناث وما لا يعقل. ومنها اسم الجنس المفرد إذا دخل عليه لام التعريف من غير إشارة إلى معهود قد سبق ذكره كقوله والسارق والسارقة فاقطعوا أيديهما. وقوله : الزانية والزاني فاجلدوا كل واحد منهما مائة جلدة، ومنها الأسماء المبهمة مثل من وما وأين وكيف ومتى وأي، ومنها ألفاظ يؤكد بها العموم مثل كل وأجمعون واكتعون وابصعون. وقد اختلفوا في صيغة الجمع إذا كانت مطلقة عن قرينة التخصيص منها : فتوقفت الواقفية فيها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد من عموم أو خصوص وحملها أصحاب الخصوص على ثلاثة وتوقفوا في الزيادة عليها إلى أن يكشف الدليل عن المراد بها وحملها جمهور الفقهاء وكثير من المتكلمين من أصحابنا وغيرهم على العموم في جنسها ولم يخصوا شيئاً منها إلا بدلالة.

Are you willing to embrace the broader scope of Allah's commands until He Himself gives you a reason to specify? How will that change your obedience today?

Minimum Number for Plural: Two or Three?
Baqillani reports the disagreement over the minimum numerical plural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among scholars regarding the minimum number that constitutes a valid plural in Arabic.

Core Idea
The minimum plural is disputed: Zahiris say two, major Imams say three.

The first step in understanding how general texts are specified is to know what counts as a plural. The scholars disagreed on the minimum number of individuals that form a valid plural. This disagreement has implications for interpreting general commands and prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the dispute
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note the two opposing views.
This is a straightforward report of disagreement.
2
Identify the two positions
The Zahiris claim two, while al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa say three. Notice that the author does not state his own view explicitly here.
The author's school likely follows the three.
3
Reflect on significance
Consider why this difference matters for legal interpretation. A smaller minimum plural affects how many individuals are needed to constitute a general command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في أقل الجمع العددي فزعم أهل الظاهر أنه اثنان وقال الشافعي ومالك وأبو حنيفة : إنه ثلاثة. وأجمع أصحابنا على جواز تخصيص القرآن بالقرآن وعلى جواز تخصيصه بالخبر المتواتر والخبر المستفيض الذي يجري مجرى التواتر وأجمعوا على تخصيص السنة بالقرآن والسنة بالسنة . وأما تخصيص القرآن بخبر الواحد فقد أجازه أكثر أصحابنا وأباه بعض المتأخرين منهم . والقياس إذا كان جلياً أو في معنى أصله جاز تخصيص العموم به. وأما القياس الخفي فالخلاف في تخصيص العموم به كالخلاف في تخصيص العموم بخبر الواحد.
And they differed regarding the minimum numerical plural: the Zahiris claimed that it is two, and al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa said: it is three. And our companions agreed upon the permissibility of specifying the Qur'an by the Qur'an and upon the permissibility of specifying it by the mutawatir khabar and the mustafid khabar that runs in the course of tawatur, and they agreed upon specifying the Sunna by the Qur'an and the Sunna by the Sunna. As for specifying the Qur'an by a single-narrator report (khabar al-wahid), most of our companions permitted it, and some of their later scholars forbade it. And qiyas, if it is manifest (jali) or in the meaning of its origin, it is permissible to specify the general by it. As for hidden qiyas (qiyas khafi), the disagreement in specifying the general by it is like the disagreement in specifying the general by a single-narrator report.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the minimum number that constitutes a valid plural, and the permissibility of specifying a general text (takhsis al-'umum) by various sources. First, the author reports the disagreement about the minimum numerical plural (aqall al-jam' al-'adadi): the Zahiris hold that two is sufficient, while the three major Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa) hold that three is the minimum. The author's own school (ashabuna) apparently holds the latter view. Second, the passage moves to the rules of specification. The companions (presumably the Ash'ari or Maliki school) agree on the following: it is permissible to specify the Qur'an by other Qur'anic verses, and by mutawatir and mustafid reports (which are as strong as tawatur). They also agree on specifying the Sunna by the Qur'an and the Sunna by other Sunna. However, there is disagreement about specifying the Qur'an by a single-narrator report (khabar al-wahid): most of their scholars permit it, but some later ones forbid it. Finally, the passage discusses qiyas (analogical reasoning). If the qiyas is 'manifest' (jali) or equivalent in meaning to the original ruling, it may be used to specify a general text. But for 'hidden' qiyas (qiyas khafi), the disagreement is similar to that of khabar al-wahid: some allow it, some do not.

Key Terms
أقل الجمع العددي
أهل الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the minimum plural is always two.
Correction: Major Imams hold that three is the minimum; the issue is disputed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group claims that the minimum numerical plural is two?
A) The Zahiris
B) al-Shafi'i
C) Malik
D) Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
The minimum number for a valid plural is disputed between the Zahiris (two) and the major Imams (three).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a certain religious ruling or understanding is universally agreed upon, only to later discover that it is actually disputed? How does that realization affect your humility before scholars and your commitment to seeking knowledge?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among scholars (like this one on the minimum plural), do you tend to follow the majority without question, or do you investigate the evidence? What does your reaction reveal about your desire for truth over convenience?
?
This passage shows that even foundational linguistic rules are subject to ijtihad. Does your heart humble you knowing that certainty in religious matters is often preceded by legitimate disagreement? Will you commit to not being dogmatic about issues where scholars have genuinely differed, and instead seek to understand the reasoning behind each position?

Rules for Specifying General Texts
Baqillani enumerates the sources that can specify general texts and their acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various sources that can specify a general text and the agreement/disagreement on their use.

Core Idea
General texts can be specified by Quran, mutawatir sunna, and manifest qiyas, but khabar al-wahid is disputed.

After establishing the minimum for a plural, Baqillani turns to the rules for specifying general texts. He outlines which sources can be used to restrict a general ruling, noting points of agreement and disagreement among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on takhsis
Read the Arabic passage on specification. It lists several sources and their rules.
The passage has several parts: agreement on Quran, mutawatir, sunna, and then disagreement on khabar al-wahid and qiyas.
2
Identify the agreed sources
Find which sources all scholars agree can specify the Quran and Sunna. They agree: Quran specifies Quran, mutawatir and mustafid specify Quran, Sunna specifies Quran and Sunna.
3
Identify the disputed sources
Note that khabar al-wahid is controversial: most permit it, some later scholars forbid. Also, manifest qiyas is allowed, but hidden qiyas is disputed like khabar al-wahid.
4
Summarize the rules
Create a mental table: for Quran, agreed sources; for Sunna, agreed sources; for qiyas, conditional.
This helps consolidate the information.

Refuting the Claim that 'Man' Implies Universality
Baqillani refutes the claim that conditional 'man' requires universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that 'man' in conditional clauses implies universality by showing it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Conditional 'man' does not always imply universality—Baqillani turns the argument against the objector.

In this passage, Baqillani counters an opponent's objection that the word 'man' (whoever) in conditional clauses like "Whoever comes with a good deed..." must be universal, covering both reward and punishment. He responds by showing that the same logic would force an unacceptable conclusion, thereby demonstrating the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that 'man' in a conditional context implies generality (istighraq), meaning it applies to all individuals in both reward and punishment groups.
The objection is based on the structure of condition and recompense.
2
Read Baqillani's answer
Baqillani turns the argument around: if 'man' is universal, then the promise of reward for a good deed would also be universal, but that's not always true.
He uses the same verse to counter the claim.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Note that Baqillani does not provide a positive alternative; he merely shows the opponent's reasoning is inconsistent.
This is a common method in kalam: reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: قوله من ورد مورد الشرط والجزاء. وهذا يوجب استغراق المجازين. قيل له: فقل لأجل هذا بعينه إن من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها وإنه يعطى خيراً منها وهو من فزع يوم القيامة آمن لأجل قوله: من جاء بالحسنة فله خير منها.
If he says: 'His statement "whoever" occurs in the context of condition and recompense, and this necessitates encompassing both reward and punishment groups.' We say to him: 'Then say for this very reason that whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times its like and will be given better than it and will be secure from the terror of the Day of Resurrection because of His statement: "Whoever comes with a good deed will have better than it."'
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector argues that the word 'man' (whoever) in a conditional context like "Whoever comes with a good deed..." implies generality (istighraq), meaning it applies to everyone who performs the action. The author responds by turning the argument back on the objector: if you insist on strict generality, then you must also accept that every good deed results in tenfold reward and security on Judgment Day, which does not hold universally. Thus, the objector's own reasoning leads to an implausible conclusion, proving that 'man' does not necessarily imply absolute generality.

Key Terms
الشرط والجزاء
استغراق
المجازين
فزع يوم القيامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'man' (whoever) in conditional clauses always implies universal application to all individuals.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such universal application leads to contradictions, so 'man' does not always imply absolute generality.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the claim that 'man' in conditional clauses is universal?
A) By citing another verse
B) By showing the same logic would require universal reward, which is false
C) By denying that 'man' is conditional
D) By saying the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'man' is universal by showing the same logic would force an absurd conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any aspect of your deen where you have assumed a general rule applies universally, without considering exceptions? How can you train yourself to look for context before jumping to conclusions?
?
Think of a time you took a verse of the Quran out of its context to support a point. How does Baqillani’s careful approach challenge you to be more precise in your own understanding?
?
This lesson shows that even a single word like 'man' can be misapplied. What personal assumptions about faith might you be holding too rigidly? What would it take for you to examine them honestly?

Linguistic Consistency in Naming the Grave Sinner
Baqillani refutes an objection by pointing out linguistic inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses linguistic principles to refute the objection that a grave sinner cannot be called a good-doer, and why labels are not applied based on a single action.

Core Idea
Labels like 'good-doer' and 'transgressor' are not mutually exclusive based on one act.

In this brief exchange, an objector tries to isolate the grave sinner as someone who cannot be called a 'good-doer'. Baqillani immediately turns the tables: by the same logic, a believing monotheist who commits a major sin cannot be called a 'disobedient transgressor' either. The point is not to deny sin but to expose the inconsistency in the objector's use of language.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objector makes a claim, and Baqillani responds by drawing a parallel.
The response mirrors the objector's phrasing to highlight the inconsistency.
2
Identify the linguistic principle
Baqillani states that both applications are 'a departure from language'. Understand what this means: language does not allow us to apply a label like 'good-doer' or 'transgressor' based on a single act alone.
The principle is that labels are holistic, not act-based.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this matters for the debate about grave sinners. If the objector's own logic leads to absurdity (e.g., calling a believer a transgressor), then the premise that a grave sinner cannot be a good-doer is flawed.
This is a common kalam technique: reducing an opponent's position to an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: إن صاحب الكبيرة لا يُسمى محسناً. قيل له: والمؤمن الموحد المصدّق الله ولرسوله لا يُسمى عاصياً متعدياً لحدوده. وكل ذلك خروج عن اللغة.
If he says: 'The one who commits a major sin is not called a good-doer.' We say to him: 'And the believing monotheist who affirms God and His messenger is not called a disobedient transgressor. And all of that is a departure from language.'
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This is a short objection-and-answer focusing on linguistic consistency. The objector argues that a grave sinner cannot be called a 'good-doer' (muhsin), implying that such a person falls under threat. Baqillani responds by pointing out that just as a grave sinner is not called a good-doer, a believer (mu'min) who is a monotheist and affirms God and His messenger is not called a 'disobedient transgressor' (asi muta'ddin) because of his sin. Both are departures from proper linguistic usage. The point is that labels like 'good-doer' and 'transgressor' are not applied exclusively based on one action; the believer retains his faith-based identity despite sin.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة
محسناً
عاصياً
متعدياً
اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a person commits a major sin, they lose all positive labels like 'believer' or 'good-doer' and should only be called a 'transgressor'.
Correction: Baqillani shows that linguistic norms prevent applying such labels based on a single action; the believer retains his identity despite sin.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the linguistic issue with calling a grave sinner a 'good-doer'?
A) It is correct because he has some good deeds
B) It is correct because he is a believer
C) It is incorrect because the believer is not called a transgressor either
D) It is incorrect because he is a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
Linguistic consistency demands that labels like 'good-doer' and 'transgressor' are holistic, not based on a single sin or good deed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as a 'good person' or 'bad person' based on a single action you witnessed, which part of this lesson are you ignoring? Will you commit to reserving holistic judgment until you see the full picture?

?
If linguistic consistency is so important in naming others, how consistent are you with the names you give yourself—'hopeless', 'weak', 'hypocrite'—when Allah names you 'believer'? Will you match your self-talk to Allah's labels from tonight onwards?

Demonstrating That 'Man' Can Be Specific
Baqillani demonstrates that 'man' can be specific using Quranic and poetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses linguistic and scriptural evidence to show that the word 'man' (من) can refer to a specific individual rather than a universal class.

Core Idea
Context, not just the word, determines whether 'man' is general or specific.

In this passage, Baqillani tackles a key methodological point: the Arabic word 'man' is ambiguous between generality and specificity. He does not merely state this—he proves it through three concrete examples: everyday speech, a Qur'anic verse, and a line of classical poetry. By showing that context and intention can restrict 'man' to a particular referent, he demonstrates that apparent general statements cannot always be taken at face value. This is a crucial principle for theological argumentation, especially when interpreting divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation slowly. Notice the flow: a claim, then examples. Do not worry about details yet.
The passage is dense but short. Read it twice: once for sense, once for structure.
2
Identify the three examples
Baqillani gives three distinct examples of 'man' being used specifically. List them: ordinary speech, Quranic verse, and poetic line. Reflect on each.
The examples are arranged from simplest (common speech) to more complex (divine and poetic speech).
3
Understand the reasoning behind each example
For each example, ask: Why does the specific interpretation hold? What context prevents the general reading? For instance, in the Qur'anic verse, he argues that God cannot intend all rulers as disbelievers, so 'man' must refer to some.
Pay special attention to the poetic example: it includes a theological point about God not wronging anyone.
4
Connect to the larger methodological point
Baqillani is not just making a linguistic observation—he is establishing a rule for interpreting revealed texts. The apparent generality of an expression can be overridden by context, reason, or other evidence.
This principle is fundamental in Kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع أن قوله من يصلح للعموم وللخصوص وهو معرض لهما، لأن القائل يقول: «جاءني من دعوته وكلمت من عرفته، وهو يريد الواحد منهم الذي عرفه ودعاه، وهو دعاه وعرفه». وقال الله عزّ وجلّ: بعض من: ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون، ولم يُرد أن حكام المسلمين كفَّار إذا تركوا الحكم بما أنزل الله، وإنما أراد بعض من لم يحكم بما أنزل الله. وقال الشاعر: ومن لا يظلم الناس يظلم، ومن لا يدع عن حوضه بسلاحه يُهدَّم، ولم يُرد أن كل من لا يظلم الناس يُظلم، لأن الله عزّ وجلّ لا يظلم الناس ولا يجوز أن يُظلم؛ ولا كل من كان غير ذائد عن حوضه بسلاحه هُدِم.
Moreover, His statement 'man' is suitable for both generality and specificity, and it is exposed to both. Because a speaker says: 'The one whom I called came to me and I spoke to the one whom I knew,' and he intends a single one of them whom he knew and called, and he called him and knew him. And Allah Almighty said 'some of those': 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, then those are the disbelievers.' He did not intend that all Muslim rulers are disbelievers if they abandon judgment by what Allah revealed, but rather He intended some of those who do not judge by what Allah revealed. And the poet said: 'And whoever does not wrong people will be wronged, and whoever does not defend his watering hole with his weapon will be destroyed.' He did not intend that everyone who does not wrong people will be wronged, because Allah Almighty does not wrong people and cannot be wronged; nor that everyone who is not a defender of his watering hole with his weapon will be destroyed.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the usage of 'man' (من) in Arabic. The author states that 'man' can be used for both general (عموم) and specific (خصوص) meanings, and it is susceptible to both interpretations. He provides three examples: 1) Ordinary speech: someone says 'The one I called came to me' but means a specific person. 2) Quranic usage: the verse 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed, those are the disbelievers' does not mean all Muslim rulers who fail to judge by divine law are disbelievers; it refers to specific people. 3) Poetic example: lines from Zuhayr's poem that seem general ('whoever does not wrong people will be wronged') are not meant universally, because God never wrongs anyone. Thus, the apparent meaning (zāhir) of such verses cannot be clung to absolutely, because the word itself allows specification.

Key Terms
العموم
الخصوص
من
ظاهر
الكافرون
حكام المسلمين
الذائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabic word 'man' always implies generality (عموم).
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that 'man' can be specific when context or reason requires it, as shown in ordinary speech, the Qur'an, and poetry.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Baqillani's examples demonstrating that 'man' can be specific?

B) The Qur'anic verse about judging by Allah's revelation
C) A prophetic hadith
D) A line of poetry from Zuhayr

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that the word 'man' is not inherently general; its interpretation depends on context, intention, and rational constraints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would it change your heart to pause and ask: 'O Allah, am I misusing Your words to condemn others? Give me the humility to seek the intended meaning.'
?
Baqillani shows that even a poet's line cannot be taken universally because of God's nature. Are there any verses or hadith you have taken literally without considering other rational or theological constraints? What would it cost you today to repent from that misinterpretation and seek proper understanding?
?
The word 'man' is ambiguous, and context determines its meaning. In your own life, when you hear or read Islamic texts, do you rush to apply them as general rules, or do you honestly examine the context, intention, and rational limits? What would it look like to approach Allah's words with that level of care starting right now?

Reconciling Hadiths on Intercession for Sinners
Baqillani responds to objections about intercession for sinners from hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani reconciles hadiths that seem to deny intercession for sinners with the established doctrine of intercession for sinful believers.

Core Idea
Hadiths on sin and faith can be reconciled through careful interpretation.

One of the strongest objections against intercession for sinful believers comes from hadiths that say such sinners are 'not from us.' Baqillani shows that these reports are not contradictory when properly understood. In this passage, he presents three plausible interpretations that remove the apparent conflict, then reinforces his argument with a famous hadith and a hermeneutical principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the objection first, then the three interpretations.
Pay attention to the hadith of Abu al-Darda at the end—it is a key proof.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Baqillani offers three ways to understand hadiths that seem to deny faith to sinners. List them in your own words.
The third interpretation involves hyperbole (تغليظ) and is compared to other hyperbolic expressions.
3
See how they remove contradiction
After the interpretations, Baqillani states that the contradiction is invalidated. Reflect on how each interpretation preserves the possibility of intercession.
The contradiction is only apparent, not real.
4
Examine the proof from Abu al-Darda
Read the hadith of Abu al-Darda where the Prophet confirms that even adulterers and thieves enter Paradise if they have faith. This supports the overall conclusion.
5
Learn the reconciling principle
Baqillani ends by stating that conflicting texts must be reconciled through construction and arrangement, just as with Quranic verses. This is a methodological takeaway.
This principle applies beyond this specific issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشفع الرسل صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم فيمن ليس بمؤمن؟ وكذلك روي عنه أنه قال: ليس منا من بات بطيناً وجاره خميص، فليس منا. فكيف تحصل الشفاعة لمن ليس من أهل ملته؟ يقال لهم: هذه الأخبار أيضاً محتملة لوجوه، إذا صرفت إليها لم تكن معارضة لخبر الشفاعة.
فيحتمل أن يكون المراد بقوله: لا يزني الزاني حين يزني وهو مؤمن ولا يسرق السارق حين يسرق وهو مؤمن: إذا فعلا ذلك مستحلّين للزنى والسرقة ومكذبين بتحريمهما. ولا شفاعة لمن زنى مستحلاً لذلك ويحتمل أن يكون المراد بذلك أنه ليس بمؤمن كالمؤمن الذي لم يكن منه زنى ولا سرقة في البرّ والطهارة والسلامة من الذنوب.
ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك إنما خرج على مذهب التغليظ والمبالغة في الزجر على سبيل قوله: لا صلاة لجار المسجد إلا في المسجد. وكذلك قوله: من غشنا فليس منا، وليس منا من بات بطيناً وجاره خميصاً.
وإذا حملت هذه الأخبار على هذا التأويل بطل التعارض. وقد روى أبو الدرداء عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم أنه قال: لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنة. قال: فقلت: يا رسول الله، وإن زنى وإن سرق؟ وأنه ردد ذلك عليه حتى قال له في الثانية أو الثالثة: نعم، وإن رغم أنف أبي الدرداء! وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، وجب حمل هذه الأخبار على البناء والترتيب كما يجب ذلك في آي القرآن المتعارضة الظواهر.
Do the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, intercede for one who is not a believer? Likewise it is narrated from him that he said: 'He is not from us who sleeps full while his neighbor is hungry, he is not from us.' So how can intercession be obtained for one who is not of his community? It is said to them: These reports are also susceptible to interpretations, and if they are directed to them, they are not contradictory to the report of intercession. It is possible that what is meant by his saying: 'The adulterer does not commit adultery when he commits adultery while he is a believer, and the thief does not steal when he steals while he is a believer' is when they do that considering adultery and theft permissible and denying their prohibition. And there is no intercession for one who commits adultery considering it permissible. And it is possible that what is meant by that is that he is not a believer like the believer who has not committed adultery or theft in righteousness, purity, and safety from sins. And it is possible that this was said on the basis of intensification and exaggeration in deterrence, by way of his saying: 'There is no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque.' And also his saying: 'Whoever cheats us is not from us,' and 'He is not from us who sleeps full while his neighbor is hungry.' And when these reports are carried on this interpretation, the contradiction is invalidated. And Abu al-Darda narrated from the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, that he said: 'There is no god but Allah, (one) enters Paradise.' He said: I said: O Messenger of Allah, even if he commits adultery and even if he steals? And he repeated that to him until he said to him on the second or third time: 'Yes, even if Abu al-Darda's nose is rubbed in the dust!' And if that is so, it is necessary to carry these reports on (the principle of) construction and arrangement, just as that is necessary in the apparent conflicting verses of the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a defense of intercession for sinful believers. The author first presents an objection: Prophetic hadiths seem to say that sinners are not 'from us' and therefore cannot receive intercession. He then offers three possible interpretations of those hadiths. First, the hadith 'no believer commits adultery while believing' means that when one commits such sins, he does so not as a believer but as one who deems the sin permissible and denies its prohibition. Second, the phrase 'not a believer' means not a believer in the sense of perfect faith and purity from sin. Third, these statements are hyperbole and deterrence, like 'no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque'—meaning it is not complete or ideal. After showing that these interpretations remove the contradiction, he cites the well-known hadith of Abu al-Darda where the Prophet confirms that even adulterers and thieves will enter Paradise if they have faith. Finally, he asserts that apparently conflicting texts must be reconciled through proper interpretation, just as conflicting Quranic verses are harmonized.

Key Terms
شفاعة
تأويل
تغليظ
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that hadiths like 'not from us' exclude all sinners unconditionally from intercession and even from faith.
Correction: Baqillani shows these hadiths can be interpreted as referring only to those who deem sin permissible, or as hyperbole, or as negation of perfect faith, thus preserving intercession for sinful believers who acknowledge the sin.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the interpretations Baqillani offers for the hadith 'لا يزني الزاني حين يزني وهو مؤمن'?
A) It refers to one who commits adultery while considering it permissible and denying its prohibition.
B) It means the adulterer is not a believer in the sense of perfect faith and purity.
C) It is a statement of actual disbelief (kufr) that excludes the perpetrator from Islam entirely.
D) It is hyperbole and exaggeration meant to deter.

Key Takeaway
Apparent contradictions in hadiths about sin and faith can be resolved through nuanced interpretation, affirming both the seriousness of sin and the hope of intercession for believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin while telling yourself it was 'not that bad'? How does Baqillani's distinction between sinning while acknowledging its prohibition and sinning while deeming it permissible challenge your view of that sin today?
?
The hadith 'not from us' is hyperbole to deter. When do you use harsh language against yourself or others? Are you more likely to treat your own sins with leniency or severity, and what does that reveal about your heart?
?
Baqillani insists on reconciling conflicting texts rather than dismissing one. Is there a verse or hadith you have ignored or explained away because it clashed with your lifestyle? What would it cost you to hold it as true alongside the hope of intercession?
