The Foundation of Report Classification
Baqillani explains the importance of classifying reports for correct knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classifying reports is the essential first step for attaining correct knowledge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Classifying reports is the key to distinguishing truth from falsehood.

This passage opens Baqillani's chapter on reports (akhbār). He states that the most important step is grasping the different types of reports — those that force certainty, those that require reasoning, and so on. This classification determines how we treat each report.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Baqillani lists several categories of reports in one long sentence.
The passage is dense but each category is distinct.
2
Identify the five categories of reports
Find and list each category mentioned: reports producing necessary knowledge, those known through reflection, those that could be true or false, those requiring action without certainty, and those proven false.
These categories will be explained in detail later in the chapter.
3
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Ask yourself why Baqillani begins with classification. How does knowing the type of a report help in deciding whether to accept it or act upon it?
Classification is the foundation (أصل) for judging reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن أصل هذا الباب الذي بمعرفته يُتوصل إلى علم الصواب منه هو الوقوف على جملة أقسام الأخبار، وما يوجب العلم منها اضطراراً، وما يقتصر عن ذلك مما يُعلم نظراً واستدلالاً، ولا سبيل إلى العلم بصحته مما يمكن أن يكون صدقاً ويمكن أن يكون كذباً، وما يوجب العمل دون العلم من هذه الأخبار، وما قد قطع الدليل على بطلانه وكذب ناقليه منها.
Indeed, the foundation of this chapter, by knowledge of which one arrives at correct knowledge of it, is the understanding of the totality of the divisions of reports: what necessitates knowledge from them necessarily, and what falls short of that and is known by reflection and inference, and what there is no way to know its correctness from what can be true and can be false, and what necessitates action without knowledge from these reports, and what the proof has decisively indicated to be false and its narrators liars.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement that outlines the purpose of the chapter on reports (akhbār). Baqillani explains that the key (أصل) to attaining correct knowledge in this subject is to understand the various divisions of reports. He then enumerates the categories that will be discussed: reports that produce necessary knowledge (اضطراراً), reports that produce knowledge through reflection and inference (نظراً واستدلالاً), reports whose truth cannot be known because they could be either true or false, reports that require action without knowledge (يوجب العمل دون العلم), and reports whose falsehood is proven by evidence. Thus, the passage sets the agenda for the subsequent detailed classification.

Key Terms
خبر (khabar)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
علم نظري (inferential knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the 'foundation' (أصل) refers to a specific report, but it actually refers to the principle of classification itself.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly says the foundation is 'the understanding of the totality of the divisions of reports'.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the 'foundation of this chapter' (أصل هذا الباب)?
A) The definition of a report
B) The classification of reports into divisions
C) The proof of God's existence
D) The refutation of heretics

Key Takeaway
The first step in evaluating any report is to classify it according to the categories outlined by Baqillani.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a report about a matter of faith or practice, do you ever pause to classify it as certain, probable, or uncertain? How might deliberately applying Baqillani's categories change the way you receive and act on religious information starting today?
?

?
If you were to examine the reports you most rely on for your beliefs—like those about Allah, the Prophet, or the afterlife—could you confidently say they fall into the category of necessary or inferential knowledge? If not, what needs to change in your learning to seek that certainty?

Refutation of False Conditions for Tawatur
Baqillani refutes false conditions for tawatur transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mass transmission (tawatur) does not require transmitters to differ in religion, homeland, or social status; rather, knowledge can be attained from a single community or region.

Core Idea
Tawatur does not require diversity; knowledge can come from one group.

Some scholars argued that for a report to reach the level of tawatur (mass transmission yielding certain knowledge), the transmitters must come from different religions, distant lands, and be in a state of humiliation. In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani refutes these conditions with clear counterexamples. He shows that tawatur can be achieved from a single religious community or a single region, and that humiliation or jizya payment is not required. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for evaluating mass-transmitted reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Baqillani's argument. Notice the list of false conditions followed by counterexamples.
The passage uses a dense style; read it twice.
2
Identify the false conditions
List the conditions that some people mistakenly require for tawatur: differences in religion, separation of homelands, distance of lands, difference of lineages, diversity of causes, being in a house of humiliation, and paying jizya.
These are introduced with 'ما يغلط قوم من أهل النظر بذكره'.
3
Highlight the refutation
Baqillani provides counterexamples: knowledge can come from one religion, one father's children, adjacent regions, one town, and from people not humiliated or paying jizya. Circle these in the text.
The phrase 'قد يقع العلم' signals his refutation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this affects the criteria for accepting a mutawatir report. Baqillani emphasizes that the intrinsic conditions of tawatur (large numbers, impossibility of collusion) are sufficient; external diversity is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الصفات التي يجب لزومها لأهل التواتر دون ما يغلط قوم من أهل النظر بذكره من اختلاف الديانات والملل وتفرق الأوطان وتباعد الديار واختلاف الأنساب وتغاير الأسباب وأن يكونوا في دار ذلة وأن تؤخذ منهم جزية إلى غير ذلك مما يذكرونه من الأوصاف لأنه قد يقع العلم بخبر أهل ملة واحدة وبني أب واحد وأهل بقاع متجاورة وبلدة واحدة ويقع العلم بخبرهم وإن لم يكونوا في دار ذلة ولم تؤخذ منهم جزية وقع لنا العلم بالبلدان وغيرها من الأمور بنقل من ليس في دار ذلة ولا من يعطي الجزية.
These attributes which must be present in the people of tawatur, contrary to what some of the people of speculation err by mentioning of differences of religions and sects, and separation of homelands, and distance of lands, and difference of lineages, and diversity of causes, and that they be in a house of humiliation, and that jizya be taken from them, and other such attributes they mention—because knowledge may occur from the report of the people of one religion and the children of one father and the people of adjacent regions and one town, and knowledge occurs from their report even if they are not in a house of humiliation and jizya is not taken from them. Knowledge has reached us of countries and other matters by the transmission of one who is not in a house of humiliation nor one who pays jizya.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the conditions for tawatur (mass transmission). Some scholars had claimed that for a report to be mutawatir, the transmitters must come from different religions, homelands, etc., and must be in a state of humiliation and paying jizya. Baqillani argues that these conditions are not necessary. He points out that knowledge can be attained from the report of a single religious community, or the children of one father, or people from adjacent regions or a single town. Moreover, such knowledge can be obtained even if the transmitters are not in a state of humiliation and do not pay jizya. Indeed, he says we have knowledge of countries and other matters from the transmission of people who are not in such a state. Therefore, the claimed conditions are invalid.

Key Terms
تواتر
خبر
أهل النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawatur requires diversity in religion, homeland, and social status (e.g., humiliation and jizya).
Correction: Baqillani shows that knowledge can be obtained from a homogeneous group, such as people of one religion, one region, or one family, even without humiliation or jizya.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is sufficient for a report to be mutawatir and produce knowledge?
A) Transmitters must be from different religions
B) Transmitters must be from different lands
C) Transmitters can be from one religion or region
D) Transmitters must be in a state of humiliation

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the notion that tawatur requires diversity of religion, homeland, or social status; knowledge can be attained from a uniform group.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever added extra conditions to accepting truth from someone because of their background or status, even though the message itself was clear? How does Baqillani's refutation challenge you to reconsider your own biases?
?
This passage shows that knowledge can come from a homogeneous group, even from those considered lowly (in humiliation). In your life, who have you dismissed as unreliable due to their circumstances? How can you be more open to truth from unexpected sources?
?
Are you willing to accept certain knowledge from a source that doesn't meet your personal criteria of diversity or prestige? What would that mean for how you learn about your deen?

The Companions' Approval as Proof of Valid Appointment
Baqillani presents the historical evidence of Companions' unanimous approval of Abu Bakr's appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the Companions' silent approval as evidence for the validity of Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar.

Core Idea
Silence of the Companions was active consensus, not mere politeness.

Baqillani begins his proof by pointing to the historical fact that when Abu Bakr appointed Umar, all the Companions present approved. He argues that their lack of objection, despite their ability to speak, indicates a consensus that the appointment was valid. This lesson examines that argument and the principle that the community cannot agree on error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (up to the point where it introduces the consensus argument) and its translation.
2
Identify the objection
Notice how Baqillani sets up a hypothetical question from an interlocutor asking for evidence.
3
Analyze the Companions' response
Examine the claim that all Companions approved and no one objected. Why does Baqillani say this is significant?
He contrasts this with their criticism of Abu Bakr on other matters.
4
Understand the principle of communal infallibility
Baqillani asserts that the community cannot agree on error. How does this principle support his argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الدلالة على صحة العهد من أبي بكر إلى عمر ومن كل إمام عدل إلى من يصلح لهذا الأمر
فإن قال قائل : قد أوضحتم أن عمر بصفة من يصلح لإمامة المسلمين وابتداء العقد له؟ فما الدليل على صحة عهد أبي بكر إليه وأنه جارٍ مجرى العقد له؟
قيل له : الدليل على صحة ذلك أن أبا بكر عهد إليه بمحضر من الصحابة والمسلمين على صفة ما ذكرناه، فأقروا جميعاً عهده وصوّبوا رأيه، ولم يقل قائل منهم : لِمَ تعهدُ في أمر ما جعل الله لك العهد فيه؟ ولا قال ذلك قائل في غير مجلسه ولا بعد وفاته ولو كان عهده إلى عمر خطأ في الدين لسارعوا إلى تعريفه ذلك ومواقفته عليه ولكان أجدر من قول قائلهم : علينا فظاً غليظاً؟ إذ كان ليس له أن يولي عليهم أحداً لا فظاً ولا رفيقاً وكان تنبيهه على ذلك واد كـاره به ومطالبته بتركه أولى من خوضهم في صفة يعهد إليه، لأن الكلام في صفة من يعهد إليه فرع للكلام في صفة العهد أولا : وإذا لم يصح العهد جمله سقط الخوض فيه في صفة المعهود إليه وزالت المؤونة ومثل هذا الخطأ والتفريط الظاهر لا يجوز على كافة لمسلمين وقادة الأنصار والمهاجرين؛ لأن الأمة لن تجتمع في عصر عصر الصحابة ولا في غيره على خطأ وإمساك عن إنكار ما من سبيله أن يُنكر، حتى لا يكون فيها إلا متدين بصحة العهد من الإمام إلى غيره وقائل به ومصوبٌ له، لأن القول بالعهد وفعله خطأ من فاعله الرّضي به والإقرار له خطأ من المقر له، إذ كان العهد خطأ في الدين والأمة لا تجتمع على خطأ
ويدل عليه أيضاً إجماع أهل الاختيار الذين هم أهل الحق، في القول بالإمامة : أن للإمام أن يعهد إلى إمام بعده ولسنا نعرف منهم من ينكر ذلك ولا يثبت عن أحد منهم برواية شاذة ومقالة مرويَّةٍ أنه لم يكن قائلاً بها ولا ذاهباً إليها . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً ويوضحه علمنا أن الإمام العدل لو لم يكن إماماً وكان رجلاً من الرعية لكان له أن يبتدىء العقد لمن يصلح للإمامة وإذا كان ذلك كذلك فكونه إماماً لا يحطّه عن هذه الرتبة فوجب أن يكون له أن يعقد على إمام بعده ويعهد إليه كما كان له أن يبتدىء العقد له لأن العقد في الحقيقة عقد على صفة فصح بذلك ما قلناه .
Chapter: Evidence for the validity of the appointment from Abu Bakr to Umar, and from every just imam to one who is suitable for this matter.
For if someone says: You have clarified that Umar possesses the quality of being suitable for the imamate of the Muslims and for initiating the contract for him. So what is the evidence for the validity of Abu Bakr's appointment to him and that it runs the course of the contract for him?
It is said to him: The evidence for the validity of that is that Abu Bakr appointed him in the presence of the Companions and the Muslims, in the manner we mentioned. So they all approved of his appointment and deemed his opinion correct. Not one of them said: Why do you appoint in a matter for which God has not given you the authority to appoint? Nor did anyone say that in his assembly or after his death. And if his appointment of Umar had been an error in religion, they would have hastened to inform him of that and to confront him over it. And it would have been more fitting than the saying of one of them: 'A harsh and rough man over us'? Since he had no right to appoint over them anyone, whether harsh or gentle. And his alerting him to that [؟] and asking him to abandon it would have taken precedence over their delving into the quality of the one appointed, because speech about the quality of the one appointed is a branch of speech about the quality of the appointment first. And if the appointment is not valid at all, then delving into the quality of the appointee falls away and the burden is removed. And such manifest error and negligence is not permissible upon the entirety of the Muslims and the leaders of the Ansar and the Muhajirun, because the community never agrees in any era, the era of the Companions or other, upon error and refraining from denouncing that which must be denounced. Until there is none among them except one who adheres to the validity of the appointment from the imam to another, and who says it and deems it correct. For the saying and the doing of the appointment is an error from its doer, and being pleased with it and acknowledging it is an error from the one who acknowledges it, since the appointment would be an error in religion, and the community does not agree upon error.
And it is also indicated by the consensus of the people of choice (ahl al-ikhtiyar), who are the people of truth, regarding the imamate: that the imam has the right to appoint an imam after him. And we do not know of any of them who denies that, nor is it established from any of them through an anomalous narration or a reported statement that he did not hold it or adopt it. And it is also indicated and clarified by our knowledge that if the just imam were not an imam and were a man from the subjects, he would have the right to initiate the contract for one fit for the imamate. And if that is the case, then his being an imam does not lower him from this rank, so it is necessary that he has the right to contract for an imam after him and appoint him, just as he had the right to initiate the contract for him, because the contract in reality is a contract upon a quality. So by that what we have said is established.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing the validity of the caliphal appointment (عهد) of Umar by Abu Bakr, extending to any just imam appointing his successor. The author begins by stating the chapter heading: 'Evidence for the validity of the appointment from Abu Bakr to Umar, and from every just imam to one who is suitable.' He then presents a hypothetical objection: an interlocutor asks for the evidence that Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar is valid and equivalent to the initial contract of imamate. The answer provides several arguments.

First argument: The Companions and Muslims were present when Abu Bakr appointed Umar, and they all approved and deemed his decision correct. No one objected at the time or later, neither in his assembly nor after his death. If the appointment had been a religious error, they would have hastened to correct him. Their silence on this issue, while they did criticize Abu Bakr on other matters (like the appointment of a harsh person), proves that they regarded the appointment as correct. The author notes that discussing the quality of the appointee is secondary to discussing the validity of the appointment itself; if the appointment were invalid, the whole discussion would be moot.

Second argument: The community (أمة) as a whole cannot agree on error. Since all the Companions and Muslims at the time accepted the appointment, it would be impossible for them to have remained silent about a manifest error. Their acceptance implies that the appointment was correct and binding.

Third argument: Consensus of the 'people of choice' (أهل الاختيار), who are the authorities on the imamate, holds that the imam has the right to appoint his successor. No known scholar from this group has denied this, and there is no reliable report otherwise.

Fourth argument (rational): If a just man who is not an imam can initiate a contract for an imam, then an imam, who holds a higher position, certainly has that right. The imam's status does not diminish his capacity to appoint; rather, it reinforces it. The contract is essentially based on the suitability of the person, not on any additional authority needed beyond being qualified.

Thus, the author concludes that the validity of Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar is firmly established by multiple lines of evidence.

Key Terms
إجماع
الصحابة
العهد
الأمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions' silence was due to fear or politeness, not agreement.
Correction: Baqillani argues that they did object to Abu Bakr on other matters (e.g., the appointment of a harsh leader), so their silence here indicates genuine approval. Additionally, the theological principle of communal infallibility prevents silent acceptance of error.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is the Companions' lack of objection significant?
A) They were afraid to speak.
B) It indicates they agreed with the appointment.
C) Abu Bakr was too powerful to criticize.
D) They had no opinion on the matter.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' unanimous approval of Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar serves as a historical consensus that confirms the validity of imams appointing successors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something you believe is wrong within your community, do you speak up or remain silent? What would the Companions' example in this lesson call you to do?
?
Are you quick to assume that silence equals agreement? How might this lesson change the way you interpret the silence of others?
?
The Companions did not hesitate to confront Abu Bakr when they thought he was wrong about appointing a harsh man. Is there an area of your life where you are avoiding necessary confrontation out of politeness? What would it cost you to speak the truth?

Consensus of Scholars and Rational Proof for Imam's Appointment
Baqillani presents the consensus of scholars and a rational analogy to prove the imam's right to appoint a successor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani strengthens his argument for the validity of imam's appointment using the consensus of ahl al-ikhtiyar and a rational analogy.

Core Idea
If a layman can nominate an imam, an imam certainly can.

After establishing historical consensus, Baqillani provides two further proofs: the consensus of the qualified scholars (ahl al-ikhtiyar) that an imam can appoint his successor, and a rational analogy: if a regular person can initiate a contract for an imam, then an imam, who holds a higher rank, certainly can. This lesson explores these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the later part of the Arabic passage from 'ويدل عليه أيضاً إجماع أهل الاختيار...' to the end.
2
Understand the consensus of ahl al-ikhtiyar
Baqillani claims that those qualified to choose the imam (ahl al-ikhtiyar) agree that an imam can appoint his successor. What is the significance of this group?
3
Analyze the rational analogy
Baqillani argues that if a non-imam can initiate a contract for an imam, then an imam can do so even more. Why is this considered a valid argument?
4
Connect to overall proof
How do these two arguments complement the historical evidence from the previous lesson?

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing the validity of the caliphal appointment (عهد) of Umar by Abu Bakr, extending to any just imam appointing his successor. The author begins by stating the chapter heading: 'Evidence for the validity of the appointment from Abu Bakr to Umar, and from every just imam to one who is suitable.' Their silence on this issue, while they did criticize Abu Bakr on other matters (like the appointment of a harsh person), proves that they regarded the appointment as correct. The author notes that discussing the quality of the appointee is secondary to discussing the validity of the appointment itself; if the appointment were invalid, the whole discussion would be moot.

Second argument: The community (أمة) as a whole cannot agree on error. Since all the Companions and Muslims at the time accepted the appointment, it would be impossible for them to have remained silent about a manifest error. Their acceptance implies that the appointment was correct and binding.

The imam's status does not diminish his capacity to appoint; rather, it reinforces it. The contract is essentially based on the suitability of the person, not on any additional authority needed beyond being qualified.

Thus, the author concludes that the validity of Abu Bakr's appointment of Umar is firmly established by multiple lines of evidence.

What does Baqillani argue about the capacity of an imam to appoint a successor?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that an imam's authority includes the right to appoint his successor. When you encounter a leader in your community (e.g., in a mosque, organization, or family) who makes a decision about future leadership, do you submit to that decision or do you resist because you think you know better? What is in your heart when you disagree?
?
Baqillani uses consensus and analogy to establish this ruling. Have you ever dismissed a religious ruling because you didn't like the logic behind it, without honestly examining the evidence? Will you commit to learning the proofs behind Islamic teachings before judging them?
?
The analogy argues that a layman can initiate the imam's contract, so the imam certainly can. This implies that authority does not diminish one's capacity but enhances it. Where in your life do you hold back from exercising responsibility because you think you are 'just a layman'? What would it look like for you to take ownership of your role in the community today?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Answering the objection about taqlid in the caliphal contract
Baqillani addresses the claim that the contract was invalid due to a taqlid condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani counters the claim that Uthman's caliphate was invalid due to a taqlid condition, emphasizing the epistemic status of solitary reports and the Companions' righteousness.

Core Idea
Solitary reports cannot override the established integrity of the Companions.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses an objection that seeks to invalidate Uthman's caliphate by pointing to a condition of taqlid (blind following) supposedly imposed in the contract. He argues that the report about this condition is a solitary report (khabar wahid) that does not yield certain knowledge. Moreover, the Companions were too knowledgeable and God-fearing to engage in forbidden taqlid. This lesson explores his argument and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Note the structure: objection then answer.
The passage is incomplete, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim that Uthman's contract included a condition of taqlid to the two shaykhs, which is forbidden. Identify all parts of the objection.
They also mention Ali's refusal to accept such a condition.
3
Analyze Baqillani's response
Baqillani first dismisses the report as a solitary report (khabar wahid) that does not yield certainty. Then he argues that if taqlid were forbidden, the Companions would have known and avoided it.
4
Reflect on the epistemic principle
Consider why solitary reports cannot override established practice according to Baqillani. How does this protect the integrity of the Companions?
This connects to the broader Sunni methodology of accepting the collective uprightness of the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : وكيف يكون عقد عبد الرحمن لعثمان صحيحاً وقد عقد لـه على شرط تقليده في الأحكام لأبي بكر وعمر وما روي عنه من أنه قال لعلي : نبايع لك ونعقد لك هذا الأمر على أن تحكم بكتاب الله وسنة نبيه وسنة الشيخين من بعده ، وأن علياً قال : ليس مثلي من استظهر عليه ولكن أجتهد رأيي، وأنه عرض ذلك على عثمان فرضي بالشرط وضمِنَهُ وعُقِدَ لـه عليه وقد اتفقنا على أن التقليد من العالم لغيره حرام في الدين؟ يقال لهم : هذا الخبر أيضاً من أخبار الآحاد وليس هو مما يعلم صحته ضرورة ولا بدليل . فإن كان التقليد حراماً فإن الصحابة قد كانت أعلم بذلك وأتقى الله من أن .
And if they say: How can the contract of Abd al-Rahman for Uthman be valid when he contracted for him on the condition of his following Abu Bakr and Umar in rulings? And what is reported from him that he said to Ali: 'We pledge allegiance to you and contract this matter for you on condition that you judge by the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Prophet, and the Sunnah of the two shaykhs after him'? And that Ali said: 'Someone like me is not to be pressed, but I will exercise my own ijtihad.' And that he presented that to Uthman and he agreed to the condition and guaranteed it, and the contract was made for him upon that. And we have agreed that taqlid (blind following) by a knowledgeable person of another is forbidden in religion? It is said to them: This report is also from the solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) and it is not something whose soundness is known necessarily or by proof. For if taqlid is forbidden, then the Companions were most knowledgeable of that and more God-fearing than to...
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This is a third objection-and-answer passage. The objectors argue that Abd al-Rahman's contract with Uthman included a condition that Uthman follow the rulings of Abu Bakr and Umar (taqlid of the two shaykhs). They also report that Ali refused such a condition, saying he would exercise his own ijtihad, while Uthman accepted it. Since taqlid for a knowledgeable person is agreed to be forbidden, the contract should be invalid. Baqillani answers that this report is from akhbar al-ahad (solitary reports) which does not yield certain knowledge—neither necessarily nor by proof. Moreover, if taqlid were indeed forbidden, the Companions were the most knowledgeable of that and most God-fearing; they would not have engaged in an invalid contract. The text cuts off here, but the implication is that the report cannot be used to overturn what is established about the Companions' correctness.

Key Terms
التقليد
أخبار الآحاد
الشيخين
الاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any historical report, even if solitary, can override the established practice of the Companions.
Correction: Baqillani emphasizes that akhbar al-ahad lack certainty and cannot outweigh the known uprightness and knowledge of the Companions.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani consider the report about the condition of taqlid insufficient to invalidate Uthman's caliphate?
A) Because taqlid is not actually haram.
B) Because the report is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and not definitively established.
C) Because Ali agreed to the condition.
D) Because the Prophet approved it.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends the validity of Uthman's caliphate by arguing that reports of a taqlid condition are solitary and thus not certain, and the Companions' righteousness prevents them from engaging in forbidden acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an objection to a well-established Islamic practice, do you examine the strength of the evidence before accepting it? The Companions were not swayed by weak reports—how can you train yourself to be similarly discerning?
?
Are you more likely to follow a tradition because it is comfortable, or because you have verified its foundation? The lesson shows that even a solitary report is not enough to override what is known. What steps will you take to ensure your faith is built on certainty?
?
The objectors in this passage used a solitary report to challenge the integrity of the Companions. Have you ever entertained doubts about figures in Islamic history based on weak sources? Repent and commit to seeking knowledge with rigor.

Ali's Rules of Engagement in Civil War
Baqillani enumerates Ali's prohibitions in fighting the people of the qibla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific prohibitions and procedures that Ali implemented when fighting Muslim rebels, distinguishing them from the treatment of non-Muslim enemies.

Core Idea
Ali's war rules treat rebels as believers, not as enemies to be vanquished.

Imam al-Baqillani presents a set of rulings from Ali ibn Abi Talib during the Battle of the Camel and Siffin. These rules demonstrate that even in civil war, the norms of warfare are bound by Islamic ethics. Before reading the passage, consider how Ali's orders reflect a deep understanding of fiqh and the desire to avoid bloodshed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ali's rules
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the specific prohibitions listed.
The list includes multiple actions that are forbidden during and after battle.
2
Identify the key prohibitions
Examine each prohibition: not initiating hostilities, not pursuing a fleeing person, not finishing off the wounded, not invading homes, not harming women. Consider why each is significant.
These rules are distinct from the rules of war against non-Muslims.
3
Compare with rules of war against unbelievers
Reflect on how these rules differ from the standard rules of jihad against non-Muslims. This shows the unique fiqh of fighting rebels.
The difference is based on the status of the enemy as Muslims.
4
Understand the rationale
Why did Ali issue these rules? Consider the goal of reconciliation and reducing harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما ظهر من فقهه وعلمه في قتال أهل القبلة من استدعائهم ومناظرتهم وترك مبادأتهم والنبذ إليهم قبل نصب الحرب معهم ونـدائـه : «لا تبدؤوهم بالحرب حتى يبدؤوكم، ولا يُتبع مدبر، ولا يُجهز على جريح، ولا يُكْبَس بيت، ولا تهج امرأة»، وفي رواية أخرى : ولا يكسر بيت»، ورده رحالات القوم إليهم وترك اغتنام أموالهم وكثرة تعريفه وندائه على ما حصل في قبضته من أموالهم وكثرة الأمر لابن عباس وغيره بقبول شهادة أهل البصرة وصفّين إذا اختلطوا ووضعت الحرب أوزارها والصلاة خلفهم وقوله لمن سأل ليس في الصلاة والعدالة اختلفنا ؛ وإنما اختلفنا في إقامة حـد فصلوا خلفهم واقبلوا شهادة العُدُول منهم إلى غير ذلك مما سنه من حرب قال جلة أهل العلم : لولا حرب عليّ لمن خالفه لما عُرِفَتْ المسلمين حتى السنة في قتال أهل القبلة.
Then what appeared of his jurisprudence and knowledge in fighting the people of the qibla: from summoning them, debating them, not initiating hostilities, giving them notice before waging war, and his proclamation: 'Do not start war against them until they start against you, do not pursue a fleeing person, do not finish off a wounded one, do not invade a house, do not molest a woman.' In another narration: 'do not break a house.' And his returning their baggage to them, refraining from seizing their wealth, his frequent announcements and calls concerning what had come into his possession of their wealth, and his frequent command to Ibn Abbas and others to accept the testimony of the people of Basra and Siffin when they intermix and war lays down its burdens, and to pray behind them, and his statement to the one who asked: 'We did not differ concerning prayer and justice; we only differed concerning establishing a hadd penalty. So pray behind them and accept the testimony of the just among them.'—to other such rulings that he established in war. The leading scholars said: 'Were it not for Ali's war against those who opposed him, the Muslims would not have known even the sunna concerning fighting the people of the qibla.'
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage demonstrates Ali's deep jurisprudential knowledge in the context of civil war. The author lists numerous rulings that Ali implemented when fighting the people of the qibla (Muslim rebels). These include: not initiating hostilities, giving notice before war, not pursuing fleeing enemies, not killing the wounded, not invading homes, not harming women, returning property, accepting testimony and prayer behind people from both sides after the war. Ali clarified that the difference was only over legal penalties, not over fundamental matters of prayer and justice. The passage concludes with a statement from leading scholars that without Ali's warfare and his rulings, the Muslims would not have learned the proper sunna for such conflicts.

Key Terms
قتال أهل القبلة
لا تبدؤوهم
مدبر
جريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that in civil war, all rules of warfare are suspended and any tactic is permissible.
Correction: Ali's rules show that even in civil war, strict ethical guidelines apply: no killing of the wounded, no harming women, etc.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first prohibition in fighting the people of the qibla?
A) Do not pursue a fleeing person
B) Do not start war until they start against you
C) Do not kill the wounded
D) Do not invade homes

Key Takeaway
Ali's rules for fighting Muslims prioritize reconciliation and minimize harm, treating opponents as fellow believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a fellow Muslim deeply wrongs you, do your first responses resemble Ali's (summoning, debating, not initiating harm) or do you rush to retaliation? In your heart, do you still see them as a believer whose rights protect their life and dignity, even in anger?
?
Ali prohibited pursuing those who flee, finishing the wounded, and harming women. Is there someone in your life whom you have 'finished off' emotionally—someone you rejected, humiliated, or abandoned after they lost a conflict with you? What would it cost you to instead preserve their honor as a fellow believer?
?
In your own religious or community disagreements, do you give formal notice before damaging a relationship? Or do you suddenly escalate without warning as Ali never did? This week, who can you summon to a peaceful conversation instead of a war?

Ali's Post-War Jurisprudence
Baqillani details Ali's post-war policies and scholarly praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ali's rulings on returning property, accepting testimony, and praying behind former enemies, and the scholarly significance of his approach.

Core Idea
Ali's post-war policies aimed to reintegrate opponents into the community.

After detailing the rules of engagement, al-Baqillani presents Ali's post-war policies: returning property, not seizing wealth, accepting testimony and prayer behind those who had fought him. These rulings show that after the conflict, differences were not to be held against individuals. The passage concludes with the scholars' remark that without Ali's war, the sunna for fighting the people of the qibla would not have been known.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the rulings after the battle: returning baggage, not seizing wealth, announcements about captured property, and the commands to Ibn Abbas.
2
Analyze the dialogue about prayer and justice
Examine Ali's response to the questioner: the difference was only over hadd penalties, not over prayer or justice. What does this teach about unity?
3
Understand the scholars' concluding statement
The leading scholars say that without Ali's war, the sunna for fighting the people of the qibla would be unknown. Why is this significant?

Checkpoint
What did Ali say regarding the difference between him and his opponents?
A) They differed on prayer
B) They differed on justice
C) They differed only on establishing a hadd penalty
D) They differed on all matters of religion

Key Takeaway
Ali's post-war policies aimed at reconciliation and preserving the unity of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in conflict with another Muslim, do you distinguish between fundamental matters (prayer, justice) and secondary disagreements, or do you let the conflict taint everything? What one relationship needs this distinction today?
?
Ali returned the baggage of those who fought him. Is there any right or wealth you hold that you refuse to restore to someone you've had a conflict with? What is holding you back from letting it go?
?
If your conduct in a current disagreement with a fellow Muslim were recorded as precedent for others, what would it teach? What change must you make so that your example reflects the mercy and justice of the sunna?
